A BIT EARLY, PERHAPS!
But Spring is already with us. Which means that the
SPRING FASHIONS ARE IN.
A fact so dear to every lady, whether she lives in the great Metropolis
or in the far West.
Ladies in the Country need not be at a loss to know what is going to be worn
this Spring, as Mark Foy’S Illustrated Catalogue will give them an excellent idea.

“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
Books.
WEYMAN’S STORIES.
Stanley Weyman does not write hurriedly, yet
he writes too hurriedly for fame. His historical*
romances are the best that are done to-day, but
they will hardly live beyond to-morrow. The
killing pace of modern literature permits no man
to cater at once for posterity and for the pub-
lishers. A pity. Good name is so much better
than shekels—-and so very much less filling at
popular prices.
Here is the literary order of merit of Weyman’s
books:—
My Lady Rotha I
Under the Red Robe J- 1
A Gentleman of France I
Good
The Story of Francis Cludde Fair.
The Man in Black I
The House of the Wolf >-
The New Rector
-Tolerable.
Then there are two new stories, “ Memoirs of a
Minister,” due in Australia in Nov.; and “The
Red Cockade,” due about February.
Considering his output, Weyman keeps his level
well; and there is, indeed, a marked improvement
in the method and interest of his later books.
Between the trio,at the head of the list individual
taste must choose; their workmanship is very
even. Perhaps “My Lady Rotha,” which leaves
the more familiar French field to exploit the Ger-
man States during the Thirty Years’ War, shows
the best art. The plot is very ingeniously man-
aged.;, and its current flows with an ease which all
Oonan Doyle’s labour of learning cannot'achieve.
“ A Gentleman of France ” fits into the stirring
period of Henri Quatre. The idea is to make the
aero disclose his own character by the tale he
tells, and i;i its telling, without direct touches.
“ Under the Red Rote ” is and artisti-
-3a ly tetter. There is more restraint and propor-
" on ; and the incidents are not so crowded. Then
toe climax, if similar, is more dramatic. (Note
t is unknown who comes masked to rescue M. de
Jjcheforet. IsthisO.’s wife ? Apparently. Yet
iow account for her tallness and daring, as com-
■ ared with previous description of small, womanly
voman ? Mademoiselle is supposed to be the tail
-me—vide picture. And where did Madame get
swordsmanship, horsemanship ?)
Weyman’s best quality is his wonderful fresh-
i■ ss and fertility of invention. His worst defect
his lack of effective humor. His style is admir-
* >e; he manages the “ I ’’-narrative beautifully ;
■ e , with all his pains, his characters fail to hang
t one memory.
My Lady Rotha, Under the Red Robe, Gentle-
.i n of France, Francis Cludde—each, paper, 35.;
>th, 4s.
NOTES.
Another “colonial library” is in the field—
Blackwood’s. The best of the early volumes is
Dorothea Gerard’s “ Lady Baby ” (paper, 35.;
doth, 45.)
From “Old and New, ’’recently-published poems
by Ernest Radford :
I held her hand
To-day,
And whispered a word,
And she heard ;
And I did not work,
And she did not play,
To-day.
I touched her lips
To-night
And none knew, but we two,
The delight;
And I shall not sleep,
And she will not sleep,
To-night.
Now let any “man of feeling” forget those lines
if he can !
There is room for a good literary sneer (the
sneeriest and most aggravating of all sneers) in the
fact that the newly-knighted Walter Besant,
though originator and president of the English
“Authors’ Association” (for publishing books
without a middleman), has never yet issued a
book under its auspices. Yet he and others
are constantly railing against the injustice of
publishers. All Beaant’s books are issued
through Chatto and Wind us ; but can that firm,
under the circumstances, take the fact as a com-
pliment ?
Admirers of Balzac will welcome a complete
edition of his novels in English, piloted by that
pleasant essayist, George Saintsbury. The first
volume is “ The Wild Ass’s Skin ” (Le Beau de
Chagrin), 4s. Balzac is one of the few French
authors who bear translation well, and the
“ Comedie Humaine ” in cheap French, dress is a
trial to the eyes; So Emerson would certainly buy
this edition.
B. L. Farjeon’s “ Aaron the Jew ” is being
translated into Hebrew—-a rare distinction. One
only recalls “Daniel Deronda ” and Zangwill’s
“ Children of the Ghetto ” as similarly favored.
“East Lynne,” in the new edition (posted,
2s. (Id.) is the best-selling 2s. book the Australian
trade handles. Its total of about 390,000 copies
to date, however, is still a long way behind the
700,000 of “Pickwick ” (now obtainable in a good
cheap edition, posted, Is. Bd.). 700,000, also,
Marie Belloc claimed in June Englishwoman
for Max O’Rell’s “ John Bull and his Island” —
is there an exaggerating cipher ?
Frederick Locker-Larnpson, who died the other
day, made a most admirable collection of English
occasional verse, in “Lyra Elegantiarum ” (posted,
2s. 6d., in the excellent “ Minerva ” series). His
own poems are clever and dainty, but Jack fibre.

Periodicals.
SOME SELECTED MONTHLIES.
Cosmos, per annum, Bs.; single numbers, Bd.
(fis. and fid. within N.S.W.). This Australian
magazine, now nearly a year old, is recently
much improved.
Review of Reviews, single numbers, lid. ; per
annum, 11s. One may cavil at Stead’s fads,
and resent the perhaps inevitable dulness and
lateness and scrappiness of his clippings, but
on the whole his Review covers a large field
well and thoroughly.
Sokibnee’s Magazine, single numbers, Is. 2d.;
per annum, 14s. This and the R. of R. are
(with the Century) the only foreign maga-
zines which pay Australasia the compliment
of a special edition, Scribner’s has some-
thing of the attenuation of American culture,
but is always readable and very well illus-
trated.
The Windsor Magazine, per annum, lls.; single
numbers, lid. Popular pictures, stories, and
articles.
Contemporary Review, per annum, 40s.
Fortnightly Review, ~ 40s.
Nineteenth Century, „ 40s. Three
leading English reviews, solid and strong.
New Review, per annum, 18s. A happy medium
between heaviness and flippancy.
North American Review, per annum, 375.
The Forum, per annum, 375. The two best
American reviews.
NOTES.
The best story in “Scribner’s Magazine” for
August (Is. 2d.) is “The Scab”—which has real
thrill, though allowance must be made for the
point of view. Clever illustrations brighten an
article on “All Paris A-Wheel,” and E. A.
Abbey’s pastel pictures are brilliantly reproduced.
Anthony Hope’s story, “The Wheel of Love,”
is very ordinary—the author writes too much now-
adays to sparkle as he did in “The Dolly Dia-
logues ” (Is. 3d. ) —his masterpiece.
“Windsor Magazine ” for August (lid.) is a read-
able number, and this magazine altogether shows
considerable improvement on early issues. Let-
terpress and illustrations are of good, sound, popu-
lar quality.
“ The Album ” (per annum, 265. ; single copy,
Bd.) is unsurpassed for quality of illustrations
among English picture-journals. The work is all
photographic, and the reproductions are exceed-
ingly well done. Three recent numbers make a
specialty of views of Oxford, of the Grace cricket-
ing family, and of English opera singers.
“In Town” (per annum, Bs., single copy, Bd.)
is another clever picture-periodical, issued monthly,
and making a feature of colored illustrations.
The August number has a supplement of river
Thames views, and stories by E. W. Hornimg and
Fergus Hume.
There is keen competition between the cheap
American monthlies. The Cosmopolitan led a
reduction from the 25-cent standard to 15 cents ;
McClure’s followed; and Munsey’s dropped to 10
cents. To this low level (lid. posted; lls. per
annum) the Cosmopolitan now comes, and it is so
good for the money that one can’t see how the
dearer magazines will keep the prices up. Of
course, the circulation of all three named is over
the half-million ; and profit comes entifely from
the advertisers.

RECENT BOOKS.
in Henry Parkes’s “ Sonnets and Other Verse ”
2s. 9d.) strictly commonplace ideas find strictly
■ /mmonplace expression. Occasionally a glimpse
o? feeling brings an echo from better bards, but
i ' a rule the aged author stumps stiffly along on his
fwn crutch. At every step something in 14 lines
f ills shrieking and is trodden under foot. The
s quashed sonnet assumes strange shapes—let this
9» irreverently lifted from the mangled heap :
NAPOLEON BONAPARTE.
Tis just a hundred years since Bonaparte
Appeared in Paris. Then the bloody frenzy
Was dying out: men look’d for some Rienzi,
To master madness, heal the Nation’s heart,
3 till bleeding from its awful wounds. Apart,
And self-contained, through his own mystic lens he
Survey’d the chaos with its stifled frenzy
If mangled factions, left without a chart
)r guiding star. The Man of Destiny,
With his young strength untried, reined in and
tamed
The raging elements as steeds that do
Their riders’ bidding ; ever mounting high,
He vaulted on the whirlwind, and proclaimed
His gorgeous rule ! Then followed Waterloo !
Frenzy—Rienzi—lens he (fine effort, but exhaust-
ing)— encore frenzy ! O penultimates, O Petrarch !
“ Steeds that do their riders’ bidding ” is so good
—and so new; and the moral of the whole is so
original—and so dull. Yet Parkes’s morals are
really rather lively. Why doesn’t he publish
“ Smoking-Room and Other Stories ?”
Mathilde Blind’s monograph on “ George Eliot ”
(Is. fid.) is worth reading.
The biography of “ Sonia Kovalevsky,” a clever
Russian girl who became professor of mathematics
at Stockholm University, adds to one’s impression
of the originality and strength of the Slav brain.
Morrison’s “Australian in China” (lls. 3d.),
from which liberal quotation was made in a recent
Bulletin, is a very readable book of travel-impres-
sions, well-illustrated.

We wish Ik to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves puBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens In every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little hand
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
nevereease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
smnewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth; there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

Tales of fixe Austral Tropica.
Bards of the Backblocks.
ON THE ARROW-TRACK.
[FOR THE BULLENTIN.]
Coming from the Arrow, \
With my empty dray,
Met a nigger family
Tramping on the way ;
The meanest, poorest wretches
I had seen in W.A.
He led them from the Ninety-Mile,
That greasy, grey-haired black,
His wire-like legs kept well ahead
Along the ruddy track;
The mother had a strip of baric
Slung across her back.
A swaggy would have failed to keep
That skinny nigger’s pace ;
The gin beneath her burden’s weight
Was limping in the race,
Within its littered filth I saw
A picanninny’s face.
Trotted beside, another child,
And mostly clothed in air,
Who pointed to my water-bag—
I gave what I could spare,
And to the tailboard lifted her
And bade her ride up there.
She yabbered—but the mother seemed
Half proud and half afraid,
And kept close in beside the wheels
And watched me and the maid ;
Till I gave her half a damper
That a new-chum digger made.
I tried to add a little
To the little that I knew,
“ Gooyah, Gooyah,” meant the flies
That round about her flew,
And “ Yindi”—well—the finest teeth
That ever smiled on you !
And then she started singing—
But although attentively
I tried to pick up any air
Whatever air might be —
’Twas not what I had often heard
At the corroboree.
I nearly had it off by heart
And thought what I should say
When I should bring it back to Vic.,
This truly native lay—
But in a flash I recognised—
“ Ta-ra-ra Boom-dee-ay I”
J.H.G.
CURRABUBULA.—AFTER KENDALL.
[For The Bulletin.]
Where the Everlasting moans
In the loneliness of dread,
Through the terror-haunted thrones
Of the kingdom of the dead.
There the thunder-stricken beams
Sapless, leafless, godless are—
Where the feeble little streams
Mumble—Currabubula !
In that soul-forbidding gloom
(God of mercy, mercy show !)
Dwells inevitable doom,
Mortals come, but phantoms go.
In the half-moon’s ghostly light,
Through the gorge a broken bar,
Waters flowing through the night
Moaning—Currabubula !
One secluded pheasant mocks
Twenty birds from one retreat.
Here a ramble through the rocks,
There a fall of fifty feet.
Gaining strength where torrents bend
Down the haunts of waratah.
Wailing loudly as they wend
Curra—currabubula!
Where we wandered long ago
(God of glory, glory take t)
And in unavailing woe
Sobbed as if our hearts would break.
When thy lips downcast and mute
Held the kiss to make or mar,
As the stream-sprite lapped his lute
With his Currabubula.
Where was coldness such as this T
Passion-frozen in the breath.
Bitter as a soundless kiss
Stolen from the lips of death.
And you dared to weep and live
While you dared deny to me
(God of vengeance, vengeance give I)
My immortal legacy.
Glistened there the poisoned fang
(God of pity, pity me I),
Circled there the boomerang
In the weird corroboree.
When we clamor loud in youth ;
God, why dost Thou hear our cries ?
Thou hast prescience for ruth ;
We are mortal, Thou art wise.
Solace comes in silent sleep ;
There repentance kneels no more;
There our sins no vigil keep
Oh the dark remorseful snore.
We may soar beyond in dreams,
Who in life may rise not far,
Far beyond where feeble streams
Mumble —Currabubula 1
N.S.W.
JULIAN SAKE.
Two or three instances of criminal folly
are reported from western N.S.W. In one case a
wool-shed on a “free labour” station was fired ;
on another the engine to drive the shearing
machines was tampered with. One pretty certain
effect of such acts will be to defer the release of
even the more deserving of the men imprisoned
during last season.
Captain Morse, the popular commander of
the Alameda, is about to publish his reminis-
cences, under the title, “50 Years of a Sailor’s
Life : His Experiences in Many Lands and on
Many Seas.” Chatto and Windus will probably
bring out the work. On the passage from ’Frisco
to Sydney Capt. Morse gave a lecture, much to
the amusement and edification of the passengers.
Now that the warm weather has arrived,
and the desire to wash occasionally pervades every
well-constituted human being, it is well to remem-
ber that the Natatorium, 400 Pitt-street, supplies
the largest variety of baths in Sydney. It is pos-
sible to wash in about 16 different ways at the
Natatorium, but perhaps the pleasantest features
of the establishment are the hot salt-water baths
and the big swimming bath, where a fine sample
of the crystal ocean is always on hand for the
benefit of those who’ like the briny deep brought
to their doors in a manner of speaking.*

“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has evei
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market that
anything previously. This is no idle boast, aiicl, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, fo*
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sal*
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, aft®
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended au<
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf L
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he hai
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this ; and, a<
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford O'
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIN SON & SONS, who use more American leal
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands: —“CON
QUEROR,” “SIR ROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE*
ranks supreme.

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in

communications trill be regarded as strictly confidential.

desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 7, 1895.
Treasurer Ward’s Finances.
The financial statement which Treasurer
Ward recently presented to Maoriland
H. >use of Representatives is a fine example
of what the boom comes to when it grows
old, and the money is all spent, and the
great drunk is over, and only the recovery
remains; It is the same tale which every
Maoriland Treasurer has told for 10 years
past or more—a tale of desperate efforts to
keep up appearances, and to present some
respectable semblance of a surplus, and to
make a very little go a long way, and at the
same time to make it seem as if it v/ent far-
further than it really does. The result, as a
whole, is not particularly satisfactory. Mr.
Ward’s budget is probably, with one excep-
tion, the most honest and the most solvent
presented this year in Australasia, yet it is
not wholly honest, and it proves, now that
the complete figures are to hand, hardly
solvent in any reasonable acceptation of the
term. The total expenditure for next year
is set down at £4,331,000, and the total
legitimate revenue at £4,195,000, leaving a
massive deficiency to be faced, on 31st
March, 1896, of £136,000. But by a curious
juggle this is turned into a surplus of £9OOO.
In days gone by, Maoriland floated its loans
with a sinking - fund attached for their
redemption when they fell due. The sink-
ing fund never amounted to much ; at its
highest figure it reached about one-and-a-
half millions against nearly £40,000,000 of
debt. And, in 1884, the Government
conceived the great idea of borrowing
each year, and using for purposes
of revenue, an amount equal to that
paid into the fund —in other words, it
commenced, under Act of Parliament, to
borrow the sinking-fund payments for the
year, and the interest on the past accumula-
tions as well. Treasurer Ward proposes to
cadge £145,000 under this head during the
present year ; and his deficiency of £136,000
will thus be turned into an alleged surplus
of £9OOO.
* # •
This peculiar device was adopted to pre-
serve a hollow pretence of keeping faith
with the national creditor. The British
money-lender was promised a sinking-fund
as additional security for his money. To
have actually suspended payment of the
promised annual instalments would have
looked too much like repudiation, therefore
the Maoriland Treasury pays the amount
out of the revenue with one hand and
borrows with the other a similar amount
which is put into the revenue. This is not,
in any sense, the Treasurer’s own swindle ;
it is done openly under an Act of Parlia-
ment passed for the especial purpose, and is
a national and Parliamentary swindle in
which everybody takes a hand. For some
years past the Government has been able to
produce a small surplus over and above the
amount raised by this peculiar and rather
shady device, and the money which was
virtually borrowed out of the sinking-fund
has gone, along with the legitimate sav-
ings made out of revenue, to construct
railways and other public works. So
long as this was the case the proceeding
was comparatively unobjectionable ; at the
worst it was a clumsy and foolish piece of
book-keeping whereby the surplus was made
to look larger than it really was. In the
estimates for next year, however, the amount
borrowed out of the sinking-fund is almost
all needed to meet ordinary expenditure.
Moreover, Mr. Ward has had to hunt
desperately around in the odd corners of the
Treasury, and to discover certain alleged
sums, about £25,000 in all, which he claims
to have forgotten to borrow in previous
years, and but for this professed discovery
even his borrowings would not have made
the public accounts for the coming year look
reasonably square. Under all these circum-
stances there is an ancient and fish-like
aspect about the promised £9OOO surplus—
which is not much of a surplus, even if it
were above suspicion ; and, according to any
really legitimate system of book-keeping, it
looks much more like a shortage of
£136,000.
# # *
Maoriland ostensibly stopped borrowing a
number of years ago and did not resume the
evil practice until a few weeks since, when it
floated the first 3 per cent, loan in Aus-
tralasian history. But even during the
years when it was alleged to have sworn off
the unclean thing the public debt kept
increasing in small, mysterious ways, by
conversions, the borrowing of trust funds,
and other unnoticed devices. The sinking-
fund faded peacefully away from £1,485,000
in 1891 to £751,000 in 1895. The net public
debt was £36,980,000 in 1889, and it reached
£39,635,000 in the early part of 1895, since
which date it has been further increased by
one-and-a-half-millions. The one redeem-
ing feature in the case is that the population
of the province increased considerably faster
than its debts, so that Maoriland —until a
few weeks ago —could boast of being the
only Australasian province where the debt
per inhabitant was steadily declining. Also,
the borrowed money seems, on the whole, to
have been used with great prudence, and
invested in works which will at least pay
interest on their cost. Nevertheless, the
solemn fact remains that the only Austra-
lasian province which has made a serious
attempt to stop the accumulation of debt has
failed badly in the attempt. Whether a
stronger man than Ward or a better man
than Seddon might have succeeded it is
difficult to say. Also, whether they really
desired to make the country solvent, or
whether they only wanted, like so many
other politicians, to make it look solvent for
the sake of themselves and their party, is
an open question. But there is no doubt
that the people of Maoriland swore off bor-
rowing some years ago in dead earnest, or in
what they believed to be dead earnest, and it
is equally certain that the results of that
great renunciation have been only a modified
success.
# * #
In various ways the prospects for the com-
ing year are hardly cheerful. The revenue
has shown a declining tendency for some
time past, and Treasurer Ward estimates
that for the year ending March, 1896, there
will be a further decline of nearly £86,000.
Instead of resolutely cutting down expendi-
ture to meet this expected falling-off, the
estimated outlay is increased by £65,000,
and various gorgeous proposals are advanced
for subsidising a new line of steamers and a
Pacific cable, all of which look like signs of
a tendency to fall away into the garish
magnificence of the old Vogel era. The
Government has a vast, indefinite liability'
on hand in the shape of the unfinished Mid-
land railway and the litigation attached
thereto, which should make it abjure new
steamship subsidies and cable lines now
above all times ; and it has another possible
responsibility in the shape of its £2,000,000
guarantee on behalf of the Bank of New
Zealand. It has now been found necessary
to further assist that luckless institu-
tion, which means another indefinite liabi-
lity. Also, the great experiment of the
State Loan Department is still in an embryo
state, and though it has every prospect of
success, still it furnishes another reason why
Maoriland should go slow in the matter of
incurring new liabilities. There is very
little elasticity in the revenue of the province
nowadays. With the exception of Queens-
land and South Australia, it has the big-
gest debt and the biggest interest bill per
head of population in Australasia. Its
public lands were sold in a joyous,
irresponsible fashion in the good old days,
and all that remains of its once magnificent
territorial revenue is £300,000 per annum—
less than one-seventh of that enjoyed by
New South Wales. Its railway revenue has
been declining for two or three years past,
and the Maoriland lines now, pay only £2
14s. 6d. per cent, on their cost. Under all
these adverse circumstances Maoriland has
to grind out of its people four times as much
in direct taxation as is paid by the inhabi-
tants of New South Wales, and more than
twice as much as is levied on the people of
Australia and Tasmania in general. It had
far less resources to begin with than its
continental neighbors, and the twin curses of
war and Vogelism fell upon the country with
crushing weight. The conduct of its people
for the last few years has been a brilliant
example of dogged resolution to get through
honestly under enormous difficulties, and
the pity is that the result hasn’t been com-
mensurate with the effort.
■* * *
The situation which the Sbddon Govern-
ment refuses to face is practically this : In
order to finish its uncompleted railways and
other public works at even a very slow rate
of progression, the country needs to raise
something like £300,000 per annum out of
legitimate revenue, and this can only be
done by more economy, or more taxation, or
by finding new sources of income, or by all
of these combined. Meanwhile, Treasurer
Ward proposes instead to undertake a
wholesale conversion of the public debt into
3 per cent, stock, which can only mean add-
ing a good many millions to the principal in
order to save a few thousands annually in
interest. Also, he proposes to establish a
sinking-fund for the gradual extinction of the
debt —which sounds like a wildly humorous
suggestion, coming, as it does, from a financier
who is grabbing every available coin of the
existing fund for revenue purposes. Maori-
land’s past experience in this direction shows
conclusively that no sinking-fund is safe un-
less it can be put out of the reach of the
Treasurer, the Government, the Legislature
and even the nation itself ; and the difficulty
of devising any means by which the country
can put its own money out of its own reach
seems almost insuperable. The existing
fund was protected by every legislative
device imaginable, and when bad times and
the Stout-Vogel coalition arrived they all
proved of no account. The moral is that
the average Parliament is a financial dipso-
maniac which can’t trust itself within reach
of the public cash, and unless the Seddon
Ministry can suggest some means of effectu-
ally tying itself up so that it can’t get any-
where near the new fund, that will also be
borrowed just as soon as it gets large enough
to be worth borrowing. Some day, perhaps,
a benevolent Fat Man, or association of Fat
Men, will start a sinking-fund as an
act of private patriotism, by depositing
£1,000,000 worth of Government debentures
with the Bank of England, to accumulate at
compound interest until the total is sufficient
to pay off the public debt in one sudden
operation. Then, as the money will not be
the property of the nation, it will be beyond
the reach of Parliament, and no Government
will be able to lay hands on it, and the
Agent-General will howl on the door-mat of
the Bank and try in vain to borrow it, and
the Treasurer will go “ home ” on special
missions and implore the directors to lend it
to him, but all to no purpose. Lnder such
circumstances as these it is possible that a
sinking-fund might become a useful reality,
but under no other conditions that The
Bulletin can conceive is such a thing
likely.
* * •
The Seddon Government has probably a
better record than any other now existing in
Australasia, that of Victoria alone excepted.
If it isn’t absolutely honest that fact is partly
owing to its predecessors and to Parliament,
partly to the system of Party Government,
and partly to the dismal fact that the
way back to solvency is very long and
dreary, and that the public debt has a
gruesome way of increasing imperceptibly
on its own account when the country isn’t
looking. Also, it is probably owing in part
to Treasurer Ward’s recent mission to Eng-
land. Every “colonial” statesman within
the memory of this generation who has gone
on a mission to that great and grovelsome
land, has come back with a yearning to
scatter the public money on cable subsidies
or other devices for tightening the “silken
bonds of h’empire,” or to float big loans, or
undertake huge conversion operations, or
otherwise to do things which are a great
deal too large for the circumstances of the
country. Despite all the gorgeous figures of
Coghlan and Haytbr, the obvious fact
remains that these provinces are miserably
poor. Victoria, Tasmania, and S.A. have
each a chronic deficiency. Queensland has
a bogus surplus made out of borrowed money;
New South Wales has another, made by
cooking the loan accounts and charging the
repairs of old dredges and the like to pos-
terity ; and Maoriland is living just now on
borrowing the sinking-fund for revenue pur-
poses. Under these conditions this country
can’t afford to send its statesmen “ home ”
to acquire large and gorgeous ideas in the
atmosphere of Lombard-street, and Treasurer
Ward’s “ mission ” is likely to spoil that
hitherto plain and useful person for purposes
of local consumption.
Federation v. Suppuration.
In a recent Bulletin “Treble B.” attacks the
idea of present Federation and deprecates
this paper’s enthusiasm for Australia against
the world. As his main argument, “Treble
B.” pillories the reputations of our politi-
cians and “leaders.” But why are they
failures and pettifoggers ? Simply because they
are elected to secure a local distribution oi
public works, J.P.-ships, and free railway
passes. The decision of national questions is
entrusted to them accidentally. It is as if the
contractor for street-cleaning stumbled on an
obscure clause in his bontract which required
him to provide the meteorological report —as if
the inspector of nuisances were made Supreme
Court Judge for economy’s sake. The curse of
Australian Parliaments is the parochial nature
of their work, and the revolting duties that
impudent constituents impose on their repre-
sentatives. The remedy for this is the exten-
sion and democratisation of municipal powers—
and Federation. Shall national issues be
decided by men elected for parochial issues?
As long as Australia is merely a geographical
expression it will be so. As long as small men
are elected to large duties will the Dibbses take
a title to oblige a lady, and the Reids borrow
money from England to cobble up Monkey
Creek bridge. As long as Castlemaine’s
“ Thirteen Pubs. ” dictate the issues of a par-
liamentary contest will butchers in thought
and principle be pitchforked into power to koo-
too to George and May and prospective
babies. The prostitution by small politicians
of the national dignity can be prevented by
having a Federal Parliament for the decision of
national issues only.
• * #
As to “partial Federation being peculiarly
liable to disruption ” —the facts are the other
way. Partial Federation continues till public
awakening or some pressing need makes it
perfect. Germany, Switzerland, and Canada
are not federations made at one blow, and their
history discounts “ Treble B’s ” contention 100
per cent. How then is Australia interested in
partial federation? N.S. Wales, Victoria, and
S.A. could come to an understanding, more or
less complete, on three subjects :
1. Customs.
2. Banking.
3. Railways.
Regarding Customs there have been suggested in
The Bulletin (17/11/’94) certain lines on which
interprovincial tariffs might be modified at once,
at the will of the Ministers, without legislative
jaw-wagging.
# # *
Regarding banking, N.S. Wales, Victoria,
and S.A. have indubitably at this moment an
unequalled opportunity for establishing a
federal note-issue. The very Banks themselves
are craving for Governmental intervention, and
the crying need for stability in financial matters
urges on the Governments to action. There
might be, perhaps is, objection to three separate
State banks in these provinces, but the estab-
lishment of one bank on broad lines, with good,
well-paid, able, independent men at the head
and control of things, backed by the three pro-
vinces, and run in the interests of the three
provinces, would do more to straighten up
Australia than all the “many little useful
things so momentous in the aggregate ” for
which the soul of “Treble B.” yearns. As
for the railways—has ever anyone heard of
a trust or ring among trading firms ? A
trust is a fair or unfair combination to
abolish competition. It is done, as a rule,
by companies which do not insist on equal
capitalisation and the etfacement of the
numerous irrelevant financial differences that
anti-Federationists are fond of setting up.
What objection is there to a Victorian, New
South Wales and South Australian Railway
Trust —such as has been known between mighty
railway organisations in England and the States?
At present, New South Wales, Victoria and
South Australia tax themselves for railway
expenditure which partly benefits Riverina but
is principally pure waste. An interprovincial
Railway Trust would settle the vexed question
of management for all time. When the Vic-
torian Government lately wanted railway
advice they dared not ask Eddy, because he
was the manager of a viral .system. With a
Railway Trust the three provinces could sa\e
money in scores and hundreds of thousands by
securing the three best procurable railway
managers, one for each province.—the three to
be a federal railway council. All which shows
that “Treble B.” errs when he depreciates
Federation, partial Federation, and even talk
about Federation. Federation in all or part
should commence to-day.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Botany Bay “Justice.”
The case of the cabman, Sweetman, sentenced
to 14 years’ imprisonment and two or three
floggings for his alleged share in the Mount
Rennie business, is also to be considered by the
Govt. Commissioner who is enquiring into the
main case. No more glaringly cruel case
of injustice than that of Sweetman can be
found on the legal records of Botany Bay, from
the year One. The man acted immorally, it is
true: he sat on his vehicle and crooked his
finger at a complaisant girl, who was dawdling
on the sidewalk and who got into his cab and
went where he wanted her to go —and after-
wards told a lot of transparent lies about
having been driven off against her will (through
a thickly-populated neighborhood where, by
means of one shriek, she could almost at any
moment have summoned 100 rescuers had she
desired rescue). The Court sitting in that
bloodstained time of panic swallowed her
absurd story, with the result that Sweetman
was sentenced as aforesaid. If the prisoner’s
deed deserved so frightful a penalty, where
is there a “ man of the world ” who should
go unwhipped ? Sweetman’s sentence can
be justified by no one who is not pre-
pared to attribute to that libidinous cabman the
omniscience of Gou Almighty. He drove the
girl off, it is true, for Ids own purposes,
but how was he to know what would be the
ultimate effects of an excursion for the whole
results of which, he was told, when sentence
was passed upon him, he was “ morally respon
sible ” ? The fact of the matter is that there
never was (taking the teim in its accepted
sense) any Mount Rennie “outrage” at all.
The girl, a lying little street tramp, went away


voluntarily with a dirty cabman to a dirty
location for a dirty purpose, the consummation
of which purpose was interfered with by the
appearance on the scene of a number of dirty
little larrikins, with whom, or some of whom,
she at once voluntarily entered into immoral
relations. Where her consent ended and the
“outrage,” if there ever was an “outrage,”
began, is by no means clear. Certain it is that
the girl lied when she professed to identify all
those who she said had outraged her ; and it is
a fact that she subsequently declared in writing
that she was only sure of one. And certain it is
that in the forged-cheque-passer, Stanley, and
the arsonist, Smith, the chief Crown witnesses
of the “outrage,” the prosecution had two
deponents so utterly conscienceless and un-
trustworthy that no human being would be
justified in shedding blood upon their testimony
even to the extent of slicing from a decaying
leper material for a microscopic-slide.
The Convict Brand of Justice.
The conduct of Westralian Government in
smothering the prosecution of one Gehald
Raikes met with some remarkably plain
criticism from W.A. Judge Hensman recently.
Raikes was a person who is alleged to have
owned Oscar Wilde’s vice without Wilde’s
intellect, and he claimed the name of and rela-
tionship to a former English Minister of the
Crown. Consequently, as soon as he struck
Westralia, society received him with open
arms, and he was given a lucrative
position in W.A. Civil Service. A few
months ago society was shocked to hear
that a conscientious J.P. had issued a warrant
for Raikes’s arrest for vicious practices. He
was several times brought before Perth police
court, but apparently no attempt was made by
the authorities to produce the evidence against
him, and he was quietly discharged, and seems
to have lost no time in leaving the province.
During a libel case tried before Judge Hens-
man last month, which turned on alleged
indecent actions in which “20 or 30 boys ” of
a leading Perth school were said to be implicated.
Mr. Cowan, P.M. of Perth, was examined
as a witness, and gave the following evidence :
Was it through your instrumentality, Mr. Cowan»
that the action was started against Raikes ?—lt was; I
discovered it and reported it.
Raikes was arrested by the police?—Yes.
The case dropped out, and subsequently an information
was laid by a private individual? —Yes.
He was in the Government service?—Yes.
Where is he now ?—He has left the colony.
Was he dismissed, or did he leave?—l believe he was
dismissed. If your Honor will allow me to say so, I may
stale i hat when I left for the south I left two magistrates,
Mr. Traylen and Mr. James, the latter of whom I ac-
quainted with the whole of the evidence. We were send-
ing for two boys, but we had complete evidence against
him, so far as I could judge as a magistrate. I believe
that it was afterwards thought right that no further
action should be taken in the matter, and that the boys
should not be brought forward. It was thought best to
suppress the scandal.
By his Honor: Who allowed him to escape ?—I don’t
know, sir. There are those hitjher than I.
Upon which Judge Hensman, in summing-up,
made this comment :
I only know of Raikes by what I heard in this court in
evidence. This man was in the Government service, and
he seems to have been charged with a certain offence, and
I say no more than this: That there has been a deplorable
miscarriage of justice in not having him brought to trial
and his guilt or his innocence established, and I trust the
time will never come when any suspicion would arise in
the minds of the public that considerations such as the
feelings of others, or the position of the party, would be
allowed to interfere with the due administration of jus-
tice. I have said this because it has appeared to me the
occasion warrants it, and I hope it will be long before I,
at all events, have occasion to make any such remark
again.
In plain words, the Westralian gang in officer
already accused of being mixed up in electoral
thimble-rigging and unsavory mine transac
tions, now practically stands charged by a Judge
of the Supreme Court with a conspiracy to
defeat the ends of justice in the interests of a
well-connected society man. One law for the
rich, another for the poor, and none at all for
gentlemen with English letters of introduction.
“ Encouraging ” Local Industry.
In the sacred name of Revenue, with the sole
hope of swelling the importation of foreign
wines, the Vic. Assembly has inflicted a swing-
ing amendment on the present duties. Still
wines— i.e., the brands that compete against
local production —pay 2s. per bottle at the
Customs now. The reformed tariff will let
them into the importer’s warehouse for Is. per
bottle, consequently the absorption of foreign
claret, hock, chablis, sauterne, port, sherry,
&c., must be doubled ere the revenue can make
an extra penny. Only one excuse was offered
for this big move. Somebody had an idea—or,
he said he had it—that the public liking for
Victorian wine, in preference to beer and spirits,
would be encouraged thereby. Such very
bright ideas are Flinders Lane inspirations,
as a rule. Those people for whom cheap,
wholesome dinner wines have no charm
are not likely to cultivate a taste for
liquors of similar qualities at high prices.
On the other hand, it is clear that wine-
drinkers, being, for the most part, stubbornly
prejudiced in favor of the foreign article, and
only taking local “ imitations ” on sufferance
since the duties were raised in 1892, will simply
he encouraged by this shilling reduction to
return to their old love. Men now in course of
slow compulsory conversion will backslide and
become more rabid bigots than ever. With the
exception of the bright idea aforesaid there
wasn’t a semblance of a reasonable argument
raised by the tariff “ reformers.” Mr. Tucker
showed that the sale of foreign wines
had dropped to one - third of the boom-
time average, also that the consumption
of Victorian grape-juice last year was slightly
less than before, and because “bounders” don’t
squander champagne now, as in the period of
fraudulent “prosperity,” and because many of
their depressed victims can’t afford an occa-
sional shilling for Victorian claret, Tucker
called for reform. Mr. G. D. Carter reduced
his argument to an idiotic conundrum —“ What
was the use of maintaining high duties when
the wine of the country was so cheap
that he could supply any member of the
House with 100,000 gallons at lOd. a gallon?
Carter’s conundrum should have deteated
its object by persuading the House to put
a prohibitive duty right away upon cheap
French and German stuff, extracted from grape-
skins and not from grapes —but no. Mr.
Trenwith reminded them that the export
trade in Victorian wine was increasing annually,
the best of all proofs that it deserves to be
forced into favor on the spot by high protective
duties. Nevertheless, prejudice and com-
mercial influence carried their point. The
Minister of Customs thought it “ perfectly
safe ” to knock Is. per bottle off the tariff, and
expect the demand for foreign wine to be
doubled immediately, and the Government
voted with the majority, which was only a
majority of seven.
Treasurer Reid’s Trickery.
Treasurer Reid proposes to collect the present
New South Wales Customs duties till the end
of September, and to levy the land and income-
tax, which is supposed to replace them, from
•the beginning of July, and by thus double-
banking the taxpayer for three months a surplus
of .£lBl,OOO is expected. This would go a
good way towards paying for the repairs to old
dredges, and for patching the Windsor bridge,
and building the bridge over the Bogan at
Monkey, and wood-blocking certain Sydney
streets, but these are all to be charged to loan-
expenditure as previously proposed, while the
surplus is largely fooled away in “ special
grants to municipalities.” There is no
earthly reason why the municipalities should
have a special grant just now, except that it
may buy support for G. H. Reid ; the whole
idea of local government is to make the muni-
cipalities pay their own expenses and to do
without grants, and, in any case, the municipal
grants are fixed by law, and no Treasurer has a
right to make additional allowances, either for
advertisement or to buy votes or for any other
purpose, until he has first paid all his legitimate
expenses out of revenue. And, considering that
Mr. Reid took £389,000 out of loan funds
and included it in revenue, and charged
.£216,000 of his road and bridge expenditure
to his predecessors, thus adding it to the
deficiency, and that he proposes to charge
between £600,000 and £700,000 of his own
current road and bridge expenditure to loans,
and that he has left over £200,000 of the year’s
ordinary expenditure unprovided for by passing
it on to next year, and that he closed last year
with a deficit, his pretence that he has a large
sum of money to give away needlessly in
municipal endowments is an unspeakable
mockery. It is merely another aspect of the
old, old story : no average Treasurer under the
party system cares a straw whether the finances
are square or not: all he wants is to make them
look square. The business of every Treasurer
is to cook his predecessor’s accounts so as to
show a big deficit, and to cook his own so as to
show a sham surplus, and to reduce taxation
and increase the road and bridge expenditure
and darn the consequences generally. Some
day, of course, the consequences may refuse to
be darned any longer, but that is a mere detail.
Victoria’s Amateur Magistrates.
The dignity of Melbourne’s unpaid magistracy
suffered a severe shock the other day, when a
J.P. made a few well-chosen remarks from the
Bench regarding himself and his species in gene-
ral. Taking advantage of a moment’s silence,
this gentleman started, apropos of nothing at
all, to state that some very unpleasant things
had been said about the injustice, ignorance,
and one-sideduess of the amateur magistracy,
that most of these accusations were strictly
true, and that the sooner the whole system was
“burst-up” the better. His fellow-J’s.P., as
soon as they discovered the drift of his observa-
tions, shook him imploringly by the elbow and
besought him to keep quiet, but he went right
on with his blasting observations until he was
smothered by the hurried adjournment of the
court. The scope for strong language afforded
by the Victorian J.P. system is practically un-
limited. In Victoria, almost all the people of
any consequence, and many other people, are
J’s.P., and have a right to sit on the Bench and
overrule the stipendiary magistrates. Most of
them know little or no law, and almost all of
them turn up only when they have their own
special axe to grind. In liquor cases the bench
is rushed by teetotallers and wine-and-spirit
merchants, &c., the former being there mostly
to decide against the publican regardless of the
evidence, and the latter to decide for him regard-
less of the evidence. The shameless old system
about which, years ago, Marcus Clarke
had so much to say, is still in full blast.
“ When some poor devil of a civil-servant dis-
putes a pawnbroker’s account for interest on
a pledged waistcoat,” wrote Clarke, apropos
of the appointment to the magistracy of many
usurers who had as bond-slaves politicians
of the day, “ the Bench looks like Pelican
Island in the breeding season, by reason of
the multitude of beaks hanging over the rail-
ings.” And the same statement applies, in
various forms, all round. A few J’s.P.
are more or less crazed on sexual questions,
and, when a shady female accuses a man of
“assault,” they arrive with a rush to jump on
the unfortunate accused in the interests of
morality—the dirty variety of it. Masonic
J’s.P. will roll up to see the suspected brother
through his troubles. Irish J’s.P. frequently
come along when Patrick Murphy is in worse
trouble than usual; Scotch J’s. P. do likewise.
Fat Men J’s.P. roll up to promptly dismiss the
charge against the influential person who is
alleged to have run a cronk bank, and J’s.P.
who lost their money in his bank arrive to vote
against them. When an influential young man
“goes wrong” his uncle and all the other
J’s.P. in the family come along to help in dis-
missing the case. Pious, churchgoing J’s.P.,
in long, black tail-coats, fill the Bench and
overflow into the body of the Court when an
especially good man is charged with something,
and fat, chuckling J’s.P. to the number of 40 or
so overcrowd the premises when a particularly
objectionable case of any kind is on the board.
The J.P. has no salary or billet or legal reputa-
tion to lose by his vagaries, so he does very
much as he likes, and his administration of
justice is a scandal and a disgrace. In the
capital of New South Wales he has been prac-
tically abolished in his judicial capacity, for
the sufficient reason that the community could
endure him no longer, and though the stipen-
diary magistrate who succeeded him is far from
satisfactory at times, he is at least responsible
for his actions, and has some elementary ideas
of law and occasionally of equity ; also, the
police-magistrates are growing cleaner as the
years roll by. Victoria, for some unexplained
reason, still tolerates the amateur “ beak.”
The Logic of Isaac Mo,
Apropos to a comment upon Daimio’s price
(3 to 1) for the Caulfield G.N. Steeplechase, a
fortnight before the race, sundry bookmakers’
champions have supplied this journal with
trumpery evidence in favor of Ikby Mo as
against the betting machine, which draws a fixed
commission and doesn’t swear. The Bulletin
gathers from these excited correspondents that
whereas Ikey Mo sometimes falls into liquid-
ation and is finally wound up, the tote
goes placidly on for ever, therefore Ikey’s
profits must be extremely moderate. One
earnest pleader asks “where are Cuss,
Swear, Scream and the hundreds of one-
time kings of the ring ? ” (giving their
real names.) A question as to the where-
abouts of the Jubilee Juggins would not
be further from the point. When Cuss, Swear
and Co. laid to their books steadily, and
didn’t back horses, own horses, play hazard,
and live like twenty fighting-cocks at a
time, instead of like one individual fighting-
cock, they amassed riches at a much greater rate,
in proportion to their annual turnover, than the
tote can rake it in. When they either didn’t
stick to their legitimate business, or did wildly
foolish things outside of it, or were “ let in ” by
land-boomers, they were as likely to come to
poverty as the punters who had kept them in
affluent circumstances for many a year. Apart
from the unchallenged fact that bookmakers
are laughed to scorn by the tote every time
a rank “outsider” gets home (which is not by
any means a rare accident), figures show that
the tote’s average dividend on second, third,
fourth, &c., favorites is much greater than
Ikey’s starting-price. Also, the tote’s first
favorite, when he wins, is a more remunerative
animal than Ikey’s “red-hot pot.” The pos-
sible ultimate failure of that parasite to reach
the cemetery in a coach and four, and sleep
under a costly tombstone, is, considering all
these circumstances, very discreditable to him.
He is taught thrift at school ; Providence is
awfully good to him; he ought to cut up
fat after his decease. Worldly justice wouldn’t
throw a bone to a starving bookmaker. As for
the contention that long odds from the tote
only go to benefit “ drunken fools or chows,”
this journal can’t imagine any type of citizen
less deserving than Ikey Mo. Likewise, this
journal doesn’t accept the “fools or chows”
theory. The ignorant investor who throws
away his sovereign on a horse that has “ no
chance,” and scoops the pool for his temerity,
is more often than not one of the steadiest and
broadest-minded fellows on the course. He has
come there for a careless day’s outing, not for
the foolish, chowish purpose of trying to make
money by his blessed “knowledge” of the
business in hand.
A Local Option Bill.
The Cold Tea faction in Maoriland has urged
the reluctant Seddon Government into taking
some rather uncertain steps in the direction of
Local Option. Judging from present appear-
ances, the most probable result is that the
Ministry will disgust the liquor-party by its
interference, and won’t satisfy the Cold Tea
brigade—for the reason that its action is feeble
and lukewarm, and is constructed on a plan
which promises the maximum of fuss and the
minimum of result. The Bill provides that, at
certain dates, a poll shall be taken all over the
country on four distinct questions : (1) Whether
the number of licences then existing in the dis-
trict shall remain unaltered ; (2) whether the
number of licences in the district shall be
reduced ; (3) whether licences shall be alto-
gether abolished in the district ; and (4) whether
licences shall be altogether abolished through-
out the province. A bare majority may decide
that the existing licences shall be maintained or
that they shall be reduced, but it takes a three-
fifths majority to enforce total prohibition.
The anguish of the Cold Tea taction arises from
the fact that this arrangement deliberately
splits up their vote, while that of the
liquor crowd must of necessity remain solid.
The drinky section of the community has
only one thing to vote for —that the number of
licences remain as it is. The teetotal crowd is
invited to spread itself over two issues—reduc-
tion and absolute prohibition, and thus wreck
its chances by splitting the Cold Tea vote. As
Robert Stout pointed out in the House, 3000
citizens might vote at a local-option poll, 1709
declaring for absolute prohibition throughout
the district; 1209 for a reduction in the number
of licences ; and one—only one—for preserving
things as they were. Whereupon the 1790
would be defeated, because 1800 constituted
the three-fifths majority necessary to enforce
total prohibition; and the 1209 would be
of no account because 1501 votes were
needed to effect a reduction. Therefore,
the lonesome, red-nosed drinker would carry
the day with his block vote of one, and
things would remain as they were until next
poll, despite the fact that 2999 people were
arrayed against him, simply by reason of the
splitting-up of the Cold Tea vote. The Govern-
ment, however, refused to be moved by Stout’s
eloquence, and rushed the Bill through its
second reading. It will probably have cause to
regret, later on, that it touched the matter at
all. The liquor crowd looks askance at
the Seddon Ministry, and wants to know why
it interfered witli the question, and the
Cold Tea gathering will probably feel very bad
towards it by-and-bye, when it fully realises
the futility of this peculiar kind of local option.
As a rule, when a Cabinet is invited to tackle
the drink question the safest thing it can do is
to be so busy starting a State Bank, or convert-
ing the public debt, or something, that it has
no time to attend to the matter.
Australian law doesn’t travel on any fixed
principle ; you drop a penny in the slot and
get three months, or ss. fine, or get hanged,
pretty much at random. The following is a
case in point. In Sydney, a few weeks back,
a somewhat inebriated individual was run in
for having in his possession “ property reason-
ably supposed to be stolen.” There was not
a shadow of evidence that he had stolen
the property; he wasn’t known to the
police as a thief or any other kind of
criminal; he was simply found with pro-
perty in his possession like 99 per cent,
of the men one meets in the street, and a
constable chose to think he looked like a
man who might have stolen the property,
though no one had reported losing it. If the
accused had been sober he would probably have
explained, but unluckily he wasn’t, and the
law, without waiting for him to recover his
faculties, gave him three months for (as already
mentioned) “ having property in his posses-
sion,” &c. When the prisoner realised
his position he explained where he bought
the property, and his statements proving
absolutely accurate he was released in a
gradual sort of way, when the law got suffi-
ciently rested. There was no inquiry; the
policeman who accused him of being “ reason-
ably suspected ” wasn’t called on, so far as is
known, to explain his reasons, nor was the
magistrate who convicted him on no visible
grounds save that a policeman said he was
“reasonably suspected.” The accused was
simply discharged, and when he got back to his
lodging he found that all his worldly goods had
been pawned during his absence. The man who
took them was hunted up and convicted, and he
was fined 405., or one month. As the other man
got three months, it follows, therefore, that it is
just three times as criminal to be found with
goods in your possession, which a policeman
says he thinks, on no visible evidence, are
stolen, as to be absolutely and definitely con-
victed of pawning another man’s property.
The appeal to the Privy Council on New South
Wales Payment of Members Act will have a
little value if the Judges there can be led into
expressing an opinion on the general wording
of that particular measure. To soften antagon-
isms, plain English was eschewed by the
framers of the Bill, and, as it passed through
Parliament, it was not payment of members it
provided for, but a refund of expenses incurred
in the Parliamentary representation of elec-
torates. Thus a man is not paid for being a
member, but for the money he is presumed to be
out of pocket and the time he consumes in dis-
charging his Parliamentary duties. How in
this sense are M‘Millan, M.P. for Burwood,
and others situated? Assuming M'Millan
draws the <£3oo during his absence in England,
how can he exhibit the equivalent in services
rendered ? The Payment of Members Act, or
Parliamentary Allowances Act, should be
amended so as to make it really what the
general public intended it to be —that is, pay-
ment to the member as member, without
any mystifying phrases to placate a few indivi-
duals and possibly provoke litigation.
South Australia finds itself in a tight place
financially, and, despite the fact that the ordi-
nary expenditure has been cut down nearly
£230,000 since 1891, it has to save another
£60,000 at the least —possibly £lOO,OO0 —to
make ends meet. Treasurer Holder, proposes
to raise £30,000 of this by the stupid and cruel
device of an all-round reduction of Civil Service
salaries. This comes on top of another reduc-
tion made only last year. There is here no
attempt at discrimination between good men
and bad the same-sized piece is to be
carved off in each case, just to show the S.A.
civil-servant that it is as profitable to be a
lazy, incompetent ass as to be anything else,
for the reduction will come in either case.
Holder’s budget is one of several things which
seem to suggest that the Kingston Ministry
has seen its best days. S.A. has now air
accumulated deficit of £900,000, and the
Government dare not propose further taxation,
and makes no serious effort to meet the diffi-
culty. Of all the Australian financiers Turner,
of Victoria, is the only one who has shown
himself equal to the occasion. In the other
provinces the same old crowd still potters
round and lives in hope ; the financial crisis
brought- no new genius to the front. But
Victoria had the w’orst crisis of all, and
straightway an inconsiderable-looking little fat
man with a shamefully bad hat emerged from
the ruck and proved himself the one individual
who was capable of meeting the emergency.
There was a chorus of Sydney-press jubilation
over the fact that Acting-Judge Cohen sent
Alfred Windon, Stewart Dawson’s jewel-
robber, to the Carpenterian reformatory for
three years, instead of consigning him to gaol
“to mix with felons.” Yet the Carpenterian
reformatory at Eastwood (N.S.W.), better
known as the Brush Farm reformatory, is little
better than a hell college. The men admitted
there are all supposed to be able to do a little
work, though unable to earn a living outside.
The reformatory boys also work, and go
through a daily farce of two hours called
“school.” There is no schoolmaster; but an
official known departmentally as “ the atten-
dant,” and who, to ordinary eyes, is a turnkey
without keys to turn, does his level best
to do the duty asked from him. Men and
boys have their meals and spend their
evenings in the same room, though they
do not at these times actually mix. At
other times there is practically free inter-
course. The boys are far and away the more
vicious. Before any reforming work can be
done on the lines of Mary Carpenter (1) all
the adults should be shifted ; (2) the boys
themselves should be classified ; (3) a compe-
tent schoolmaster should be appointed ; (4) a
system of rewards and encouragements should
be provided to cultivate the manly instincts
that cannot yet be quite dead in the minds
of the boys ; (5) the pretence of gaol regime
should be abolished. All the reformation of
which the Brush Farm institution is capable at
present is insufficient to save the millionth part
of one stained juvenile soul.
The Young Women’s Christian Association
and the Women’s Christian Temperance Union
are said to have agreed to canvass against Premier
Kingston at West Adelaide next year.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION,
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art hac
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
The Wild-Cat Column .
The N.S.W, Government is going to float, in
London, a loan of £3,727,'>00 to meet £2,750,000 worth of
Treasury hills which fall clue next \ ear and £977,01/0 of an
old 6 per cent, loan incurred in 1867. After this is done
Premier Held promises to swear off borrowing in England
and float his loans on the local market, as he thinks “ it
will be well not to increase the burden of foreign indebted-
ness.” Under these circumstances, it would be interest-
ing to know why Premier 1L id is going to do the very
thing he says it would be well not to do. Of the above-
mentioned Treasury bills, £750.000 worth are held in New
South Wales, and, by borrowing in London to repay this
amount, Mr. Reid deliberately increases the foreign in-
debtedness of the country to that extent and deprives the
local holder of the investment. And—even apart from the
fact that the N.S.W. Premier is tints doing the direct oppo-
site of what he says he should do—if Mr. Reid has realised,
as he says he has, that it is better to depend on the local
market, why doesn’t he begin at once? When a man is
going to swear off drink, it is no good to commence by
marking r the date on the calendar, 12 months hence, when
he will begin to sober up. It would he easy. The Bul-
letin firmly believes, to obtain locally, at a reasonable
rate of interest, the money required to renew next year’s
liabilities—at all events, it would be as easy to get that
lot of money as the other lot which the Premier says he
is going to get later on, and the experiment is worth
trying in any case. Therefore, The Bulletin sums tip
the case in two questions: Since Premier Reid believes lie
shouldn’t increase .the country’s foreign liabilities why
does he do it? And, since lie believes he should depend
on the local money-market, why doesn’t he do it?

The public debt ofN.S. Wales, on 31st July,
was £58,219,533; annual interest, £2,205,722. A truly
remarkable circumstance is that the debt has been
reduced by £5900 since 31st April.

The S. A. State Land Bank scheme is a beauti-
fully simple device which, if it only proves workable, will
reduce the difficulties of the scheme to a minimum. The
borrower who wants to raise £lOOO w ill mortgage bis land
to the State Bank in the usual way at a rate which is not
to exceed 5 per cent, and which may be as low ns
4J. .The bank, instead of giving him money, will give
him £lOOO worth of bonds bearing interest at 4 per cent.,
and he will sell these to investors and get his cash. As
the bonds will be a safe investment, guaranteed by the
State, easily' transferable, and bearing a fair rate of in-
terest, it is expected that there will be a constant demand
for them by banks, insurance and building societies,
trustees, and private investors, and that they will always
fetch par or thereabouts. Tie borrower will pay his in-
Italments of principal and interest to the department,
ind as fast as they come in the money will
be used to buy up aod extinguish the bonds.
Consequently, the department will never have on
hand any money w hich is not bearing interest, as would
be the case if it borrowed a big capital to start with and
lent out actual cash. If .the public takes kindly to the
new securities the scheme looks perfect, save for one
thing o dv—it is seemingly proposed to charge only
er oueh additional interest to cover the working expenses
of the d part men I, so if, despite the precautions taken,
there is su h a fall in land-values as to make many of the
securities insufficc nt, there will he no reserve-fund to fall
back upon—at. least, there is no definite provision for the
establishment of such a fund. And as the total unim-
proved land-values of S.A. are alleged to have dropped
from £45,000.000 in 1884 to ‘ 25,000,000 in 1895, even the
most careful Land Bank may be “left" some time or other,
if it doesn’t prepare for contingencies.
• * • *
The Kingston Govt.’s scheme for converting
the S.A. public debt looks unpromising. By far the
biggest part of the debt falls due between 1907 and 1939,
and when the money-lern'er has got his 4 or 4J per cent,
safe for 30 years or so, it is useb ss to ask him to exchange
for a 3J per cent, slock on any terms which will he of the
slightest assistance to the borrower. The fooli.-h policy
of borrowing for fixed periods blocks the way, as usual.
If Australia floated its loans on the British principle
for 10 or 15 years certain, and thereafter to he repaid
at its own option on giving six months’ notice, it could
bang on when times are bad, and convert its 4 or 4$ per
sent, loans into 3 or 3i per cents, when the occasion is
suitable. This fact has struck every Australian Treasurer
like an avalanche during the last few months, hut all the
recent loans have been floated on the old fixed-dale prin-
ciple just the samel No Australian financier cares a
«t*aw about what will happen 10 or 15 years hence.

The London Statist mildly suggests that it
would be well for the British creditors to shove the other
Australian reconstructed banks into liquidation, after the
fashion of the City of Melbourne, and have done with the
business. Evidently that blatant organ forgets that the
"other hanks" have regularly paid up the interest agreed
upon, and have, so far, met the principal as it fell due,
aod a good deal of it before it was due, and under these
circumstances they would probably tell the British
creditor to go to perdition, or some such place, if he
proposed to liquidate them against their will.
• • • *
The British creditors of the City of Melbourne
Bank will probably feel very sorry before the liquidation
Is finished. If they had taken charge of the institution
U proposed, they would almost certainly have got 2J per
cent, on their money in the meantime, and with ahun-
iant patience they might have got it all hack in the long
ran. Now they will probably realise 10s. in the f, and
the highest interest at which they can invest it in England
Is Just about 2J per cent. The bank-rate on deposits in
England is now something like 1 per cent., and the City of
Uelb., at its worst, would have given a lot more than that.

Last week it was suddenly announced, for the
fourth time in its recent history, that the Bank cf New
Zealand was tottering on the verge of disaster, and it lias
been decided that the Government must again come to
the rescue. The adventures of this mistrably unlucky
bank within the last seven years make interesting read-
ing. In 1887 it had a paid-up capital of £1,000.000 ami an
tileged reserve of 1600,000. In 'BB it wrote off the whole
reserve and £300,000 of capital to cover losses, making
6900,000 of the shareholders’ money gone in one aet. The
jecond reconstruction followed a year or two later, when
£300,000 more was written off, making a total loss to the
lhareholders of £1,200,000; and £500,000 fresh capital
was called tip. At the same time the hank's landed pro-
perty was formed into a nominally separate concern—the
B.N.Z. Estates Co. —but as the B.N.Z. held all the shares
in this ill-fated concern, the separation was only nominal.
Also the estates were pawned in England to raise
61,335,000, on terms which actually amounted to 6 per
jent. The third great hurst came along in '94, when the
Jeddon Government saved the bank from closing its
joors by guaranteeing an advance of £2,000,000 at 4 per
jent. Another £750,000 was borrowed in London at
the same rate to pay off a similar amount of the 6 per
jent. mortgage on the estates, and it was reported that it
night be necessary to write off another £300,000 to cover
inore losses. Also, the Government auditors insisted on
mother call of £500,000, and thereafter people hoped
that the unhappy B.N.Z. was at last really and truly over
its difficulties.
The fourth great smash eventuated quite sud-
Jsnly last week. A Parliamentary committee investi-
gat-d the condition of the Estates Co. and reported a
•hortage of £1,340,000, for which the 8.N.Z., as the sole
shareholder, is responsible. The committee also, accord-
ing to the cabled report, speaks of a defi iency of
£376,000, and a possible further deficiency of £200,000, in
the bank owing to more depreciation in the \alue of the
assets. This will absorb all the remaining paid up capital
Dl £900,000, all the £450,000 which is expected to be the
net result of the call, and will involve another call of at
least £600,000 to give the institution a fresh start. The
total less to the shareholders since 1887 will then be some-
where between £2,600,000 and £3,000,000.
It is now proposed that Govt, should take up
£600,000 worth of preference shares; that when the
existing losses of the Estates Co. are provided for the
State should guarantee any further deficiencies, which
practically amounts to taking over the hank’s properties ;
and that the bank should then make a fresh start for the
fourth time, and try to keep square for the future. If
tills scheme is carried out Govt, will practically own five-
sixths of the bank, or possibly a good deal more, so that
the attempt to keep it going as a private institution
might as well be abandoned. A private bank which is
almost wholly conducted by State ollicials, and which get#
five-sixths of its capital under a State guarantee, is an
anomaly anyhow. Meanwhile, the notes of the B.N.Z.
have been declared legal tender throughout Maoriland, in
Order to prevent any sudden rush on its resources. The
bunk has paid about £2,500,000 in divs. during the 34
years of its existence, so the disasters of the last seven
years have swallowed practically all the profits of its long
career. Still, taking it all round, the B.N.Z. has had
unusual luck. If it had been wound up when its
troubles began to he apparent in ’B7, it would have
probably made a smash very like that of Matthew
Davies’ Mercantile, or the situ llftil Federal, or the V.D.L.
As it is, the directors have managed to break the news
by instalments, and have quiet'd the public eacli time a
new disaster came along with the assurance that the
worst was now over. Any desire there might be to kill
the original management has thus evaporated through
sheer effluxion of time. One thing, however, is certain:
the Slate’s £2,000,000 guarantee may yet involve a
serious liability if the calls prove very hard to collect,
or if more losses turn up, and Maoriland will have to risk
some more money in propping up its big bank in order to
safeguard the money it has risked already.
» • • •
During the discussion in Maoriland Parliament
on the 13.N.Z. reconstruction bill, it was again proposed
to make that institution a Slate Hank. Proposal rejected
bv 45 voles to 17. The B.N.Z. is already a State Bank, in
so far as the State has all the worry and responsibility,
and finds most of the money, and takes most of the risk ;
yet the Seddon Government is too much scared to curry
the matter to its only logical conclusion.

The balance-sheet of the Land Mortgage Bank
of Victoria is a remarkable document in several respects.
The total loss for the half-year is £6617. The reseive
fund of £105,000 has disappeared, and at least £60,000 of
it sec ms to have been already used to meet losses. The
bank’s investments amount to a tiifle over £1,000,000,
and though the chairman admitted that a lot of interest
had been written off as irre overahle, he frankly statid
that, nothing had been written <ff the principal, which
stood in every cise at the “ hook value.” How far that
may differ from the real value no one knows, but. when
the interest, is irrecoverable it often happens that a lot of
the principal is gone also. Moreover, it has been found
advisable to square the accounts by inserting among the
assets, “conversion expenses, £22,500.” This, so f>r as
appears from the chairman’s explanation, is not cash or
property of any kind, but is a sum which the bank might
have had to pay under certain circumstances, and ns these
circumstances didn’t turn up it hadn’t to he paid, there-
fore it is set down as being on hand instead. Unless the
explanation is wrongly reported this sounds very much
like saying that the chairman might have been run over
in Collins-st.reet ami damaged to the extent of £lOOO, and
as he wasn’t run over he has a right to insert the
£lOOO as an asset in his private balance-sheet. The Land
Mortgage Bank proposes to ask for a reduction of interest
on its £900,000 worth of 4j per cent, debentures, and it
is obvious that it needs something of the kind badly, and
needs a lot of it. Also, a good-sized call is suggested by
director Pratt, M.L.C.

The now liquidating Australian Mutual Invest-
ment and Building Co. is in a deplorable condition. A
report dat- d 28th July reveals a di-astrous state of
aff'irs. Liabilities amount to £336,774, and assets
£163,842. Liabilities for (tie most part consist of deposits,
so that, the loss will fall heaviest on the small thrifty
person who wants a big interest for his savings. During
the half-year the realisation on properties and official
expenses hasincr ased the debit balance fr m £99,561 to
£172,531 caused principally by mortgagees foreclosing
on allegedly valuable city properties. Taking these
figures as they stand, it follows that if the liquidator
have extraordinary luck, and realise on all the assets at
their own valuation—which they probably won’t—the co.
would pay about 9s. 9d. in the £, exdus ; ve of sale and
other expenses. By a series of calculations, the liquida
tors themselves show that they might, under various
circumstances, pay Bs. 3d., 35., or 2s. 91d. in the £.

Messrs. Bergl and Brabhin, of Sydney and
London, have now, after a series of experiments, finally
arranged to export 5000 cattle annually from Australia to
London. Since they began operations in Australia, in
January last, they have sent 1000 head of cattle. One
small consignment sent for a squatter, Mr. M‘Master,
realised £7 11s. per head net. In other words, after ad
freight and commissions had been paid, the squatter got
£7 11s. per head, whereas had he sold the cattle at Home-
hush at that time, he would have obtained £4, oral the
most 14 10s. per head.

* • * •
The National Mutual Life Assurance Society
has issued a table of half-premium rales. Under this
system a person who wants to get the largest amount of
insurance for the smallest immediate outlay can pay half
the usual rates for the first five years, after which the
ordinary premium is payable. To any peison who is tem-
porarily in financial straits this system is exceedingly
useful, as it enables him to get a large insurance f r a
very small present cost; in other words, it is another
embodiment of the great principle of shunting ihe re-
sponsibility on to the morrow. The National Mutual is a
local institution, managed by its policy-holders, where all
Ihe profits go to the insurers, and it claims the credit of
being the first office to b troduce Ihe system of keeping
overdue policies in force out of surrender values, whether
the insured ri quests that this be done nr not. As the
average insurer hasn’t sufficient presence of mind to look
after the matter, this automatic system—now common in
all Australian offices—is a far greater boon than it may
appear.
If Queensland’s Government gets safely through
the elections, it is probable that it will next year
turn the Queensland National Bank into a Slate institu-
tion, and shove its load of indebtedness on to the
shoulders of the taxpayer. If there is one honest and
capable man to be found in the Sodom and Gomorrah of
Queensland politics, and the Nelson Government will put
him in charge of the institution, and will inaugurate the
new departure by a full slati ment of the bank’s position,
then the idea is wholly commendable. Any big local
institution which can be nursed into solvency should be
preserved, if only to prevent the English banks getting a
bigger hold on the business of the country than they
already possess, and with State support even the Q.N.
Bank should get through all right. But the Queensland
Government is < tie very worst one in Australia to under-
take such a work. The lijst time it showed any signs of
straightness is so fur back that no one now living can feel
sure that he remembers the incident.

Hundreds of exact half-sovereign counterfeits,
in pure brass, are being extensively circulated in Melb.,
and it is very rarely that their genuineness is questioned.
