One of the very oldest and hitherto reputedly
wealthiest mercantile firms in Westralia is in difficulties.
• « • •
A certain Vic. merchant with an excellent busi-
ness. and owning a lot of good freehold, emb irked in
land specs, during the boom, and, being a careful man,
took the precaution before doing so to transfer every-
thing he possessed to bis wife. The merchant did no
harm in land, but the transfers bad Uot been annulled
when his wife died suddenly. By her willshe has left
ev'rytbihg to a distant relative between whom and her
husband there has always existed a special aversion.
Speaking of surprises following death, none is
so great as the discovery that the senior partner in one
of Melbourne’s oldest and reputedly wealthiest firms, has,
despite his having hen chairman of directors in several
of the sou dest Southern co.’Sj and owning several
s aliens, died insolvent. ■

During Tas. boom-time a Slowbart share-jobber
and his partner, who lived at Zeehan, had between! them
a special code for scrip dealing over the wires. The Z.
man wired one day: “Have yon any children yet?”—
“children” being the c.\ filter for certain well known
stock. The city man being out of town, the message
was delivered to bis wife, wdtom it considerably riled ;
her childless state was a source of great annoyance to iter,
and she look the telegram as some rude joke at her
expense. Her partner got a hot reception on his return ;
woman-like, she heeded not his attempts at explanalion
of the system. As a last resort he wired to the “ Co.” :
“Have 50 children. Advise what to do with them.”
When the answer came her anger was appeased.

The cable that the M'Arthur-Forrest co. has
been permitted to amend its specifications will be a
“ hard knock ”to cyanide co. promoters generally. The
M‘A -F. Co. is a shrewd Scotch firm, which seems to claim
nearly all the “ologies” known to science. A “foreigner”
first tried to circumvent it, hut he was promptly set
back. An appeal to Judge Romerended ion verdict against
it on all points, hut the co. must .have had a lot up its
sleeve. If the win is as substantial as it looks, the co.
will make millions during the next eight years—after
which the patent will be everybody’s property.

The tobacco-industry has made great strides of
late in Queensland. One million pounds were raised last
year in the province— two-thirds of the total con-
sumption was home-grown. The Treasurer’s smoke
income—excise nod duly—omnunts.to about £130,000 per
annum. Four-fifths of the persons engaged in the indus-
try are Asiatics, and the local demand is being rapidly
overtaken.

When that vile political job, the Marrickville-
to-Burwood-ioad railway, Sydney, w»as opened nine
months ago, it was plain that it would prove an unprece-
dented and ghastly failure, as there was practically no popu-
lation along the route—before the railway was opened the
traffic could hardly keep two ’buses going. The officials
have now been taken off the stations, and train-guards
supply passenger-tickets. As the line does not pay
engine-grease, the Commissioners, doubtless, will either
close it altogether, or have the work done with a dunimy-
enr and a mule-team. Such are the jobs consummated by
the Legislature and passed on to the tailway department.

For the week ended Aug. 23, COfiO tons of
Broken Hill sulphide ore were eoncentrated, shipped in
the crude state, or smelted. This is the re< ord to date.
Block 14 during the week put 2315 tons through ils en-
larged concentrating plant—the record for any single
mine.
After almost everybody who believes official
reports had settled down to the belief that, the fire in the
B. H. Proprietary’s block 11 was out, and after that plea-
sant belief had remained undisturbed for a fortnight, the
news is conveyed in a halting fashion that smoke con-
tinues to issue from the crevices at the bottom and along
the sides of the open-cut. At the moment of w riting it is
impossible to say whether there is still fire under-
ground or whether the carbonic acid gas, which
has latterly been introduced at another point, is forcing
out some smoke front parts which the gas had not before
readied. Most likely, however, there is still fire in the
mine. Several subsidences have occurred lately, and
these appear to have afforded fresh ventilation arid thus
given the embers another lease of life.
What is really causing the trouble in connection
with this Are at Broken Hill is the existence of the huge
quarry or open-cut, the bottom of which is amOngat the
timbers, rendering: the absolute cutting off of air and
vents almost impossible. When the system of square-set
timbering with Oregon was introduced it was never ex-
pected that the timbers would be exposed in this way ; so,
that, although this does hot improve the case for the
system, it removes the responsibility from the dead
Pulton’s shoulders, upon which some have placed it.
A correspondent:—Much more than a month
ago Manager Stewart expressed his conviction that the
fire was out, and the occasional whiffs of smoke
from crevices in the open-cut floor were explained
as having been liberated from underground cavities.
Physicists do not favor the theory. Smoke, it
is said, would not long retain its form under such circum-
stances ; bottle some for a week and see. The old proverb
that “ where there’s smoke there’s fire ” is substantially
correct.
Many people regarded all the efforts to put out
the tire, except the gas-application at which the manage-
ment long hesitated, as practically wasted. Water is
notoriously useless for combating mine-fires, unless abso-
lute flooding is possible. The blasting operations, to fill
up ttie cracks in the floor of the open-cut, and so
“smother” the fire, probably did no good. But the gas
was hound in time to succeed ; the only difficulty was
that it was impossible to fill the mine with gas above a
certain level, and above this the fire might continue to
burn—as it evidently has.
One of the chief points of difference between the
new and the old management of the Proprietary mine
was on the question of output. Manager Howell kept the
reduction of the limited smelting ores going at breakneck
speed, and though 15 smelters at the mine and five at
Port Pine were running, three more were leased (at £75
per week rental) from the British Co. Manager Stewart
has all along been desirous of putting on the brake—an
operation which needed to be approached circumspectly.
So that, whether the fire is found in the end to have
materially curtailed the smelting-ore supply or not, no
one expects to see the whole 23 furnaces rtinning again.
It was the 15 mine furnaces which were closed down when
the fire started, and the “south nest,” w'here the gas-
generating operations were conducted, is still idle.

B.H. Junction reports satisfactory progress
with its sulphides. Last week 580 tons crude, assaying
17oz. silver and 28 per cent, lead, produced 140 tons con-
centrates, worth 65 per cent, lead and silver—
besides seconds and slimes. Block 14 got 698 tons con-
centrates from 2229 tons crude, with good silver and lead
contents results.

Dear Bulletin,— Re the C.Q. G.M. Proprietary
at Calliope. Didn’t Dunn go up to inspect the pro-
perly? Why wasn’t his report published? The bank-
manager is made to say that 280,0000 z. gold were taken
out of N.Z. Gully in 12 months. 1 think about 20j000oz.
would be a big order. Queensland didn’t produce that
amount of gold in ’64—the year N.Z.O. was working,
though Peak Downs (a much larger and richer field) was
in full swing. Hickson and Rolls solemnly declare that
the whole gully will go 6dwt. to the load worked on a
face. H’m ! Plenty of the gully was worked for 4or
sdwt. to the load, taking from 9in. to a foot of wash.
Seems we were sinking through the good dirt to get at the
poor! It starved every one out of it long before it was
selected. There were plenty of “puddlers” on the look-
out for poor ground, and if it would have shaped up to.
Idwt. per load there wouldn’t be a yard of it left.
Manager Larkins says Jdwt. to the load will pay well for
sluicing. So it would—and if the Jdwt. had been there
the water would have been put on it 25 years ago.
What do the promoters consider a fair trial for reefs?
Spicer, Dunn, Tom Brown, Watson and Co., with a five-
stamp battery and Chilian mill, stuck to them for nearly
two years—as long as they could make anything. Arid
since I left, the local people—Kiikman, Stoney,.and
otheiw—have given them another trial; but they all had
to gi\> it best. I reckon the N.Z. Gully reefs a very poor
show in a poor reefing district. No bar, beach, or point
in the Boyne River has ever been worked, and no gully
carrying gold has ever been traced into it, so far as I
know. The gold in N.Z. Gully runs out nearly two miles
before it junctions with the Boyne. Anyone who has had
any experience in river-work would naturally conclude
that if the shallow ground was no good, the deep was
worth leaving alone. I believe the prospects that have
been got in the top drift to be due to batteiy, cradle, tom
and sluice tailings and hopperings. By the way, wasn’t
the 340 z. slug got on the “ Eastern Boyne ’’—miles away
from the co.’s claim? Mr. Sachs says “Old Miner” leaves
out of consideration the profits to be derived from dredg-
ing, beyond giving a contemptuous sniff at ’em. Well, 1
reckon he can put’em in a wine-glass and cover’em up
with a gooseberry-leaf. Matter of opinion, of course
That’s mine.— Another Old Q. Mccrsiiiftbr.

“Joseph of Canaan ” has evidently struck
the underlying piety of Sydney, for business is
better at Her Majesty’s than it lias been for many
months past. Tne said business, however, looks
very unlike the usual variety; it consists mainly
of murky females in black frocks, and with their
hair combed straight back from their horny fore-
heads, and men with monkey beards and those
badly-fitting clothes which are a sign of grace
within. The whole establishment, in fact, bears
the appearance of a vast prayer - meeting.
“Joseph of Canaan” is notable chiefly for its
serious aspect and its redness. A reddish Joseph
is sold, by ten red, bad brethren, into a red land
of Egypt, and bis ten red brethren, and his old
red father, follow him there and are left high in
the favor of a bright-red Pharaoh. Joseph, who
grows redder and redder as the years pass over
his head, marries a noble-hearted red wife, after
poor old Potiphar, a stately brick-colored hero,
has died of a broken-heart. Potiphar’s wife, who
is only light-brown, repents in the last act, and
becomes a better woman, and disappears in charge
of a glaring red officer and some troops who are
the color of blood. There is no trace of comedy
in “Joseph;” if the idea of making brother
Zebulnn a funny man occurred to the author,
he promptly suppressed it, and all the
brethren are profoundly and desperately serious.
So is everybody else, and the awful Egyptian
lack of humour grows oppressive. The play con-
sists very largely of intervals, and when it is
actually in motion about half of it is processions
of priests and dancing-girls, and magnificent
scenery, and “Joseph” himself —all there is of
him—could be easily compressed into two hours.
If this were done there would be time to finish up
with the orie-act play of “Jonah and the Whale,”
with George Rignold as Jonah, and Scot Inglis as
the Whale, and Plimmer as the converted popula-
tion of Nineveh, or the Deluge, or the fall of
Jerusalem, or some other Scriptural character.
# • *
One of the most remarkable features of
‘-‘Joseph,” by the way, is the vigorous manner in
which Potiphar’s wife gives “ back-talk ” to
Pharaoh when that
capable monarch is
seated on his throne.
Pharaoh can hardly
get a word in edgewise
owing to the interrup-
tions of this aggressive
person—which hardly
accords with the
general impression of
the Egyptian poten-
tates of old, who are
generally believed to
have impaled anyone
who even breathed too
loud when the king was
offering a few remarks.
Which reminds The

by his many Sydney
friends on the 10 th

eighth anniversary of
his lesseeship at Her

* * *
Prof. Kennedy, the
mesmerist, who opened
on Saturday night, at
Sydney School of Arts,
hasn’t changed visibly
since he infested Syd-
ney a good ipany years
ago. His show hasn’t
changed, either. He
calls for subjects from
the audience in the
same old way, and an
exactly similar crowd
of seedy-looking pati-
ents bolt for the plat-
form. They are put
“under the influence,”
ami proceed to fall with
sickening thuds on their
heads, or their teeth,
or their ears, or with
their feet on top of
other patients' heads,
and to lie helplessly
about the stage in all
directions. The pro-
fessor then sticks
)arge darning-needles
jnto them, or sits on
them to show their rigidity, and makes them fall
out of imaginary balloons, and drink horrible con-
coctions of kerosene and the like, which are
alleged to be whisky, and otherwise make a
grotesque-;and hideous display of themselves in
several dozen ways. The show on Saturday night
attracted the biggest and most enthusiastic audi-
ence which could be compressed into the hall, yet
it was a coarse, brutal and purposeless exhibition,
none the less. The spectacle of dozen presumably
intelligent human beings made to surrender their
manhood to a fat showman, and reduced below
the mental level of the ape, is tolerably sickening
at any time. The average man doesn’t amount to
much at the best, but when he is persuaded that
he is an infant and howls in a cradle with a feed-
ing-bottle in his hand, and otherwise plays the
inebriated monkey lor the public amusement, he
is a show that should make any audience feel a
desire to escape from the hall and get something
to calm the upheaval in its stomach. But the
audience doesn’t bolt—any more than it would
from a hanging, or a man-and-dog fight, or an ear-
chewing match between two hulking pugilists, or
a Saturday night “scrum ” in a back street when
two drunken women are trailing each other around
by the hair and hitting each other’s heads with
beer-pots. The Bulletin drops the subject ju-t
here lest it should be betrayed into making some
severe and well-deserved remarks about the pro-
fessor. It never yet met a showman who deserved
them more thoroughly than does that hard-faced
personage.

The farther deliverances of the Countess
Wacbtmeister in Sydney haven’t thrown any
special light on the murky ways of Theosophy.
The most that can be gathered from her utter-
ances is that her creed is divided into two parts
which have no visible connection with each other.
One half of it is a beautiful doctrine of toleration,
brotherly love, and universal peace. The other
half consists of the astounding fish stories of the
’"lamented Blavatsky about certain very old and
useless men who moon away theirexistence in oaves
in a land so remote that hardly anyone can go there
to discover their absence, and who seem to do
nothing but sit hard down in a hairy and un-
kempt condition and be ineffably wise and wholly
uncomfortable. There is no visible connection
between the doctrine of brotherly love and these
alleged troglodytes, but the believer is expected
to take them all together. If Protestantism were
built on the same plan it would also consist of two
equal parts, the first being a belief in the New
Testament gospel, and the second a firm convic-
tion that John Huss could eat 144 hard-boiled
eggs at a meal without any bad effects. And
when one comes to think over it, Protestantism
is built on that same plan, or one very like it. The
Bulletin cheerfully subscribes to the thin end of
Theosophy—the end with the brotherly love and
the universal peace in it. But it lias serious
doubts about the thick end, which sets forth that
Mine. Blavatsky was supernaturally guided by a
mysterious Indian over 7ft. high, and which con-
tains the stories about the ancient seers who vege-
tate in Thibet, and the writings which drop down
from unseen hands. The trouble about the thick
end of every creed is that the miracles never com''
to convince you unless you believe already, n>•<’
then you aren’t in a condition to investigate (h •

Canaan, ” at Sydney Her Majesty's Theatre.
if they do come. Even the believer, however,
seldom sees anything; all he gets is the Copntess
Wachtmeister’sassurance that Madame Blavatsky
told the hairy old Colonel Olcott that she saw
something.

Dudley Wyatt, the calculating boy, is the
newest sensation at Sydney Empire. In his own
dreary art this very small youth—he seems to be
seven or thereabouts—is truly remarkable. He
can mentally add up, subtract, multiply, and
divide, huge sums that run into billions in a few
seconds, and can tackle mountains of fractions
and decimals almost in a breath. Nothing in the
way of figures comes amiss to him ; anyone who
wants to know how long it will take a man to go
100| leagues and | of a furlong at the rate of 19f

That airy nothing, “ Gentleman Joe,” will
be performed for the last time at Sydney Lyceum
on Friday, and Saturday begins another six-nights’
season of “In Town,” After that, the “ Gaiety,
Girl’’ will be resurrected to till the brief remainder
of the season. The management is probably well-
advised in thus arranging things, for “Gentleman
Joe” is too weak to hold its own for any
lengthened period. It is a well-meaning produc-
tion, but it suffers from advanced tuberculosis of
the libretto, or something of the kind, and the
best thing for it is complete rest.

“ Ma Mie Rosette ” revived itself at Melb.
Princess’s, on Saturday, with a good deal of the
original success. Nellie Stewart has departed,
but the opera remains as intrinsically naughty as
before. It exhales the same spicy perfume of un-
hallowed love, old associations cling to the catchy
melodies, and Brownlow’s regal legs stand where
they did. Miss May Pollard, who has many a
time fallen into Nellie’s characters, and always
fallen gracefully, is a very likeable Rosette. Her
stride is somewhat pngey : she hasn’t the florid
Gothic innocence of Nellie’s rustic manner in the
first act, but her dream of what might happen to
a damsel, after bringing fresh milk to the king’s
chateau every evening for three months, is forci-
bly interpreted by May Pollard. Also, she has
the courage to sit upon the chateau stair-
case and sing “I Was Dreaming,” the loud
encore which she wins for that hackneyed
ballad being more than well-deserved, under
the circumstances. Another change from the
first cast of "Ma Mie Rosette” is Miss
Nash, as modest Martha who knows a lot. The
new Martha’s dance with Lauri brought down
the house on Saturday, and went far to explain
why she had already planted three hu-bands.
She must have rushed ’em into early giaves.
Brownlow, Tapley, and Howard Vernon each
takes up his part in the familiar style of yore, and
Corisandre improves on her previous work. Just
now Miss Young is singing equal to two ordinary
artists. She gets no good solo opportunity in this
opera, but her voice is particularly valuable all
the same.
• • •
On Saturday they will set up “ Ma’mzelle
Nitouche,” supplemented by a one-act musical
comedy, in place of the Living Pictures that used
to HU up the programme. The title of the novelty
is “An Arcadian Eve,” and it is “ by Alfred
Plumpton.” If Plumpton wrote the book as well
as the music, the Eve ought to be an entertaining
young woman.
** * *
The last profit of Bland Holt’s wondrous
season at Melbourne Royal is “The Prodigal
Daughter,” a drama which, besides being almost
reasonable, is full of that noble animal, the ’orse.
In fact, there is so much horseflesh and stable in
the plot that the erring daughter seems of very
little consequence. She comes back to the dear
old home in the final act, and stands concealed at
the rear of her venerable dad, whilst he pretends
not to know that she is there, but nobody would
feel hurt if the young woman died abroad, and
sent a few parting words to her family by post.
Bland Holt and joyous Mrs. B.H. are the most
interesting figures in this entertainment. The
manager is a head lad of shaky principles who
feels tempted to nobble the favorite for five
hundred quid, but becomes commendably honest
when he can’t very well help himself, and the
lady is a frisky quakeress. Walter Baker is less
convincing than usual. He gives the audience to
understand that he has gone off to ride the
favorite, or break his neck, and he even presents
himself after the race as the winning jockey.
Yet, in the meantime, an unknown hero, who
weighs, at a guess, fully four-stone less than
Walter, has been seen taking the water-jump in
Walter’s colors. There is some mystery here
winch the drama doesn’t clear up. Miss
Elizabeth Watson’s appearance as the prodigal
daughter is disappointing, after her more
natural acting in “Saved from the Sea.” Then
she suggested her sister Henrietta to some
extent. Possibly an attempt to improve upon
that suggestion is responsible for her entire losp
of resemblance to her clever relative. Miss E.
Watson’s voice, as it happens, is a gloomy remin-
der of the dirge introduced to Australia a while
ago by Miss Mabel Hardy (of the Dacre Go.).

to keep its gates ajar, and the “b,oy” pianist of

seems to have lashed an unsympathetic city into a
state bordering on enthusiasm.
** * *
Proposals for building a new theatre in
Adelaide assume definite shape; the site fixed is on
North Terrace, in the centre of the city. Hudson,
the minstrel comedian, and Williamson are protni-
nent in the negotiations, but there is a difficulty
through the heavy terms demanded by the owner
of the freehold.

The British consumer is in doubt as to
whether exported Australian meats undergo
proper examination. S.M. Herald
rushes to the rescue by asserting that there is no
cause for alarm, as it finds that the exported
article is subject to the same sanitary regulations
as “that killed for our own use.” A stranger
walking through Sydney would belittle impressed
with the community’s daintiness. He would find
everywhere meat exposed outside of butchers’
shops, and overhanging the filthy pavements—a
vigorous salesman, with a broom possibly, stirring
up the dust to insure its due incorporation with
chops and steaks, the while some subject of bron-
chitis or consumption stood by and spat.
Last week, some Sydney cattle-salesmen,
charged with having sold cattle affected by tuber-
culosis, pleaded that the diseased animals had
been passed by the inspector, and that it was im-
possible, in most cases, to detect tuberculosis in
live beasts. The plea is a sound one—and is only
an argument for the close inspection and remorse-
less condemnation of every tnherculosed carcase.
The disease is clearly transmissible through meat
to human beings, and one affected bullock may be
the means of endowing with consumption a dozen
or 50 people. It will be well if the demand from
abroad for the scientific inspection of every carcase
intended for export, leads to inspection of every
ounce of meat wiiich is to pass into local consump-
tion—The Bulletin had almost written tuber-
culosis.

This time the Deeming blessing. After
the hero was hung, Miss liounsevell, who so
narrowly escaped being bis victim, tried life as a
barmaid in Broken Hill and as a milliner in
Temora (N.S.W.), and found it none too pleasant.
But the blessing was working. A former admirer,
now a prosperous Canadian citizen, bought “The
Last Confession of Deeming,” read of his old
'flame’s'‘peril, and thought many things. Result :
Mi-s Ronnsevell a few weeks ago accepted his
offer of marriage, sailed from Sydney for Canada,
and by this time, doubtless, has achieved woman’s
destiny. Perhaps the Curse is entailed on the
ma'e line.

A Sydney newspaper correspondent ob-
.ects to the Philharmonic Society performing the
oratorio “ Mary Magdalen,” on the ground that
Mary isn’t fit to be introduced to respectable
people. Considering the unhappy fact that Mary
Magdalens are a positive necessity to monogamic
civilisation ; that chastity is inevitably nourished
on prostitution—the purity of shielded women
being only made possible by the sacrifice of the
shieldless to the sex-MoIoch; and that, conse-
quently, the prostitute is the greatest bulwark of
respectability, this is merely another illustration
of the fact that mealy mouths mean reeling brains
—as well as selfish instincts.

The case of Johnson, Mrs. Butler’s im-
prisoned brother, is slowly percolating through
the Silurian rocks of official ceremonial. As his
eventual release is beyond doubt, The Bulletin
suggests that he be let out at once on suspicion of
being innocent, while the forms are gone through
at leisure afterwards.

In view of everything which has occurred
lately in connection with the ill-luck of a certain
Judge—and the very remarkable ill-luck of those
tried before him —would it not be well to appoint
a Commission (one more or less would hardly
count) to re-try the whole of a certain class of
cases and review all sentences with which his
Honor has been associated—even, if necessary, to
revise all future ones ?
* * *
There will have to be a commission of
inquiry into the Snffield case. Sn(field was con-
victed before Judge Windeyer and sentenced to
10 years’ imprisonment on a charge of attempted
rape at Newcastle, N.S.W. From first to last
Snffield, a most intelligent young man of previously
excellent character, strenuously protested his
innocence. There is more than one plausible
theory, utterly at variance with the Crown case,
as to the real facts of the matter; and at New-
castle, where all the parties are best known, it is
widely believed that a terrible injustice has been
done. In a few days there will reach the N.S.W.
Minister of alleged Justice a petition signed by
many thousands of persons for a commission of
inquiry.

a Melbourne insolvent lias disappeared
Rnd - s much wanted by tlle bankruptcy officials.
Accordingly they summoned his wife before the
court, and cross-examined her for nearly an hour
as to the lost one’s whereabouts, but all to no pur-
pose. “I don’t know where my husband is,” was
the substance of her answers; “I never hear from
him or of him. I don’t believe he’s in Victoria.
I don’t believe he’s in Australia. I don’t believe
he’s anywhere.” “ Yes,” said Judge Molesworth ;
“ the best witness for a man is his wife or his sweet-
heart.” Which is true in a sense. But the worst
witness against a man is also his wife or his sweet-
heart—or his mother-in-law, or his unmarried
aunt, or his hysterical daughter, or his elder sister,
or the woman next door.

MISUNDERSTOOD.
SHE (a member of the W.[?].T.U.): “My dear man, will you read
this little tract entitled 'The lips that touch wine shall never
touch mine!”
HE : “My oath they won't! I'm not so drunk as all that!”
Doctors talk of an awful awakening in
store for the festive D’isy and her husband who
start married life on a bicycle built for two. By
the same token, there seems to be a possibility of
Parliament passing a law in the dim future, pro-
hibiting the banns between Angelina and any
member of a wheelman’s league. Which seems a
matter for little Angy to take to heart imme-
diately. The other day a young married couple
of companion bikists went to a Melb. physician
with a tale of nuptial woe, which they, in their
innocence, had reckoned unique. The confidential
adviser undeceived them as to the supposed singu-
larity of their case. He told them of three other
unhappy pairs who were suffering from the same
affliction. Moreover, he anticipates a steady
rush of muscular Christian husbands to his con-
sulting-room anon, when they shall have attained
the age of 35 or thereabouts, and found that the
patent pneumatic rolling machine has unwrung
their withers beyond recall, so to speak. This
medicine man foresees a great decline in local
production as the result of bicycles. His pre-
scription for the two patients aforesaid is worth
mentioning. He ordered them to discard their
bike, and advised them to give it to some poor,
struggling couple whose family had already
exceeded the limits of prudence.

On Tuesday week, at St. Mark's, Darling
Point, Sydney, Wm. Bede Dailey, eldest son
of the late W. B. Dailey, was married to
Miss Pauline Lo Monnerie dit Fattorini. Last
week, by the way, ‘‘Plain Bill’s” second son,

visit to Australia. Facially and otherwise he
much resembles his lamented father, and is deter-
mined to succeed at the Bar,
* # *
There are 30 J’s.P. in Carlton (Melb.),
and ye 1 wlvu a batch of prisoners was trotted
into the local court, the other day, there was not
a single magistrate available. The police rushed
round hunting justices, but had no luck; so
accused were solemnly marched back to gaol,
where, however, the prevailing authority refused
to accommodate them without remand-warrants
signed by a P.M. or a J.P. Then the hunt began
afresh, and, finally, one of the great unpaid was
roped in, and the prisoners were gaoled pending
the convenience of the cheap and nasty magis-
tracy. It is now quite understood in Melbourne
suburbs that the law holds every man guilty pend-
ing the convenience of the J.P. Honorary magis-
trates are too dear at the price.

Medical etiquette is
not respected everywhere.
In Castlemaine (Vie.) a
married woman, who had
been under the care of
Doctor X. for several
weeks, remarked to him
as she paid his bill :
“That’s a lot to give
you for nothing, doctor.”
Being told that it was
more than nothing to
save her life she informed
the astonished physician
that it was not he, lint
God Almighty, who had
cured her. “ Well,” said
he, “ that was why God
Almighty made you send
for me. If he had meant
you to die he would have
made you go to that old
fool Blank instead ! ” | |
The Austral Salon is working hard for the
Vic. artists’ carnival. Gifts of flowers, ferns,
lattice porches, carpentry work for stalls, cigars
and sweets have been promised to the persuasive
Salonites. Now and then difficulties occur. One
was with artist Ford, who had mapped out a com-
plete scheme of costume and scenery. One stall
was to be a garden where refreshments were to be
sold by comic-opera peasant girls in short skirts
and red stockings. But, unfortunately, the ladies
running this are married and matronly in propor-
tion. They objected to the skirts and stockings,
and though Ford battled hard for his elaborate
costume-scheme it had to be modified. But the
man who runs up a back-lane when he sees a
Salonite approaching is George Gordon. The
poor fellow at a meeting—mostly consisting of
the aforesaid matronly ladies—hastily remarked
that he hoped “ the younger and more energetic
ladies would interest themselves,” &e. It was
particularly unfortunate, because the energy and
enthusiasm has been with all the weight-for-age
members. The consequent horrible silence and
stony glare of 20 outraged dames paralysed
George, his legs gave way, and he dropped back
into his seat.
* * *
“ Lottie Collins on the one hand, and the
starving seamstress on the other, are the two poles
of our city civilisation,” says Labouchere :
It is cabled that the French army in
Madagascar is getting very near to the capital,
and that the missionaries in that city have nobly
refused to retire with the retreating Hovas, “ pre-
ferring to die at their post.” This sounds large
and noble and gorgeous, but as the French army
isn’t likely to kill any missionaries—it is much
more probable, in fact, that the officers will invite
them to dinner and fill them with good liquor
the self-sacrifice isn’t worth cabling about. If the
missionaries retreated along with their flocks they
would have a lot of hard walking to do, and would
have to leave their furniture behind them, and
would probably get killed at last in some casual
scrimmage, or take fever, or otherwise come to
grief. So they wisely remain behind in their
comfortable quarters and cable that they are
‘‘dying at their post.” And if The Bulletin
was a missionary, and had the alternative of
waiting to take omelettes, Benedictine and coffee
witli a hospitable invader, or retreating through a
trackless forest with its old sermons in its tail-
pocket, it would do the exact same.

A Buenos Ayres correspondent writes to
WALTZ TIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
A dance of dreams floats idly from the band
In a rich ball-room. He among the guests
Whose very presence all her soul invests
With subtle majesty of vague command,
Drifts with her darkly to a shadow land
Of ghosts who spoil the freshness of fair breasts.
His pleading eyes preferring strange behests
Thrill through her, and the magic of his hand.
The waltz ends, but her pulse beats madly on.
In time to surging strains of passion song,
His lying tongue has whispered in her ears,
And purpose sweet-voiced, slaying slender years,
Sweeps her in seas of ecstacy along
Into a grey night’s endless horizon.
N.S. Wales.
HESEETH.
TO A MISOGYNIST.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
You damn all women as wantons or worse
For a lover proved false in the days gone by.
There are women to worship as well as to curse—
And vows will break while the sun rides high !
Had you never a sister who held your hand
As you loitered together in Babyland,
Who guided your steps to the brightest bowers
Where the life-dawn flushed on the fairest flowers ?
Had you never a mother who heard you lisp
Your baby prayers at her dear old knee
Before Love’s flame like a will-o’-the-wisp
Had lured you away to the storm-tossed sea?
Had you never a lover—before this last—
True to the dream ere the dream had passed ;
Never a token—a tress or a curl —
To bind your life to one true-heart girl ?
Yon damn all women as wantons or worse
For a lover proved false in the days gone by,
Say, was the blame of it—all of it —hers ?
We are not so immaculate, you and I !
GLENROWAN.
Gay jewels gleam and glisten on the wanton
dancer’s crest,
While in the slum the mother pines with baby at
her breast;
Vice stalks in royal robes abroad while Virtue
bows and begs,
They starve who raise but children —they thrive
who raise their legs.
P. LUFTIG.

O' - a train between Adelaide and Melbourne on a
Photo. by Talma.
Miss JULIET WRAY, the new Australian prima-donna.
charge of “ grog,” recalls to a Bulletin correspon-
dent aS. A. incident of about five years ago. “An
Irish lady said to me: ‘You are a great friend
of the Commissioners’. The boys have lost their
places and I want you to get them put on again.’
I found that ‘ the boys ’ had been in charge of a
goods-train which had failed to put in an appear-
ance in reasonable time. The stationmaster grew
anxious, as a passenger-train would be shortly
along, so he dispatched a man on a tricycle, who
picked up the train eight miles distant. Seeing
no one about he climbed on board ; a bottle and a
pannikin were vertical, but the men were hori-
zontal and sound asleep. He roused them up and
started them off. At the enquiry their excuse
was that they had.pulled up the train to have a
smoke and presumed that in the heat of the
weather they had fallen asleep. Discharge re-
sulted. ‘Well,’ I said, ‘I consider that they
thoroughly deserved it.’ ‘Not at all, she re-
plied, ‘ if they had 'gone on they should have had
dismissal —for there might have been an accident;
but they should have been commended and not dis-
missed for their prudence in stopping.’ ”
* * *
Pastor Bevan, of Melbourne, asks, apropos
of the Chinese massacres, “Where would England
be to-day if it were not for her missionaries ? ”
Nobody answered—people are not allowed to
answer in church, by the way—so it is taken as
conclusively proved ■ that England is now in a
desirable situation not very well defined; that but
for its missionaries it wouldn’t be there, but some-
where else also not very well defined; and that in
order to retain England where it is, wherever that
may be, and prevent it going to the other place,
wherever that may be, it is necessary to
keep the missionary craze going with energy.
All the same, there is no logic in the
kind of argument which consists in wav-
ing one’s arms in the air, and asking where
something would have been but for something
else, and what would have become of the thing
that is if the thing had been that wasn’t.
“Where would England be if it were not for her
missionaries ?” Where would Gladstone’s high
collar be if the British had been licked at
Waterloo? Where would Parkes’s baby Cobden
be if Dibhs had assaulted Julius Ctesar at New
York? Where would anything be, for that
matter, if it wasn’t there, or if something else was
absent ?
• # *
At a R.C. church bazaar held the other
day in South Melbourne, one of the side-shows
was a tug-of-war between six priests and six
lawyers. The lawyers were never in it; the six
priests simply gave one mighty heave and dragged
the legal men all over the place like a bit of meat
on the end of a string. The average lawyer hasn’t
much muscle these times—he is worn out with
waiting for the client who never comes, and with
the haunting dread of becoming a “Gent one.”
Meanwhile the priest prospers very much as of
old. So long as Gehenna keeps in good blazing
order the church will never want, and when the
average father takes the end of a rope in his two
big hands, and hangs the weight of his brawn on
to it, he is equal to three ordinary attorneys or
even more.

A picture : the disappointed surgeon. A
very clever practitioner had, the other day, an
extremely delicate and urgent operation to per-
form upon a woman who was in a low condi-
tion, and had, in any case, but a mere outside
chance of recovery. The operation was per-
formed with remarkable speed and machine-
like precision, and when the cutting, sewing and
bandaging were done, the great medico began to
glow with pride at having accomplished
another masterpiece. Suddenly he darted at the
patient, raised her head, and endeavored to force
brandy between her set teeth. “ Drink, woman,
drink ! ” he cried fiercely. But at that moment
the woman died. Letting the head fall roughly,
the artist flung himself out of the room mutter-
ing : “Damn it! Damn it! Damn it all 1 ”

with a mine, plans, reports, and, above all,

As a matter of Kerristian fact all Sunday-school

Three frightened
Judges composed N.S.W.
Full Court on Friday
week. Rev. James Clark,
of Kogarah, who was apply-
ing for leave to petition the
Privy Council, had been
seen in the train in a state
of high-wrought excite-
ment ; and some of his
fellow-passengers, fearing
trouble, had reported the
fact to the Judges. The
information made them
noticeably nervous. A
posse of tip-staffs was sta-
tioned behind the rev.
gentleman, two policemen
were ready for an emerg-
ency, and the most bellicose
of the Judges tempered ,his
usual roughness, and kept
himself well in the back-
ground. However, no
blood was shed.

Melbourne recently had a sensation which
would serve as a congenial text for Conan Doyle.
An old pensioner named Morrison, formerly
belonging to the Govt. Printing Office, was the
leading character. Morrison was a venerable
person of 70, and lived with a sister aged about
60. The sister disappeared one day, and about
the same time the pensioner apparently went off
his venerable head, and took to going about the
street with a jug of beer in his hand and his pants
worn wrong side to the front. This went on for
about a week, till at last a friend called on
him, and found him sitting on the bed with
his clothes on upside-down, smiling amiably at
things in general. He explained vacantly that
his sister had “been misled by another woman,’.
which, considering the lady’s age, seemed m
probable. Next evening the friend called agan
and found the decayed printer sitting in the same
condition, and still smiling amiably at things in
general, but apparently too far gone for explana-
tions. Then a search revealed the old womau
lying dead, in her nightdress, on the floor of an
adjacent room. So the authorities came along
and induced the ancient civil-servant to get into
Miss ADA COLLEY, the young Sydney soprano.
his clothes properly, and took him away to gaol
to be medically examined, and when last heard
from he was still smiling cheerfully at the universe
and getting into his pants wrong side to the front,
and trying to put his boots on his head as hereto-
fore.

At a recent meeting of Newtown (Sydney)
W.C.T.U., a Miss Pemell “gave a short address
from the words, ‘Why does a donkey prefer
thistles to oats?’” A Bulletin man, who has
been hypnotised by the W.C.T.U. “Superin-
tendent to Influence the Press,” supplies the
following answers : —(1) For the same reason that
a woman prefers hugging a sappy bachelor to
recording her vote for another woman’s husband.
(2) Because it is nicer to kill your own man with
bad cookery than it is to work slippers for a mar-
ried parson’s corns. (3) Because a donkey can
choose his own thistles, but he has to take any
oats that he can get. (4) Because a donkey
doesn’t prefer thistles to oats—especially wild
oats.
# * *
A year or two ago a Wellington (M.L.)
lady obtained a divorce, the court giving her
custody of her child. She married again. Then
she deserted her new husband, leaving the infant
behind her as a little item of flotsam which the
wave of matrimony had deposited at the second
old man’s door. The second husband hunted for
her to no purpose. Then he called on the first
husband and offered him the infant, but it was
rejected with contempt. Then he applied to the
magistrate, who refused to give an opinion. So
at last the step-father walked into the police-
station in broad daylight, and formally deserted
his encumbrance by leaving it on a bench, in order
to compel the police to sue him for maintenance
and get a legal decision on the matter. When last
heard from, the court was still struggling with the
case and getting no “forrarder.”
The only case at all like this which The Bul-
letin remembers is that of a Vic. farmer still
living. He married three times, and all his wives
died in quick succession. The first one left her
mother (a partially-blind old lady) on his hands.
The second bequeathed him a depressed maiden
aunt with a chronic cold, and he accepted her also
in a chastened spirit. And when the third was
dying she implored him to take in and provide for
her elderly half-sister, who was crippled by a
spinal affection, and the patriarch manfully took
her in also. He didn’t marry again, probably for
fear of having some more crippled old women
left on his hands, but the three look after his
house carefully and live harmoniously together,
and the grizzled fanner plants his potatoes cheer-
fully and acknowledges that things might be a lot
worse. x
“Still Apart,” song, by Dr. MacCarthy, as
sung by Mine. Enriquez, has won a pronounced
success.*
Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson and Sons, of
Collins-street, Melb., ore exhibiting a lady’s habit
which will be of interest to equestriennes. The
danger of being dragged by the skirt has militated
against the pleasure of many a run, and a simple
fall has only too frequently culminated in a serious
accident from this cause. By a clever device, this
danger has been overcome in a patent-sabty
riding-skirt, which the manager of their ladies'
tailoring department—Mr. B. King—has cleverly
designed. It is well worth inspecting.*

September 3, 1895,
My Dear Moorabinda—
Toe dressing in “Joseph of Canaan,” at Her
Majesty’s, is highly emotional. I have seen
nothies; so close to the knuckles as Maud William-
Bun’s flesh-pink temptation draperies in the second
act since Airs. Brown-Potter rushed out of the
t-mple of Sestos in a nightgown to rescue
Bellow, narrowly draped in a blue scarf. It is one
ot the cleverest simulations of startling sartorial
impropriety I have ever seen, and Miss William-
son (who has been playing impossibly virtuous
heroines up to this) deserves much praise for the
way she extracts every ounce of seductiveness out
of her clinging garments without any vulgarity
whatsoever. If a thing’s worth doing, by all
means do it well, and the way Mrs. Potiphar
coquets with her pink gauze shawl fluffed with
white swansdown makes the d.c. hold its breath—
as it most certainly should. The Potiphar scandal
was a very spicy on dit in its generation, and
there ou : jht to be a lot of promiscuous blushing
about the house when it’s reproduced as a tableau-'
vivant —that is, if the mummers know their biz.
Maud Williamson made a Bernhardtish-looking
Mrs. P., her narrow, expressive eyes being
most effective; but no actress on earth could-
make a great hit with 'the tame, ridiculously
modern dialogue ascribed to Potiphar’s wife,
whose Ternpe-refuge sort of repentance in the last
act was altogether disproportionate to the times —
taking a line, that is, from the unconsidered
goings-on of highly-respected representative
prophets. Also, her speech on woman’s in-
humanity to the woman who trips was as archaeo-
logically incorrect as a spring costume on the
Sphinx. There isn’t half an inch of “fat” for
Mrs. Potiphar (or anybody else, for that matter)
la the whole play—not one line a hair’s-breadth
above w.p.b. commonplaces. The play succeeds
by reas m of good acting, subtle costuming, fault-
less staging and marvellous scenery. It’s a Lord
Mayor’s show for glory, and a picture-gallery of
Eastern manners.
The toilet of George Rignold as Joseph, the
model co-respondent, passed through many vicis-
situdes, but ended smartly in white embossed vel-
vet with jewels. Indeed, magnificence reigned, and
the pomps of Pharaoh’s court can’t be depicted
without colors. The costumes of the Osiris ballet-
girls argiud that they were all little Mrs. Poti-
phars at heart; their primitive dressing—a cloud
of star-spangled black gauza over buff apparently,
suggested girl en aspic. By the way, the pictorial
success of “ Joseph ” owes a great deal to the
quantity of realistic nose carried as an everyday
thing by Her Majesty’s stock co. It is a great
advantage—if you want to stage scriptural stuff—
to have your supecs. recruited from the tribes of
Judah, I never saw anything so absolutely real-
istic as Joseph’s brethren seen in profile.
The musical event of last week—in fact, the
musical event of the year—was the Kruse concert,
at Sydney Centenary Hall, under the tegig of the
Much-Travelled Smythe, and in the presence of a
full-sized audience. It was a treat to see the
people’s faces coming out; they all seemed to be
(metaphorically) licking their lips, like so many
cream-stealing cats. The show was good all
round. Audiences who pay ss. a seat resent
having to “ make allowances” for the performers
—but, at the Kruse affair, the boot was on the
other foot, its artistes did the overlooking of the
want of genius on the audience’s part. For
instance, at the conclusion of the andante
movement of a seraphically-played Mendelssohn
trio, when Kruse was drawing a soft sostenuto to
its sweet, trembling conclusion, premature ap-
plause fell and mangled the whispering sound,
and the movement finished like a fox-hunt. Then,
in the final Vieuxtemps concerto, there were Goths
rushing out between the movements, under the
very nose of the artist. It strikes me that there
are politer ways of catching your tram—sitting
near the door, for one, or leaving before the final
item. By the way, the Kruse co. also suffered
above the ordinary from an ant-like procession
behind of people of no importance, loaded to the
front teeth, one could see, with soul-sickening
compliments. The artists’“crown of sorrow” is
the mob the interval brings. Another dreadful
thing was the handing up of a full-grown, able-
bodied laurel wreath to Kruse. The last lob of
laurels I saw went up to Elton and Kyley, at the
Munkhouse matinee. One of them hung his honors
on his ear, and the other his on his tall hat.
Kruse is indeed an artist. His music percolates
—you take it home with you. He is not only a
musician, he is an explorer who goes about dis-
covering human souls. You don’t notice details.
Drowning people don’t criticise the color of the
water, and Kruse drowns you in real melody.
He is a most'self effacing genius, too, although he
is the chosen of Joachim, the husband of what
Johnny Sheridan would call a “counter,’’ and
the owner of a genuine Strad. Any of these
would be enough for most people to strike an atti-
tude about, but, with all three, Kruse remains the
picture of male modesty in evening-dress. Yet,
when you come to think, it really isn’t such a sur-
prising thing that there should be no flies on
Kruse. Madame VViedermann, wife of the Aus-
trian Consul at Melbourne, and ex-pnma-donna
of the Imperial Opera, Vienna, was “honorary
vocalist.” Her style was altogether admirable,
boldly operatic in very classical solos by Wagner
and Brahms; and her toilet of cream brocade, with
black lace fichu, daffodils and diamonds, was in
good taste. Signor de Beaupuis (piano) and Herr
Hattenliach (’cello) also appeared_ to be artists of
high degree. But the moon turns to cheese when
the sun’s up. It was emphatically a Kruse con-
cert. .. .
By the way, if anybody thinks there is n°
diversity in the way a man can “do’’ his hair,
they ought to compare the coiffures of the Kruse
concert co. The star himself was clipped at the
rear, with the vegetationwaving at curling length
on top. Signor de Beaupuis cultivated a long
black wisp, which hung over his.forehead like the
curl of the naughty little girl—or a horse’s fore-
lock ; Herr Hattenbach wore a sawdust-colored
coryphee crop ; and Mr. Sykes (who accompanied)
was neatly barbered. It lias never before been so
strongly borne in upon me how easy-going Euterpe
is where the hair-dressing of her votaries is con-
cerned.
At Sydney Opera House, the other afternoon,
Dudley Wyatt, the calculating boy, inaugurated
the infant-phenomenon season, and gave a medical
and press view of himself, in a red plush suit, to a
stallsful of 'more or less professional persons.
Nearly all small boys are calculating, in a general '
sense, but Dudley is a daisy, who mentally does i
arithmetical puzzles, repeats myriads of tiresome
figures, solves problems,-and works sums with the
ease of a small boy devouring a bun. The only
drawback to Dudley is that nothing short of a.
rival prodigy could possibly say, at the moment,
whether his snap-shot snmming-np was correct or
not, for lie rattles off totals that would take
mathematicians whole hours toarrive at with the
aid of black coffee and wet towels. However, the
figures are all there to be copied down and proved,
but I am quite prepared—judging by the football
shape of Ins head—to take Dudley’s word for it.
By the way, if the calculating hoy’s bear-leader
made an effort to speak better English it would
make the show still more convincing. ’Twas
amusing, too, when said bear-leader conjured the
crowd not to give Dudley any logarithms off-
hand, whereupon everybody looked as if that was
just what they would have done in another
moment.
Talking about prodigies, it is cabled that Miss
Maude M’Carthy, the talented little violinist,
daughter of Dr. Charles W. M’Carthy, of Sydney,
has played before Queen Victoria and smaller
royalties at Osborne. Miss M’Carthy is going to
be a Johann Kruse in petticoats, and is another
artistic feather in Australians cap. London Times
recognises the leaven of a very great genius in the
child, who is even now completely installed in the
most exclusive artistic circles at’Ome. Austra-
lians will watch her career with interest.
The latest vice-regal news (there’s been a paucity
lately relieved by the publication of two rather
heavy-looking photos, purporting to be the Misses
Brand) is of a distinctly encouraging nature. It
seems that the promised Governor Hampden half
thought, ’pou me word, doncherknow, he’d trot
across to Australia!! by the same tub as his Excel-
lency and Lady Victoria Buxton and suite, but
on enquiring as to Sir Tough Eowell’s tastes he
decided that the following week’s steamer would
suit him better. It is reassuring to know that
the imminent N.S. \V. Gov. doesn’t run to tea-
and-toast and mothers’ meetings in his moments
of recreation, but it’s a pity, all the same, that he
didn’t endure a voyage with the saintly brewer,
since Tough Eowl’s tub lands him, bar accidents,
in neat time for Melbourne Cup, on Ist Nov.,
while Hampden, who is a sport, will be days after
the fair. Always back a saint for a front place
when the curtain goes up, even if he’s only come
to draw a moral.
Lady Hampden is said to be a replica of Lady
Jersey, but this doesn’t apply to her photos.,
though these are said to do but indifferent justice
to that “decisive dame’s speaking physiognomy.”
Lady H.’a Force of Character seems to me to be
the only real rock ahead in our roseate vice-regal
future. In an enumeration of the necessary attri-
butes of a colonial Governor, an “ex-privatesecre-
tary ” gives bis views on the subject in an English
journal, and firstly enumerates: “If possible, he
should be a peer, with a pleasant wife.” Now,
the question is : Does a lady with the soul of a
horse-marine and the geographical profile of the
Cavendishes seem likely to come under the head-
ing of a pleasant person ?
Popularity is an unaccountable tiling. To
Australians who remember Sir Hercules Robinson
as a past idol, it is strange to see bow a lot of the
Cape Town folks boil about his re-appointment as
Governor. “There is only one person less
acceptable than Sir Hercules, and that is Lady
Rvibinson. Government House receptions are
still remembered.” After all, in vice-regal popu-
larity, plenty of tea-parties is the secret of success.
Which reminds me that a certain Acting-
Governor’s unpopularity dated from the time it
leaked out that for a ball-supper for nearly a thou-
sand guests he paid the Scottish sum of a little
over a century—or at the rate of two shillings per
caput! Nothing excites .latent disloyalty like
sardine sandwiches,
“The Idlers” Amateur Dramatic Club has
fixed next Thursday (12th) as the date of its
performance of “Going It,” a smart farcical
comedy. The Standard Theatre is to he the place
of action, and a large Johnny Hed attendance is
confidently anticipated, with acting vice-royalty in

cessful “ Magpies ” with another name on their
note-paper.
The very latest society wrinkle observable in
Melbourne for levying polite blackmail loaves the
good old wedding-present highway robbery a bad
second. The new notion is to give an “at home”
when an interesting family event is pending, so
that friends may gather round to gush over the
tucks on the christening robe, or the superior
finish of the pincushion, and generally admire the
gifts sent in for the approaching little stranger.
Under these circumstances the R.S.V.P. on the
invitation card might be cynically translated
“ Real Snap ; Visitor Pays," for the guests feel it
incumbent on them, of course, to add their little
mite in the form of a mug ora pair of bootees to
the mound on exhibition, and so the merry little
show works out its own div.
The ways of committees are generally very dark,
and the ways of the committee who over-manage
Sydney Ladies’ Club appear to be a shade darker
than most, judging by the feathers that are flying
at the corner of King and George streets, where
the club premises are. The altercation over the
president foisted on to the club without the help
of the ballot-box still continues with much enthu-
siasm on the part of the ordinary members, who
maintain that ten committees cannot make a
president without the formality of an election,
and who promise the unwelcome interloper a warm
time on the first official occasion. It is probable
that the first time she presides at a meeting her
own membership will be called in question, and
then, if women are still women on the good old
plan, the scene should be a study in hysterics.
However, nobody dreams of blaming the members
for the rumpus—the fault lies with the autocratic
committee, who want everything their own way.
There is not the least hope of the “president”
peacefully resigning any more than there is of a
certain Judge peacefully resigning. Masculine
arbitration would be the most sensible way to end
the business.
A batch of recent music includes “The Yaralla
Gavotte,” dedicated to Australia’s richest and
most.philanthropic heiress, Miss Eadith Walker,
and composed by Mrs. Emilie Ludlow. Coming
amongst the first droppings of what promises to
be a shower of gavottes raking the place of the
recent storm of waltzes, the Yaralla is worthily
in the van. It is a tuneful, rhythmic and dainty
composition, well-marked and harmonious, pos-
sessing the old-world air from start to finish.
Another pretty piece of work is “ Adieu,” a song,
words by Alex. Montgomery, and music by the
redoubtable Miss Maud Fitz-Stubbs, whose
speaking likeness lends additional joy to an
already artistic title-page. The sorrowful words
go hand-in-hand with the subdued music, and
though the song lias not the showiness and
screechiness of “A Broken Promise,” it will
still appeal to people who don’t care about their
music reaching for eight miles in every direction.
From South Australia comes a copy of “Thula
Waltz,” by J. Ingham Cox, in a green cover (I
mean the waltz), and dedicated to the ladies of
S.A., to whom I offer my deepest sympathy ;
and Miss Lena Brasch (Sydney) sends a copy of
her “Olga Waltz,” now scudding before the breeze
of a fourth edition.
Messrs. J. W. Johnston and Co., machine im-
porters, Sydney, are showing a gorgeous article in
“ The New Home ” sewing-machine, which works
as softly as a muffled clock, and is constructed in
very handsome and novel patterns, including one
with a flat table-top on which you could write
letters or dispense afternoon tea after the needle-
work was done. Under circumstances of this kind
a sewing-machine becomes a parlor ornament, a
great deal more lovely than the cracked piano in-
variably found there. What a pity, too, that
female cyclists don’t get some return for their
violent exertion by strengthening their calves on
sewing-machine pedals instead.
“Gentleman Joe’’ went well en matindc at
Sydney Lyceum—it is a good morning show
from all points, because the locally-made and
triumphant gowns have set all the women talking,
besides putting them on warm terms with local
frockists. Now that it has been proved, on a bril-
liantly-costumed stage, that Sydney can “make”
equal to London and Paris, the woman who con-
siders it high tone to say (at least) that she
“imports all her best gowns, of course,” will
rapidly become a curiosity. Grace Palotta and
her black-and-white blend are a combination
which will take a lot of beating by stage
elegantes, and I absolutely predict a black-and-
white Melbourne Cup lawn on the strength of it.
By the way, I hear that no local frock-builder is
responsible for Maud Hobson’s startling water-
melon gowning in Act 1.
A novel sort of “at home ” eventuated at the
very workmanlike rooms of Sydney Fencing Club,
the other Tuesday, when, at the invitation of the
President of the Cut and Thrustees, Dr. Reuter
Roth, a number of ladies assembled to see the
fun. The proceedings unwisely began with tea.
It is very undodgy to begin with tea at a hen-
party. You should hold out the promise of tea at
the finish as you hold out cold carrots before a
donkey—if you want to keep your audience. The
fencing exhibition made up into a picturesque
show: the plain walls covered with murderous
weapons—bayonets, swords, rapiers, foils, as the
bane, with padded gloves, tunics and masks as
the antidote; the lithe attitudes of the duellists;
the bare and dusty floor, the wooden benches
covered with brightly-attired femininity—all quite
magazine-pictureish.
Fencing is a healthy and graceful pastime, a
knowledge of which enables a girl to rely more
upon her parasol in an emergency than another
maiden does on her frequently-unavailable big
brother, and the only drawback to it is purely
aesthetic—the gear is so ugly. During the after-
noon one nice-looking and agile youth, who did
wonders with a bayonet and sword item, was
simply a sketch in a granny bonnet trimmed with
russet ribbons and white frilling, with a black net
veil over the face—at least, that is what it looked
like, though the awful structure was really a most
warlike bit of millinery in leather, steel and wire.
He also had a leather cuirass padded to the verge
of excessive embonpoint, and when this shifted in
the stress of combat the effect was most comical.
Still, the uglier the gear the more bloodthirsty the
sword-play. In fencing circles the more you re-
semble a pearl-diver the greater your powers.
As times go, every girl who dreams of engaging
herself to a nice boy ought certainly to know how
to defend herself. A gift of getting in neatly
with her left would be worth a lot to a
modern engaged girl when Gussie became
dominated by the idea that they must die
together. By the way, a recent Sydney society
wedding ran to a very up-to-date honeymoon, the
happy man and maid “departing on a shooting
expedition”—a gun being one of the bride-gifts.
Considering how Cupid has lately been carrying
on, this way of starting married life seems ex-
tremely apropos.
My Melb. correspondent writes o—
“ There is a really Magbificetit Person in Melb.
at present, and as ho only appears to know those
who sit amonij the most exclusively enthroned
gods on the highest-priced seats, outsiders want
to know who he is. The fashion of his garments
is so smart as to be weird. His pants are of the
pegtop order, and have huge pleats inserted at
each hip, just beside the side-pockets. His over-
coat is showy and brown, and is lined with
rustling satin, and adorned with much brown
velvet and huge smoked-pearl buttons. In any
collection of instruments of torture, his boots and
collars would hold honorable positions. Now,
who is he ?
“An amusing scene took place between I’Abb4
Titheradge and la Comtesse Olliffe, the other
evening, at Melb. Bijou. When la Comtesse is
going through spasms of remorse, L’abb6 puts a
fatherly, comforting hand on her shoulder. Owing
to Mi ss Olliffe’s huge sleeves, G.T. only sncceede 1
in clutching a mass of buckram and silk. Miss
O. wriggled slightly, and managed to shake the
hand off. At last, with a good deal of dexterity,
G.T. managed to insinuate his hand on to the inch
of silk between the monstrous sleeves and the
lady’s bare neck. Then the Comtesse and the Abb£
looked at each other and laughed, and the audi-
ence joined in.
“The exodus of Melb. society-girls to India
still continues. Fciur really pretty creatures
leave this week to spend the corning season in
different parts of the Empire. A gallant military
horse-buyer, who was lately in Melb., for three
weeks buying remounts, was snapped up by a
demure-looking little girl. These twain marry
early next year, and will live in some remote
village near Mandalay, which will probably be
almost as lonesome as Smellbourne is at present.
“ Miss Sibyl Bevan, youngest of Dr. Sevan’s
three daughters, is studying medicine, with a
view to going to China as a medical missionary.
The eldest daughter; Muriel, is a talented
musician, and is studying under Marshall Hall.
“The shabbiest-looking member of his Cabinet,
as usual, Premier Turner went to the Agricul-
tural Show luncheon in the same old brown suit
and bowler-hat in which he works in his ooice.
Sir Beef Patterson was there in frock-coat, shiny
hat and gaiters, and all the other Sir Nobody
Thingumys were attired in their Sunday-clothes.
But nothing spoils Premier George. He wasn’t
built for a show object, and he left his office to go
and speak at the luncheon ; and, having said his
necessary say, he went straight back again to his
work.
“ The stand at the Agricultural Show on
Friday, during the jumping-contest, had quite a
Cup-Uay appearance; Spring, hats bloomed all
around, like splashes of paint on a huge palette.
‘That get-up,’ said a girl just from- the Con-
tinent, ‘really brings a whiff of the Champs
Elysees.’ She referred to Florence Young, who
really did look Frenchy in a blouse of match-box
silk, worn (she glories in her IGin. shaiie) without
a waistband, tlie'divorce of bodice and skirt being
prevented by a vast gold safety-pin, her hat a
wilderness of green, and pink, and purple. Mrs.
Bland Holt, in', Chow-green silk, with bodice of
bronze-ami green shot-brocade, had on the very
first bonnet she ever wore ; it was of bronze bead-
work and plumes, and in it she looked charming.
The ladies not on the stand mostly favored cos-
tumes of gay sateen, of weird cut —even a ‘ bustle ’
was discerned here and there—boots of cruel-
looking, unyielding leather, and altitudinous hats
of plush and plumes; and all, without exception,
clutched handbags.
“The most wonderful sight in the Agricultural
Society’s show was hot fn the bull-pens, nor in the
horse-arena, nor amongst the machinery, but on
the head of Lady Holled-Smith. It was one of
the new fashionable hats. If all the contents,of a
painter’s box had been squeezed on to it, it cduld
not have contained more colors. Reds laughed at
greens, blues howled across the crown at yellows,
and pinks chased browns, and blacks, and purples’
round the brim. It was a horticultural nightmare
of a hat; and all the women said it was “ lovely.”
“ There is this to be said to the credit of acting-.
Gov. Madden : he and his family are well worth
looking at. They look well at the top of a table.
At the Agricultural Show luncheon, the Madden
girls were quite the prettiest in the room. 'Mrs.
Governor was as good-looking as any woman
of her age present. The Madden ladies on these
occasions do not pursue the Mrs. Hopetoun tradi-
tion of outvieing every other woman in the matter
pf costume. They dress with plain, wholesome,
undowdy, becoming simplicity.
“It is the regulation thing that the
opening speech at, the above show should cause
‘roars of laughter.’ The burden of a historical
speech by Gov. Nonnanby was that Victoria was
the best place in,the world to breed pigs in.
People have not yet ceased to giggle at acting?
Gov. Madden’s lovely unintentional double?
entente, at the recent show-opening.
“The acting-Governor’s daughters have pretty
hands and arms—hence their desire to divest
themselves of gloves, whenever possible. At the
theatre, the other evening, tin ir manual curves
and dimples, accentuated by rings and bangles,
were edifying the proletariat before the attendant
aides had decided on the respective merits of the
■vacant seats in the vice-regal box.
“From a private source, I learn that Mine.
Melba’s fee for singing two songs at a London
‘At Home ’ in the afternoon is 120 guineas ; and,
unless she knows the hostess very well, she never
sings more than two. Taking the average length
of a song, this figures out at the rate of about 10s.
per syllable. ‘ Oh, would I were a bird ! ’

pinned to their' breasts is becoming common in
Melbourne. /

Personal Items.
After a dozen years of matrimony, John
Burns’s wife is about to add to the population for
the first time.
George Lansell, of Bendigo (whose deepest
shaft is now being sunk to 3300 feet), was 72 years
of age on 24th August.
Card. Moran and Chief Justice Frederick
Darley were both born in Sept, 1830, the first on
the 16th and the latter on the 18th.
According to the London correspondent of
S.A. Advertiser, a niece of “Joe” Chamberlain
is to marry the only son of Vie. ex-Governor
Bowen.
Begging-priest Vaughan has been given a
free pass over Q. railway lines. This is the cheap
Boodlewraith way of acknowledging letters of
introduction —at the public expense.
Yankee sky-pilot, Joe Cook, said one
smart thing during his Sydney season. Asked
his opinion on spiritualism, he replied ; “My
opinion is this—spiritualism is Potiphar’s wife,
and I’m Joseph 1 ”
The Late HENRY WISE.
The Earl of Jersey is now running an
oleomargarine foundry near London. When up
to his neck in the business his lordship frequently
imagines himself back in Sydney amongst the
61ite of Potts Point.
It’s five years, according to oracle Cook,
since he was asked a new ethical question. Which
pretty well samples the mental inertia of the
people who hang reverently upon the ipse dixit of
the bandy-legged Joseph.
Mr. Lormer, J.P., who denounces the
corruption of the Melb. honorary magistracy, is
the beak who had the honor of being specially
objected to by defendants’ counsel when the
Mercantile Bank case was dragged as far as the
District Court.
That harmlessly nebulous personage, Coun-
tess Waciitmeister, says she lived six years with
Madame Blavatsky, “and never saw any evidence
of fraud.” The enthusiasts of any cult never do
«ee any hanky-panky, even when it is absolutely
flaunted in their fares.
Mrs. Campbell Praed, who has forgotten
more about the Australian bush than she ever
knew, writes, in her Age story, about men lying
down to die under the spinifex bushes, and stock-
men in dry country travelling with their water-
bags slung round the horses’ necks.
Members of the Victorian Alliance assem-
bled at their annual conference at Melbourne were
consternated on being told by veteran Longmore
that confirmed whisky-drinkers had seats on the
executive. The M.P. instanced ex-Premier Pat.
terson as a man who tasted, touched and handled
“ the curse ” without compunction, and frequently
beguiled others to drink in the course of politics.
The chairman then regretfully admitted that the
Alliance included in its ranks men who fought for
its principles, but were not themselves quite total-
abstainers. In this, conventional teetotalism is
very like conventional religion : it is run largely
by people who consider it a deuced fine thing for
the other fellow.
Old man Parkes was a
pretty good friend to N.S.W,
civil servants. Two of these
gentry were once “on a tangle.”
Parkes was on “in the offing.”
No. 2 tried to push No. 1 out of
the way round a corner. No. 1,
, instead, cannoned up against the
veteran, and sent him sprawl-
ing upon his back into the mid-
dleof the road. Acantankerous
prohibitionist M.P, called atten-
tion to it in Parliament. Parkes’s
reply was: “Hi hassure the
honorable gentleman that Hi
never saw hennythink of the
occurrence.” And the lurcner
is “ there,” still.
The Maoris in and about
M.L. Parliament relate that
when Louis Becke and his friend,
J. D. Fitzgerald, of Sydney,
visited the native korero in the
King country, they were called
upon for a speech. J.D.F., as a
politician, got along famously,
and quite as fast as Martin (a
young Maori who acted as in-
terpreter) could translate. But
Becke was not such a success.
He floundered through a sea of
painfully-embarrassed common-
places, and looked as if he
wished the ordeal finished. A
bevy of barefooted Maori
maidens ogled him throughout
the speech, but surely this could
never have discomposed the
author of “ By Reef and Palm !”
Eventually he blurted out:
“ This is the fu-fu-flrst time I’ve
spoken in pup-pup-public, and
I’ll tut-tut-take hlanty fine care
it’ll be the last!” Which mag-
nificent peroration the interpre-
ter repeated faithfully, hairing
the blanks, which, thanks to the
virility of the English language,
the Maoris understood and ap-
preciated without the interpre
ter’s artful aid.
The N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor-leader,
J. S. M’Gowan ; “The Labor-party is supporting
the policy of the Government because the Labor-
party shaped that policy. Deny it who can!”
Then, the Labor-party “ shaped ’’the Freetrade

T. H. H. Goodwin, again member for
Gnnnedah, N.S.W., was the Govt, surveyor who
laid out the town of Broken Hill—and made an
excellent job of it. ’ He was one of the two Govt,
officers who waxed fat on silver. Like a wise man,
he quitted his shares at the first favorable oppor-
tunity.
The Age, in along leader, piteously pleads
for an Imperial pension for the widow of Marcus
Clarke. If Marcus Clarke’s widow should have
a pension, Australia should pay it to her. It is
enough for Imperialism to “ recognise ” the talent
that is in a MTntyre, a Benjamin Benjamin, or a
Snowden.
S.M. Herald thinks the intellectual pas-
sion which impels Parkes at 80 to produce poems
is “remarkable and admirable.” No more
“admirable” than the “ passion ” which impels
an elderly whale to secrete ambergris. Both
poems and ambergris are products of disease—but
the whale’s product is worth £lO 10s. per oz.
COOLALTA VINEYARDS.
No one in Australia writes less legibly
than Howard Willoughby, of the Argus. He
sent in “copy,” the other day, on the reverse
side of which were some of his shorthand
notes. Presently the foreman was asked by a
comp. : “Which side of tins shall I set, sir? I
can read one side just as easily as the other.”
Fisher, the Nunawading (Yic.) Messiah,
is reported to be still extant. The old gentle-
man’s church amuses itself, on moonlight eves,
with a game called “ Chase the Devil Round the
Stump,’’ when the ladies and gentlemen thread the
mazes of the stringybarks, red-gums, blue-gums,
and apple-trees of the vicinage, with much gusto.
Yic. Labor-leader Trenwith is to be the
recipient of a subscription at the hands of brother
M’s.P. of all colors to help him on his way ’Ome
to consult leading London oculists. A pity the
democrats of the province have not taken action
to render the Parliamentary collection unneces-
sary. Trenwith will be rather delicately situated
when politics compel him to fall foul of generous
subscribers amongst the enemy.

A Yic. Town Hall, which for magnificence
and site would be a credit to any Australian
metropolis, was built in sections, as funds per-
mitted, and each addition has a most glaring
foundation-stone, with the names of the various
Mayors of the day deeply cut in gilt letters.
There were 18 names altogether, and of these one
man is now an Australian Cabinet Minister, three
have skipped hurriedly to other climes, four are
in gaol, one is a J.P., three are insolvents not yet
certificated, two are dead, and four others are
Heaven knows where.
Q. Supreme Court Judge Harding, who
died on the last day of Aug., after 16 years’
judicial service, was not quite 57 years of age—
very young for a Judge to die. He (though nick-
named “Bruin,” in recognition of his wondrous
“ liver ’’) wasn't a bad-natured man, with more law
than physique, thoroughly honest, and affable
enough if yon kow-towed with a proper sense of
his dignity. Harding acted temporarily as Chief
Justice during Lilley’s absence, at the end of ’B3 ;
and when, on Lilley’s retirement, Griffith jockeyed
himself into the position—to which Harding by
standing and competence had far better claim —he
keenly resented the slight and the trickery. The
old man worked till the last—proceeding with a
Case within two days of his death, though in great
pain, in order that the parties might not suffer
inconvenience by postponement.

The popular fallacy that all Ausfcrahai,
wine lacks maturity is easily refuted by the nuv>
long-established Coolalta vineyards, of which
there is a picture on this page. These wines speak
volumes for the future of this industry in
land, whence the owners frequently get letten
expressing surprise at their excellent quality and
maturity. The vineyards have been planted ovei
30 years on volcanic soil, rich in potash, lime, and
iron, and the wines have obtained medals u
Bordeaux, Amsterdam, Calcutta, London, and
the colonies.*

REMARKABLE ACCIDENT }■
AT SEA,
A VESSEL STRUCK BY A WHALE.
A strange tale of the sea was told yester-
:'y t 0 the visitors who boarded the bar-
luontine Handa Isle upon her arrival from
vlercury Bay, timber-laden. Captain A.
lobmson states that early in theaf ternbon
vatch on August 24, when the vessel was
n lat. 36deg. ISmin. south, and long.
■.ssdeg. 31min. east, two whales were sight-
ed making a course to cross, her bows,
rhey were each about 60ft. long, and were
ravelling at a high rate of speed, their
orogress being closely watched by those pa
board. When quite close they suddenly
altered their course, and came end on do
the ship. Both seemed likely to strike
abq.iit amidships, but one avoided the colli-
sion-, fty diving.‘the other hitting just abaft
the mainmast, and a couple of feet beiptf
the waterline.

-ALL TOTE AND BETTING SHOPS
TO BE SUPPRESSED.
Yesterday morning the police department
■through Sub-Inspector Tyndall, of the de-
tective office, at last took decisive action
with regard to the indiscriminate betting-
and swindling that have been carried on in
ispite of all laws to the contrary in the city
1 for months past. Sub-Inspector Tyndall,with,
one or two, other detectives, walked quietly
round the whole of the betting sho'ps- and
rooms yesterday, and peremptorily ordered
that Operations should cease forthwith/
