POLITICAL
POINTS
If N.S.W. Civil Service is to be retrenched
throughout with the same sort of nepolistio dis-
crimination as has been shown in the Works
Department, then may the Lord have mercy on
the N.S.W. Civil Service !
The S.M. Herald’s appeal to Lyne not to
continue “ flogging the dead horse of Protection ”
is probably based on the fact that Reid is just now
busy flogging that same animal, and there isn’t
room for two men to get at him simultaneously.
Federation must be far-off when Treas.
Fysh(Tas.) objects to “foreigners” (Vic. fisher-
men) casting their nets or lines into the ocean
within three miles of his island. Why doesn’t
Fysh prohibit Victorians breathing Tasmanian
air ?
Under the new S.A. Public Salaries Re-
duction Bill Ministers themselves will sacrifice
£l7O each and M.sP. £7 10s. each yearly. The
Agent-General will have to give up £307 10s. out
of his £l5OO a year, while the Governor drops
from £5OOO to £4OOO.
Charles Gavan Duffy, one of Victoria’s
oldest pensioners, i 5.79; Andrew Clarke is 71,
and Childers is 68. They draw among them
£2500 a year, and Premier-Treasurer Turner, who
wants that £2500 to help put his deficiency, feels
towards them much as Cain did towards Abel.
The calm that should pervade every right-
minded Governmentdepartment is never disturbed
now as in the days of old “ White-horse” Steven-
son, ex-M.P., the ten-years’ model of N.S.W,
road-and-bridee members, for the old man was a
more regular attendant at the offices than the
Ministers. He used to boast that he had never
made use of a committee or scrutineer nor shouted
for an elector.
The Argus says that
nationalists in Australia are
far less in evidence than the
anti - vaccinationists. But
the Aegus, with all its eyes,
has no more discernment than
a seed-potato.
It is just three years since
Bryan O’Loghlen and hia
little following of office-
seekers abandoned the Shiels
Government and thus caused
its downfall and the rise of
J. B. Patterson. And now,
with the solitary exception of
M'Coll, not one of that party
of deserters remains in the
House. Shiels didn’t amount
to much at the best, but the
country never forgave, the men
who sacrificed him to elevate
groveller Patterson.
Robert Stout opposes
the idea of levying local rates,
in Maoriland, on unimproved
land-values, on the assump-
tion that it will induce city
landlords to crowd as many
buildings as possible on their
ground and so aggravate the
slum evil. Stout’s theory
figures out somewhat as fol-
lows : If a man has £4BO worth
of laud the land-tax. at Id. in
the £, will be £2 per annum,
and if there is one house on
the property bringing in a
rental ol .£5O a year the tax
will be 4 per cent. If the
owner crowds four houses on
the same area, he may get
£l5O a year and reduce the
tax to less than 1£ per cent.
Which might pan out all right
if every landlord was a hair-
less logician, with a chess-
board mind, who would pursue
the decimal of a halfpenny to
the bitter end—but then he
isn’t.
Treas. Ward, of Maori-
land, made about the worst
mistake a politician could
make in his budget speech.
He had grown wildly effusive
about the Canadian recipro-
city treaty, and circulated a
copy of the Canadian tariff
ihowing the duties which he
had arranged to baveabolished
in favour of Maoriland ex-
porters. Then Stout got up
and grimly pointed out that
Ward was fooling round with
an old abandoned tariff of
years ago, and the most im-
portant duties which he
thought be had got abolished
Ss a special favor to Maoriland
had been altogether done away
with some time in 'l)4. The
Ministry felt so small that it
had thoughts of hiding itself
under a form, until fighting
Dick Seddon arose and stated
in general terms that Stout
was a serpent and a deceiver,
or words to that effect.
Eight N.S.W. Leg. Councillors voted
against Pigott’s motion, to the effect that a state-
ment made by Premier Reid that the Council “ * is
a rotten and corrupt House ’ was scandalous and
false.” Therefore, eight N.S.W. Councillors have
voted that the House, of which they are members,
is “rotten and corrupt.”
A bill now before S.A. Parliament makes
it penal for any brewer or spirit dealer who has a
mortgage over a publican to compel the latter to
buy liquor from him exclusively. When the
brewer can no longer sell his bad liquor to the
mortgaged publican at an exorbitant price, there
won’t be much joy left in the business.
“ The hospital-nurse dispute here,” writes
an Adelaide correspondent to The Bulletin, “ is
a bigger thing than you seem to think, and it is
not at all improbable that it will cause the over-
throw of the Kingston Govt., as the medical
trades-union and ‘Society’ (represented by the
unjustly-promoted nurses) are arrayed against
them.”
The Foreign-trade S.M. Herald admits
mournfully that “ the question raised in the
(British) House of Commons relative to the in-
spection of the cattle and sheep killed in Aus-
tralia for export as frozen-meat ” is “dictated by
a desire to establish protection to British agri-
culture.” Britain is now the only great civilised
nation that even pretends to be Free trade in
policy.
Says Sydney Telegraph ; “The proposal
to amend the tariff and impose Land and Income-
taxes was the thing upon which the verdict (at
last N.S.W. election) was specially asked for. . . .
The country’s answer was given at the polls with
equal plainness.” Yet Premier Reid and the
Telegraph both alleged at the time that they
went to the country on the one definite issue of
Upper House reform. What was the issue, and
what did the country really vote for, or did it
really give a distinct vote for any tiling?
Schey, M.P.’s, question to Premier Reid
as to whether he would afford an opportunity for
Price, M.P., to bring on his motion dealing with
Justice Windeyer was pertinent. Can justice be
properly administered, or full satisfaction be felt
by the community and those who come under the
operation of the courts, while such a serious
motion hangs over the head of a Supreme Court
Judge ? No other Premier would tolerate it —nor
would many Judges sit under the circumstances,
practically with a noose round their necks, to wait
for the dull sickening thud of Parliamentary con-
demnation, or for the coining of a reprieve.

STOLEN THUNDER.
DIBBS: "I'm getting too old to stand the fatigue of farming Ministries .”
PARKES: “ My dear Dibbs, do say something horiginal. For the Last 20 years I said repeatedly I was too
hold to ’old Hofjice !”
In connection with the Melb. honorary-
J.P. scandal, it would be interesting to learn who
advocated the appointment of those J’s.P. who
are either brevvt-rs, wine-and-spirit merchants,
serated-water and cordial makers, or shareholders
in breweries ? Fancy a bench of the Bung family
adjudicating in a case when they or their class
hold a bill-of-sale over the defendant?
Joseph Cook wouldn’t have anybody in
politics who hasn’t “ succeeded in life”— i.c., come
out on top in the universal dollar-worry. But
special capacity for dealing witli large questions
and broad general principles is often found asso-
ciated with a total lack of that aptitude for detail
which makes nine-tenths of the art of fortune-
building. And Cook, himself, is good proof of
the converse —that a pronounced cash-cultivating
talent may co-exist witli a short-range intellect
and a wooden-legged soul.
B. R. Wise (in effect) ; “ The whole aim
and end of present N.S.W. politics is to procure
tor Premier Reid a trip to England.*’

Office
THE RAILWAY-TICKET PROBLEM: ITS SOLUTION.
LADY TRAVELLR: “ Give me a first-class return to Sydney ,
please .”
CLERK: “ Yes, madam! But before handing you this ticket,
kindly take this Bible in your hand and swear that you
do solemnly and sincerely declare you will not sell to
any person or persons this return-ticket. ”
The story of the Totalisator Bill in Yic.
Assembly was that of the Quieting of Titles Bill
repeated.
If it is an undisputed “constitutional ”
axiom that N.S.W. Leg. Council cannot in any
way interfere with the details of “ loan ” expendi-
ture —as has been authoritatively asserted both
by the Assembly and its Speaker—how is it that
N.S.W. Public Works Committee has always con-
sisted of members of both Houses? What do
Legislative Councillors want in that gallery at all,
if they may not modify “ loan ” expenditure?
Again : Was there ever since the world
began so cold-blooded a set of people as the suc-
cessive N.S.W. Ministers of Justice? One in-
vents or approves of a new flap for the hangman’s
cap; his successor approves of a “humane gaol
gag.” Who can wonder, in view of these men’s
toleration of judicial scandals which fairly shriek
to God for vengeance, that the populace affects to
regard routine incidents of the cap and gag order
as typical of the administrators in question ?
“ We have got into debt with the lordly
audacity of half-a-dozen emperors.”—Symow,
M.L.A., South Australia.
Rev. Haweis, on Aus-
tralian loyalty: “I have
seen more statues of Queen
Victoria in a week in Aus-
tralia than I have seen in 20
years in London.” Rev.
Haweis might also see mors
Chinese joss-houses, and
more hideous heathen gods,
and more quaint Polynesian
frescoes of island deities, in
one week in Queensland than
he would in a lifetime in
London, and might also
draw therefrom a number of
equally startling and ridicul-
ous deductions,
Angus Cameron, M.P.
(N.S.W.), addressed a letter
to Speaker Abbott com-
plaining that he was not
chosen as one of the deputy-
speakers appointed to re-
lieve Chairman M‘Court.
Of course the reason is ob-
vious : Mr. Cameron has
declared against and was
elected as an opponent of
payment of members, and
therefore should not be ap-
pointed to any paid position
where he would have to
refuse the salary. Hence it
should be useless for the
Government to nominate
him to the Public Works
Committee, where fees would
be forced on him, to his dis-
gust. The Bulletin trusts
that members will not insult
this patriot by forcing him
into any position where he
will be offered money.
Frank Cotton, M.P.
(N.S.W.), who was always
of the inner brotherhood of
automatic Land-taxers, now
“ barracks ” for exemptions.
He was, if The Bulletin
mistakes not, the father
of the famous 13th plank of the original Labor
platform, which declared for the tax absolutely
without exemptions.
Could the democrats who cheered N S. W.
Attorney-General “Jack” Want during the late
election have seen him at the opening of Parlia-
ment “with them all on,” what would they have
thought of the declaration of his conversion to
popular views since joining the Reid Cabinet?
Had he gone into King Division in his “ Court ”
costume—knee breeches, cocked hat, and lace
ruffles —it would have lost his colleague the elec-
tion.
Strange that the Henry Georgean land
taxers like Cotton, Cook, Hollis & Co., who were
wont to talk a species of patois composed of
Georgean phrases, carry no weight in the N.S.W.
Assembly land-tax debates now in progress. On
theotherhand, men with practical knowledge of the
land question, such as Ashton, Millen and Crick,
are listened to in a House whose mental attitude
generally is “lead, kindly light, amid encircling
gloom. ”
The Westralian Government has decided
to largely reduce taxation on the ground that it is
getting more revenue than it requires. The idea
of using the surplus revenue, while the boom is on
and there is a surplus, for railway construction, is
too preposterous to be entertained by the average
Australian politician, so the borrowing process
goes on joyfully. By-and-bye, when the boom is
dead, and the bottom falls out of the revenue, and
the railway returns have dropped heavily, and the
public lands won’t sell, and Westralia is struggling
hard to pay interest on her borrowings, her people
will want to hunt up these gay and hopeful legis-
lators of to-day and hang them on a jarrah tree.
If Lyne will only leave off syndicates, and
have no dealings with anti-ant compounds, and
give up all ideas of selling the railways and tram-
ways to an English co., and officially abjure his
friend Waller, against whom there seems to be an
unaccountable prejudice, and swear off all sem-
blance of association with one or two M.sL.A.
whose names the public somehow regard with a
sinister eye, and will try to get a little more in
line with the democratic sentiment of theconntry,
he be a great success as leader of the
N.S.W. Protectionist party. He can’t possibly
have such bad luck as Dibbs—there isn’t enough
bad luck to go round. He is not commonly sup-
posed to have any emotions. He hasn’t Dibbs’s
craving to provoke a hopeless fight against heavy
odds when there is nothing to be gained save the
joy of fighting, so he will never tell the whole
globe to go to perdition, as his late chief was quite
capable of doing. And he has brains enough to
do great and good things—if he likes. But the
country has never quite made up its mind whether
he is likely to like, or not.

At Poverty Point.
Inez Bensusan, the Sydney brunette, has joined Mr,
and Mrs. Kendal’s co., and with (hem will tour the Eng-
lish provinces playing inyinue parts.
Mark Hamhourg, the lightning and thunder pianist,
was riding in an Adelaide Mar near when some laities
started to discuss his gymnastics of tire previous night.
He happened to lie sitting on their side of tire car, shut
off from view by an intervening fat man. The ladies
decided that Mark might have made quite as much noise
with far less exertion, and they were bringing in a verdict
of “rather ridiculous, I’m sure,” when the Russian artist
fleered round the stout person’s waistcoat and spoke : “ It
a all-ways de way wid some la dies dat de less dey knows
demoredey liaf to chatter.” The word “ridiculous"
had pierced little Mark’s armour of reserve.
Rose Alhu, the well-known singer, who a few years since
toured Australasia, was married lately in London to a
wealthy merchant. Her sister, Lire charming Julie, who

In Oscar Wilde’s “Importance of Being Earnest’’one
of the characters talks about emigrating, and he is
advised that he will have to choose between “ this world,
the next world, and Australia.” “The accounts I have
received of the next world and Australia,” lie replies,
“ are not very encouraging.”
An aggressively foolish comment on Oscar Wilde’s
“The importance of Being Earnest” appeared in a
M ■lb. paper. The critic found that Oscar had got one
of Ilia “smart sayings ” from France, “ where the leaders
of loquacity are held to be dentists and hairdressers.”
Evidently lie heard somebody laugh when Cecil Ward
said to Boucicault, “ You talk like a dentist,” which, by
the way, is not a peculiarly smart saying as it stands.
The stnartness lay in its application to a previous remark
by Boucicault, who was encouraging his friend to make
full confession of a certain little secret that troubled him.
“ Come, sit down and hace it out,” said Boucicault.
“You talk like a dentist,” said Ward. A plain, old-
fashioned joke, with never a suspicion of French meaning
in it. The newspaper genius missed the joke.

Vic. Parliament wrestles with a Theatres Act Amend-
ment Bill, and is.pra.v erfully petitioned by 83 members of
the profession that the law may be so framed as to secure
“ the absolute prohibition in any form whatever of con-
certs or other public entertainments on the Sabbath day,
wheth u r in licensed theatres or other places, or whether
right of admission is by payment, ticket, or collection.”
This is an intolerant attitude to assume towards the
churches. By the way, “ respectable managers ” contend
that their Monday night audiences are spoilt by the
Sunday shows at the ’alls. The people running the
S ihbath evening entertainments will not feel much hurt
if the bill is carried, judging by the fact that every Sun-
day night, at each, after the collection, a blighted
individual in an abundant dress-suit advances to the foot-
lights with a pathetic complaint t hat these entertain-
ments will have to be discontinued if, on the following
Sunday evening, so many mangled 3d. pieces and trouser-
buttons flow into the plates. Here, at least, is a bond of
sympathy between church and stage.

“Kosher”; Australians are at last repenting their
grievous errors of judgment in not early recognising the
genius of many native-born artists who, condemned in
the lind of their birth, have risen to eminence in
countries where the standard of excellence is, or should
lie, higher. In palliation of tills blunder they rolled up
gallantly to witness Miss Juliet Wray’s initial Melbourne
performance in a professional capacity, and verily her re-
ception must have been most reassuring to her. From
the very first mom nt of her appearance before the lights
she did not seem to suffer from any undue diffidence. Her
wig was fair and h»r toilette charming, hut her thin, not
to say emaciated, figure was the subject of much unfavor-
able comment, especially among the male section. How
is it that men so devotedly admire figures plump and
even buxom in Hie opposite sex? They can seldom
reflect that grace and ease of movement reach a
higher state of perfection in slim, lithe forms. Miss
Wray looked very bright and elegant in a dress slightly
above her ankles, but methinks that in tights she would
closely resemble a v iotim of the Indian famine.

Tue sight at Krusi’s M 4b. concert was a well known
N iw Woman in the three-shilling spats calmly reading one
of those pink publications which lately were de-
nounoed In the Assembly as immoral. She listened with
rapt attention to Johann, but in the intervals she read
her paper. Every time she I urned over, displaying the
illustrated pages of horrors and naughtiness, thrills of
excitement radiated all around her. When the music re-
commenced she kept her thumb on the gory atrocity she
was reading, and was again absorbed in the soulful
strains.

Dear Bulletin, —Rev. Geo. Waiters, acting, no doubt,
in his clerical capacity, has re-ciiristened Mrs. Potiphar
by the name of Ayesha. Ayeslia is a very nice name,
hut why not stick lo the one which has done duty this
couple of thousand years or so? Zuleikha is just as good
ns it was when the modest Joseph left his garment with
her for a keepsake. According to the tradition, however,
Joseph grew in wisdom as lie grew in years, and when
Mr. Potiphar died (I believe he received a little assist-
ance) Mrs. Potiphar became Mrs. Joseph. Zadig.

up prophetic possibilities. It reveals to the weary play-
wright, haggard wilh the ceaseless strain of idea-hunting,
an unworked field waiting to lie ploughed. Bland Holt
has already in his mind a spectacular production of

whale. (H. Parkes might bs specially engaged for
“lead.”) “ Before and After the Fall,” for scenic effects
and picturesque but iuexpensiv • costuming, will, no
doubt, in lime commend itself to librettisls and managers.
(A certain N.Q. functionary, when no longer permitted to
play the deuce, might lie persuaded to play Cain.) As
for tlie problem-play of the future, The Book will be in-
valuable as regards plots. “ David and Uriah,” and a
few oth Q r choice themes, dramatically treated, will biing
about the dethronement of Pinero, Ibsen, and On. for
evermore. But the true satire lies in the fact that it is
the parson who shows the way 1

Hamhourg, is all yellow overcoat, from the tops of his
shoes right up to the tops of Ins ears, surmounted by a

face has got music written all over it. He generally sub-
sides into a chair directly lie conies in, and sits somnolent
and indifferent until stirred up again. But see him on
the racecourse !

The S.M. Herald asks Lyne, the new
N.S. W. Opposition leader, not to attempt “to
flog the dead horse of Protection back to life.”
That isn’t the question at issue, or anything
faintly resembling it. The Herald should rather
implore Reid to start at oneft and flog the unborn
horse of Freetrade into existence, that animal
never having arrived yet in N.S.W. At present
it is Reid vvlio is trying to welt into activity the
deceased moke of the Lyne party by granting
Protection to the local pipe-making industry.
Y/hy not let each man wallop his own horse?
Sydney has provided a first-class horror.
A watchman, prowling round the unsavory shores
of Woollooraooloo Bay, on Ist inst., found a large
packing-case lying in a street extending down to
the water. From the case issued a fearful stench ;
the police were called to prise open the top ; and
the body of a woman covered with blood
was found inside. Medical examination showed
that apparently the Caesarean operation bad been
attempted : there was a deep gash in the abdomen,
made by skilled hands, and signs that a child
had been recently born. The woman was
young and good-looking, and her hands, in the
opinion of the daily-paper reporter, showed that
she was unaccustomed to toil of any kind. The
bottom fell out of the mystery within 24 hours
after the discovery. The girl was the daughter
of a farmer at Mulgrave, a few miles from Sydney.
She had “gone wrong” in the old, old way, and
her condition preyed on her mind. Her half-
demented threats of running away from home so
a'armed the mother that she locked away most of
her daughter’s clothes to prevent her departure ;
but the girl went out one morning scantily attired
in a wrapper and a cloak —the only costume
she could lay her bands on-and disappeared.
The police theory is that she came to Sydney and
fell into the hands of some of the ghouls who live
by illegal operations ; that the ghouls butchered
her in an unsuccessful attempt to earn their fee ;
and that they finally shoved the remains into a
box and dropped them in a back street in the
hope that they wouldn’t be traced. Four per-
sons belonging to a mysterious “ British Medi-
cal Association ” have since been arrested,
and, so the detectives allege, letters were
found on the premises showing that a large
and flourishing trade in abortion was carried
on. Probably similar evidences could be found in
forty other establishments in Sydney, that city
being notoriously the home of the abortionist. In
any case, the fact is sufficiently certain that the
deceased died through an attempt to procure
abortion.

The usual weekly leper has turned up in
the North. He was foreman at a Brisbane tan-
nery, and is supposed to have had the disease for
about a dozen years. During all that time lie lias
presumably scattered the germs of his complaint
around, and quite a crop of new lepers may be
expected by-and-bye.
Dear Bulletin, — Re your par. (Aug. 3)
regarding Woman’s Suffrage in S. A. So far from
Mrs. Birks, Mrs. Nicholls, and myself “not com-
ing into the field until the struggle was virtually
over,” Mrs. Birks has been treasurer (and a
splendid treasurer, too) from the league’s in-
ception to its dissolution. Mrs. Nicholls has
been on the council continuously from within
two months of the formation of the league;
while she and the W.C.T.U., of which she is
president, secured over 7000 out of the 11,000
signatures to the monster petition presented
to Parliament, and this may be taken as typical
of her share in the work generally. I never
“claimed a chief part in obtaining the franchise.”
Although I only joined the council on the Rev.
Hugh Gilmore’s death in 1801,1 publicly advocated
Woman’s Suffrage on the platform before Mrs,
Lee came to the province, and 1 helped the move-
ment in other practical ways long before joining the
Council. There has not been the slightest attempt
to “rob ” Mrs. Lee of any credit due to her for
her share in the fight. On the contrary, my
article in Jan. Review. OB' Reviews referred to
her twice, and in one place I said “the women of
South Australia ought to cherish the memory of
Lady Colton, Mrs. Birks, and Mrs. Lee, who have
practically been connected with the League from
its inception, and who have worked earnestly for
it ever since.” In addition to this, toy press copy
shows that I wrote this special commendation of
Mrs. Lee, which, however, did. not appear in the
printed article Mrs. Lee has been a particu-
larly active and effective newspaper correspondent
on the subject.” Her photo, would have been
published in the Review, but for the fact that
when I applied to her—as I did twice by letter
and once personally—she said she had not a photo,
which could be used for the purpose, having only
an old one taken before she came to the province.
The subscription for Mrs. Lee was really begun
by the members Of the Council who are charged
with being jealous of her; and they obtained
about .£l5, chiefly subscribed among themselves
and a dozen friends who recognised Mrs. Lee’s
useful work. —C. PnouD. [The Bulletin finds
on enquiry that Mr. Proud’s statements are
correct, and regrets the publication of the inac-
curacies in the paragraph which he complains of.]
The interest in Cellular Clothing is be-
coming widespread. The extent of application of
Cellular fabric is so large that it maybe said to
cover every variety of clothing for all seasons and
for all climates. iSinee the doctors recommend it,
and since it gives the maximum warmth with the
minimum weight, and is therefore peculiarly suit-
able for Australian climates, it is not surprising
to find it quickly superseding the old materials
used for underclothing. The sole agents for the
colony of Victoria are Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson
and Sons, of Collins-street. Economy is also
among the virtues of Cellular Clothing.*
Mr. Tom W. Furse, secretary of Sydney
Eight-hour Demonstration Committee, publishes
in this issue the full programme of sports to be
held in connection with the gala on Monday,
October 7, at Rosehill. He also wants the public
to understand that the tickets are operative in the
art union, whether the holders are present or not,
so that a purchase will be a paying investment,
even if the purchaser has no idea of going to the
big shivoo. It is better than trying to pick a
double, anyway. The entry-list for the band
contest has closed with 12 entries, which include
all the best bands ; and Wollongong, Bathurst,
Orange, Adamstown, Newcastle, and other coun-
try bands intend to make anything local blow the
plug out if it wants to win. The secretary is in
Ids office every Saturday, from 8 to 10 p.m., to
receive entries for the events on the sports-pro-
gramme.* 1

Melb. E. Herald v. Melb. E. News —the
Priests v. the Levites.
Era : Two medical items. In certain damp
dwellings cancer constantly recurs, and the tene-
ments thus become known to the medical profes-
sion as “ cancer houses.” It would be advisable
to have official records kept of the houses lived in
by all cancer patients. . . . Was at an Indian
eye-doctor’s, in Melb. lately. His assistant
came in and took his umbrella : “ he wanted to
grind some medicine with the handle,” he said.
Reminded me of the old story where the officer
finds his Hindoo servant straining coffee through
a sock, and receives the excuse that it isn’t a clean
one.
For true humor in journalism one must
go to the masonic organs. A brother refusing to
settle an advertising debt gets righteously cursed
for his flagrant offence by his brother who runs
the suffering rag. The fact that they have both
been sworn on the same sacred gridiron to love
one another evermore, is treated as a very trivial
detail compared with the awful wickedness of the
monster who doesn’t cash up. “We are averse to
publishing details of business matters which,
while concerning ourselves, might possibly be an
infliction upon our readers,” says Masonry
(Melb). Then the enraged publication proceeds
to shriek the details of a country case in which it
sued Brother somebody for 20s. and achieved a
glorious victory.

At Seymour (Yic.< Free Library recently,
a motion that The Bulletin be taken for the
reading-room was defeated—chiefly on the ground,
it would seem, that it was considered “a disloyal
paper.” The opposition was led by the inevitable
clergyman, whose name has not yet reached
this office. As this paper is run, not for
the good of its proprietors’ health, but
to a large extent for the filthy purpose
of making money—an end in nowise sub-
served by its being read gratis at Free Libraries—
The Bulletin co. will, as a token of gratitude,
and in accordance with its custom, post the
paper free to the rev. gentleman in question for
one year, if he will kindly supply his address.
The Prince of Wales, we read, gets 12 new
dress-suits every season, and uses three new silk
hats every fortnight, also two new frock-coats
monthly. In vain he strove to make the black
cut-away coat fashionable —the tall, slight young
men whom the frock-coat suited were too power-
ful, For a frock-coat the Prince pays .£l2, for a
dress-suit .£l5, for a tweed suit £llO to £ll2. He
is most particular as to fit, and the slightest crease
or wrinkle ensures instant return of the garment.
No alterations are allowed—a misfit ever so
trifling involves the building of a fresh coat or
what not de novo. Tummy pays 255. for a silk
hat, and his shirts, &c., cost him £1250 a year.
For boots, he pays £2 to £3 a pair, and always
has a score of pairs kept on lasts for immediate
use. He never wears a pair of gloves twice, and
probably spends £2OOO a year on clothes, apart
from uniforms.
To obtain charming natural complexion—p. 22.*
Mes-rs. George Robertson and Co., of
3GI, 363 George-street, Sydney, have greatly
enlarged their fancy goods department, which
now contains almost every gaud that the soul of
man or women can desire. Among the leading
lines are purses, pocket-books, bags and the like
in a great variety of leather—crushed morocco,
French morocco, Moorish calf, crocodile, dog,
lizard, alligator, French calf, and the hide of
almost every other animal save the kraken
and the unicorn, and perhaps the bunyip.
The firm keeps a vast stock of purges,
card-cases, letter-cases, wallets, blotters, tourist
cases, photo, frames, hand-bags, memo.-books,
cigar and cigarette cases, all in artistic
tints, some of which are new, while all are
beautiful. Messrs. G. R. & Co. also supply every-
thing that comes under the heading of fancy
stationery ; find and emblazon arms, crests, and
mottoes ; prepare illuminated addresses for the
stout alderman who is going to Europe, book
plates, and monograms ; and do other things too
numerous to mention. Tire illuminating and
heraldic department is under the especial charge
of Mr. H. C. Skarratt.*
There are 31 lady missionaries from Aus-
tralia in China.
Hares are being hawked on Melb. Block
at 6d. each—and no takers.
“At present there are not 50 European
cabinetmakers at work in Melbourne, whereas of
the smellful, uncleanly Chinese there are fully 500
busily engaged.”—Melb. Age.
At the International Chess Congress,
Lasker, British representative and world’s cham-
pion, defeated Steinitz, of the United States.
But Lasker (like his rival, Steinitz, and pretty
nearly all the other great chess-players) is essen-
tially a German.
Journalist: “I was engaged upon the
principal Melb. evening paper, when the overseer
came to me, with a rueful face, bringing No. 1,
vol. i., of a new rival. ‘ There’s a splendid
paper,’ isaid he. ‘Why,’ said I,‘there’s nothing
in it.’ ‘Ah,’ he rejoined, ‘but look at the
printing ! ’ Always the way with your overseer.
A book’s a book, although there’s nothing in it.”
Divorces are made in Heaven. — Oscar
WILDK.
A German resident of Melbourne is the
proud possessor of a few hairs from the head of
the first Napoleon. Sort of “ hair of the dog that
bit you.”
The celebrated firm of English perfumers,
J. and E. Atkinson, of London, who bottle up ail
the most delicate odours on earth and diffuse
sweetness over two hemispheres, five continents,
and many thousand square miles, lay particular
stress on the fact that their scents are obtained
from Nature’s flower-laboratory only. Therefore,
they advise that imitations chemically made from
garbage, and old iron, and coal-tar, and similar
materials be never sniffed as they pass by, for
obvious hygienic reasons.*

He kneeleth not with prayer-clasped hands.
The yearning of his doubting heart
God sees—and understands.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Again; it is Respectable to be Wholesale. Cash-
betting has been carried on in Sydney (or close upon
three years, and one of the last men to lake it up was
Oxenham, kin* of the Sydney ring ; he adopted it some
two moiiills ago, and laiely the regular daily laying in
cash against him was £2OO. The enforcement of Terry’s
Betting-houses Suppression Act will stop all rash-betting
except upon the racecourse, but men with credit can bet
as freely now as they could under the cash-s\ stem. The
law in effect stops only the poor man from indulging his
passion for gambling; it squelches the little silver Juggins
unless he can attend meetings, but it gives the gold
Juggins as much play and opportunity as he can desire.
A Juggins is only a Juggins in relation to his
income, and the backer who, out of his silver earnings,
can spare half-a-crown upon his fancy is just as right and
wise as the richer man who can proportionately put lialf-
a-tbousand on his dead-bird. Tims the maxim, “one law
for the rich and another for the poor,” holds good in this
as in every other case, and the enforcement of the law
will give the gold Juggins the same liberty, the same
protection as heretofore, but will expose the silver
Juggins to the rapacity of the unregistered bookmaker,
who will wait for him in the streets and *• lay” him there,
or conduct him by back-ways and dark staircases into
veritable dens of thieves.
Men must gamble as they must love, figbt, and
drink. There is a great element, of gambling in every
business venture, and the only man Thb Bulletin knows
who hud none of the gambling spirit was a church elder
who, before taking the plate round each Sunday, would
back his own prospective collection against that of his
fellow elder. His was not a gambling spirit, but the
spirit undefiled which prompts a church-bazaar.
• • * *
Bookmaker Oxenliam on the recent raid on the
Sydney gambling shops: “Betting cannot be stopped,
and all attempts to arrest it are futile. The people of this
country will have betting, and it is useless to try and stop
them. . . There are places that never should have been
allowed to exist, and why the police did not suppress
them earlier I could never understand. They had the
power all the time, and it was onlv a question of enforcing
their authority. People have been talking about our
wanting a new' Act, but we wanted no new legislation at
all on this subject, for the laws already in existence gave
all the power that could possibly be wished for. The
police have done now' in five minutes what
have done at any time since the shops were opened. All
of which are immortal truths. The Sydney betting-shops
were illegal establishments which carried on their business
amid a glare of gaslight, and had their names conspicu-
omly printed on the big front window, and I heir addresses j
advertised in the papers in large black type. And as the
detective often took doubles and other wag rs himself, and
refused, even when he fell over the doorstep of the biggest
establishment of the lot, to be officially aware of its
existence, and the heads of the police didn t insist on him
being aware of it, and the Colonial Secretary for ears
took no steps to awake the force to a state of conscious-
ness on the subject—well, what was the cause of it all ?

The Melbourne purists, having coalesced with
tiie bookies to smash the tote, have now joined forces
with the Tobacconists’ Association to squelch Sunday
trading in cigars. In return the Sabbatarians wanted the
I.A. to work with them in booming Murray’s Anti-
gambling Bill, but the tobacconists jib, every second
member of the T.A. being a Hebrew. In all these cases
union is strength for the Devil, as the purists are fighting
for th» impossible. Murray's latest effort is hopeless
merely a Murray cod, in fact.

Threepenny wagers were being taken on the

of thes» was run by a man whose wife acted as clerk.

flat wagering—annex 25 per cent, c mniission and nil

percent. In the case of Raak, some who backed the

How many people who attend glove-fights have
commented on the luck of one of the boxers, who, at a
critical moment, will receive several seconds’ respite
through the referee having to adjust his glove, which has
become untied? The cognoscenti smile and the cute
second winks his other eye, and takes good care that such
a calamity' does not befall his man at a period when his
opponent is “ under the whip.”

Cricket king Grace, by July 8 this English
season, had scored 1638 rung in 23 innings. In Gentlemen
v. Players, at Lord’s, in scoring 118, he made his fifth
century this season on that ground. Mold, tip to the
date noted, led the English howlers with 112 wickets for
13 - 29 runs a wicket; Richardson had 120 at 15T ; Peel,
72 at 15-56.
* • • •
Probably Grace’s medical training lias assisted
him in taking good care of himself; hut, all the same, no
other big sport would have allowed him lo last so long.
There is neier the prolonged and terrible tension in
cricket that there is in boxing, wrestling, racing, sculling,
cycling and football. If a batsman gets winded in cricket
he can take it easy until lie gets his wind hack, hut in
other sports the man who stops to get Iris wind disappears
from the ranks of competitors. Cricket is more recreative
than arduous.

M‘Laren’s great score of 424 for Lancashire
against Somerset is one of those wonderful feats which
result from a batsman reaching the pink of perfect form,
while the day and the wicket combine for his success.
Having mastered the howling, he never gave a chance up
to 262. Nineteen years ago Grace put up the former
county-match best with 318 for Gloucestershire against
Yorkshire. Then the young M’Laren was scarcely walk-
ing, hut the famous Grace, on averages, is still, as then,
the champion of England.

•J 1 • • •
Men who stood to win or lose hundreds on a
horse at Saturday’s races did not raise one particle of the
barbarous din which came from the Wallaroo or Hand wick
harracker at Sydney Agricultural Ground. The Wallaroo*
had a little the liest of it at the start, hut several of their mm
were temporarily laid out, and towards the end of the first
half the hall itself was pulled out of the scrum, crushed
into a flat piece of leather. Just about this time, too, a
Wallaroo forward got his collarbone broken, but he came
out for tlie second half to fight over the hall as if his life’s
destiny depended on it. The whole of the second half tin
play was vicious, and probably the least vicious player
was one who struck a blow at a foe with the clenched flsl;
the rest of the combatants tried to choke each other or
break each other’s backs, and often the fool hall’s presence
seemed hut a shallow excuse for assault and battery. A
tremendous run by the Randwick Jumbo brought Du-
play early upon the Wallaroo line. Now and again a
play er would get laid out, and the crowd roared with
laughter at the way the downed man «ould totter about
for some minutes after as he tried to collect his senses.
Blood ran freely. Many women were crying, and one girl
was in hysterics. Nearly the whole of the second half lie
hall.le was fought close upon the Wallaroo line, and at any
moment Randwick promised to score. In the last five
minutes, however, a tough rush by Wallaroo forced the
play right back to the Randwick line, and there it siood
when the umpire’s whistle went, with both sides having
scored nothing, afier ttie most savagely contested match
seen in Sydney for many a day.

ing from Armidale (N.S.W.) to Sydney. He carries a dog,
a cat, a tent, two pairs blankets, change of clo’hlng, two
billy-cans, frying-pan, camera, tripod stand, iwo dozen

that when the front wheel crashed into a fence, Kennedy
was bnck-d yards ahead into the paddock. Tii’k
Buli-bhx knows another man who starts out soon on
a cycling tour carrying on his wheel all the above furni-
ture, together with a hogshead of beer, four cases of
whisky, and a harem.

St. Alban’s Revisited .
A GLANCE AT THE HORSES
[BY A TICKET-HOLDER.]
Being in Geelong last week I thought I would
like to see St. Alban’s again before the lottery, and,
driving over, was fortunate enough to find Mr.
Macdonald at home. The gardens and carriage-
drive approaching the house were in beautiful
ofder and betokened an early spring. Near the
stables we came on old Robinson Crusoe just
about to be turned out for his daily exercise ; and
well did the old sailor look despite his advancing
years. Ho cornea from a long-lived family, and
may yet sire the equal of Trident or Navigator—
two of the best racehorses that ever looked
through a bridle. Bill of Portland has
much improved since his arrival, and now pre-
sents a very handsome, racer-like appearance.
He is massive, and yet shows a deal of the quality
which the progeny of St. Simon are so noted for.
Eiridspord is as strong and muscular as ever,
while Strathmore has improved in every way.
Any of these horses separately would command
respect, but when Trenton appears he is the choice
of all good judges. Trenton is now in the prime
of life; and if his destination should be England
or America, in appearance, at least, he will hold
his own against any horse living. Many of the
best studmasters are making use of him this
season; but I hope some patriotic Australian will
win him and keep him in the country.
We were just in time to see the horses in train-
ing on the track, and although only doing slow
work they struck me as being in very good order
for this time of the year. Wallace has grown and
furnished; bub whether he, Challenger, or Trent-
hara is good enough to win the Derby I know not,
and when I asked the imperturbable Macdonald
which he fancied he quietly answered, “ Oh, any
one of them is good enough ! ” May be, but my
racing education has taught me that there have
never been three Derby colts in one stable in one
year. However, they all look well, and both Chal-
lenger and Wallace have performances to recom-
mend them. Carnage is bright and mellow, while
Merman grows more like Patron every day. Brad-
ford and Redcomb are, I think, only moderates ;
but Havoc looks as if he had completely recovered
from his accident, and has evidently done a good
deal; of work. The two-year-old unbroken colts
were in a paddock close by, and a very fine lot
they are. The Trenton-Umilta colt is a weight-
for-age horse in appearance. Nob so strong, bub
very neat, is the half-brother by Trenton to Boz ;
while the half-brother to Wallace by Strathmore
is quite as big and strong as some of the
competitors at the late Grand National meet-
ing. Turning up a lane, we came on a chest-
nut mare with a very large foal. This was
no other than Mersey, the dam of Carbine.
Her filly foal was dropped some time in March,
and is strong and good-looking; but unfortunately
will not be fib for racing purposes owing to an
accident at her birth. The old mare does not
appear in foal, but, as she would be very late,
there is no certainty that she is not. A little
further afield were four mares who had dropped
Ms during the preceding night. Pardon, a fine
colt to Eiridspord ; Umilta, a filly to Trenton.
Hatason and See-Saw had also foals, but their sex
I do not remember, although both were by Eirids-
pord. There are some 35 mares yet to foal, so
that there will be a very good percentage this
year. Among the mares in that happy condition
were Nightmare, La Tosca, She, Coin, Bonnie
Rosette, and Solitude; but Hypatia and Aura
show no signs of motherhood.
Leaving the mares, Mr, Macdonald took me
round by the irrigation works, which appear in
excellent order, but the quantity of grass on the
estate prevents them from being used very often.
Still, they would be useful in a dry season.
Along the river a new training-track has been
commenced, and it is intended to give the wind-up
gallops to the Derby and Cup horses here. A fair
amount of crop has been put in, while all the
stock on the estate are in good condition, and
there is lota of grass.
On our homeward route we interviewed the
yearling colts, and very inquisitive little fellows
we found them. The brother to Auraria does not
belie his parentage, and is as handsome as his
celebrated sister—-which is saying a good deal in
his favor. Bonnie Rosette, the imported mare by
Barcaldine, is also represented by a very fine colt;
and several by Eiridspord show good size and
shape. However, yearlings at this season of the
year are in the growing stages, and it will no
doubt suffice those who hope to win them to know
that they are in excellent health and condition,
and appear in every way worthy to take a high
place on the turf.
On the training-track I noticed that a starting-
machine had been erected, and one of the men
was going over the outer track with the harrows.
During my walk I came across the ostriches, and
was told that they had a sitting of eggs, each egg
being about the size of a football!
I had another look at the horses in training in
the stables, and, scanning them with the eye of
one who might be their owner, I must confess I
was greatly pleased. Mr. Macdonald does not
view the lottery with any kindly feeling, for he
says Mr. Wilson has had the bad luck to run
third twice, and second once, in the Melbourne
Oup in four years ; and he has never had a better
stableful of horses than at present. Now, no one
knows who will own them on sth November!
One thing which strikes the casual visitor to
St. Alban s is the forethought which has been em-
ployed in laying out the estate. Everything
seems to have been designed to carry on an im-
mense stud-farm ; and except that the training-
stables are a little too near the house, I very much
doubt if any better arrangement of the various
offices could be devised. As it was late when we
got through, Mr. Macdonald kindly asked me to
stay the night, and very comfortable he made me.
In the morning I drove away, hoping more fer-
vently than ever that Dame Fortune would for
once smile on me and allow my name to be on
the winning tickets of the St. Alban’s Estate.*
Madame Palma, the Sydney palmist, con-
fuses the average man’s opinion of the sexes by
declaring her best trade is with men, Male
clients, however, are mostly anxious about busi-
ness matters ; the females are generally girls burn-
ing to know who will marry them, or old ladies in
trouble. The deep lines of Parkes’s hand, says
the chiromancer, denote power of deep thought
—but no hand could be less like a poet’s. All
sorts of fish come to Madame Palma’s handy-net.
She read the hand of divorce-conspirator Butler a
month ago; also Mrs. Copp’s. Several Aus-
tralian Judges’ wives have waited upon her. Even
Raymond Blathwayt, interviewah, called and
took a chart away with him.

Eucalypte Special Oil.*
FREIGHTS.
The Victorian Railway Department notifies that
for Victorian-grown wools it will in future
charge for actual weight in nen of charging at so
much per bale, as formerly. It is inconceivable
why the Department held so long to an unjust
and inequitable practice. The decision of the
Commissioners in this matter will be appreciated
by wool-growers. Doubtless, the untiring agita-
tbis a ma<3e b 3J , tbe National
Wool Company, of Melbourne, will he recollected,
the company having distinctly scored in carrying
this point.*
When a workman in Melbourne sues for
wages due these times, the godly employer very
often gets into the box, and sadly informs the
Bench that he merely employed the fellow out of
charity, and the money already paid to him
(average about 3|d. an hour) was more than was
stipulated. Then the combined Bench frowns
dreadfully upon the horny-handed ingrate, and
severely dismisses the case, with some caustic
reflections upon the unconscionable greed of the
laboring classes.

Has successfully tried “ Homocea.” Writing
from Mount Juliet, Thomastown, he says:—
“It is the most wonderful stuff I ever came
across. Please send me three boxes. I gave
some to a labourer of mine who had a bad boil
on his leg, and was quite lame. I saw him after
four days, and asked him what the ‘' Homocea ”
did for him. He said he was entirely cured ; his
wife, besides, had had a pain in her elbow, so
that she could not bend her arm for a year, and
it cured that. I also gave some to a woman with
a bad leg, and it is doing her good.”

nose (which is a Hampton Court labyrinth on a
Sinall scale). Homocea at once subdues the in
flammation by reducing the swelling, and thereby
relieving the feeling of “stuffiness." Moreover, it
forms a protective covering for the delicate mem
brane, and prevents cold air, or other irritating
vapour from getting at it. In influenza, Homocea
acts in the same way, and here the germ-killing
properties of the ointment further come into play
Not much is known about the way in which influ
enza originates, but it is quite clear that it is caused
by an invisible germ or microbe of some kind.
Homocea may,therefore, be confidently recomended
both as a cure and a preventive of influenza.

situated on 4J acres of the University
reserve —the highest site in Melbourne. The
buildings are the finest and completest school
buildings in Australia. All the members of the
staff are thoroughly-trained teachers.
The accommodation for boarders is unrivalled.
The bedrooms are furnished for one, two or three
boarders. The evening study-rooms are large,

assistants.

! A well-known medical referee lately told The
Bulletin that a life-assurance agent who has sent him
numerous proponents to examine invariably demands
from the doctor 2s. 6d. per case sent. When the doctor
kicked at first, the agent calmly replied that if he did not
get that half-crown com. he would bring over the doctor
from the next town to do the examination. Further, the
agent stated that the societies are more or less aware of
this practice. Agents are usually paid nothing for cases
the doctor does not pass, so the live canvasser makes sure
beforehand of half-a-crown or five shillings out of the
guinea the society must pay the doctor whether the case
passes or not. Which means that if such a canvasser
went and rounded up all the unemployed he could find,
he would do a good day’s work. Twenty wrecks certain
of refusal would, at ss. a head, pan out £5 to the drover,
and probably they would think a pint of sheoak excellent
pay. And such a trick would mean £2l transferred by
a swindle from the coffers of the society to the pockets of
a scoundrelly canvasser and his medical partner, or medi-
cal dupe, as the case might be.

lb is averred that a potent factor in the ruin of
a once-prosperous section of the Melb. hotel-trade has
been the privileges conferred by police and excise
officers on certain temples of Lais. T lese now
trade directly with wholesale liquor houses, instead of
buying from the publicans of their neighborhood. In a
word, the most fashionable “notorious” houses of Mel-
bourne are also pubs., paying no license fee.
* ♦ ♦ *
Melb. woodcarters’ dodge. Carter calls round
just after dark inquiring if “Ye want to buy a load of
wood cheap? Only (T boh the lot.” There looks to be
about a ton of wood. The order is given, the wood un-
loaded, and money paid. In the morning a heap weigh-
ing about 3owt. is found in the yard. The cart has simply
been filled with sacks covered on top with wood. Bluff
and darkness have done the rest.

Dear Bulletin, —Jews are bad, Christians
worse. The Jew asks his big per shent right out and
that’s all, hut whether the Christian asks more or less he
is sure to double it with commission and fees of all sorts.
The Jew is an avow'ed Shylock, but ti e Christian is a
churchgoer who squeezes you per a shady agent. Had
dealings with one of the latter, whose principal—a
suburban medico—lives opposite. Every quarter when I
pay my interest I notice doctor’s wife has something new
- dress, bangle, or something. May be a coincidence,
but 1 look at my frayed trousers and swear. Doctor
sings in our choir, so 1 go to church now and curse him
all the time. The louder the music the louder I curse.
Surely my cura-s will be heard as much as his “Gloria in
Excelsis.”— Damfool.
* * * *
The deterioration of explosives in the North is
a serious danger, and attention was again directed in Q.
Leg. Assembly, the other day, to the need for appointing
an Insp* dor of Explosives. The main cause of accidents
in the North, however, is the greed for divs. and the con-
sequent rushing of the men. The law says that when-
ever a hole has missed fire an hour must elapse ere work
is resumed in that particular spot, but the rule is a dead-
’elter.
♦ * ♦ ♦
Re another buried miner. It was at an old
quartz-mine near Hushworth (Vic.). The man had been
caught in a small drive through a rush of stowage, and
the rescue entailed hours of hard and unremitting labor.
At last, by a piece of extraordinary luck—the loose reef
having been held back from a narrow aperture by a long
leg the rescue party were able to reach their man and
haul him out of the little pitch-dark den in which he had
been squatting for 18 hours, speculating upon his chances
of the Kingdom. The rescuers were most enthusiastic,
and cheered like maniacs, but the rescued was apparently
indifferent. The only remark he passed when the row
had subsided was, “ Well, you needn’t have made such an
infernal fuss about it!’’

Vic. railways annually consume 220,000 tons of
coal. Tenders for the supply of 156,000 tons of the local
article are being called. Next year, it is thought, none
but Vic. coal will be used.
* * ♦ *
Experiments at Grubbs’ mill have led Tas.
Govt. Geologist Montgomerie to conclude that the
“ float ’’-losses from the treatment of West Coast silver ore
approach £150,000 yearly. Thus the wet process is
alleged to steal one-third of the whole silver treated. It
is not usual, at Zeehan, to assay the heaps before treat-
ment by the different mills, and until this is done no com-
plete investigation of the matter can be said to have been
made. At the same time it is significant that the returns
from the mills are nearly always far below the yields
anticipated by the public. The public is an ass, of
course, but in this case it seems to have drawn only a
rational inference from the numerous assays made before
the mills were erected. At Grubbs’ Frue vanners are
used, whilst at the Western the Luhrig has been adopted.
The “float” silver is supposed to be contained in the
sulphides in such fine particles as to be carried away by
the water beyond the tailings. An assay of these heaps,
therefore, gives no idea whatever of the mills’ losses.

The gold output of the Rand (Johannesburg,
South Africa) still rises, and for June passed the 200,0000 z.
mark. Aggregate yield for each month of this year;—
Jan., 177,4630 z. ; Feb., 169,2950 z.; March, 184,9450 z. ;
April, 186,3230 z. ; May, 194,5800 z. ; June, 200,9410 z. In
June, ’B7, the output was 7340 z.! The M’Arthur-Forrest
(cyanide) and other chemical processes are now respon-
sible for over 60,0000 z. per month, mostly from tailings.
Last year the Transvaal, of which the Rand forms part,
stood third on the list of gold-producing countries, Aus-
tralia and the United States being ahead of it; but this
year the Rand output will, it is expected, exceed
2,500,0000 z. ; and neither Australia nor the U.S. will get
ahead of this—in ’95, at any rate, whatever Westralia may
help the former to do in ’96.
Compare this 200,0000 z. (monthly) output with
the best that Australia’s oldest reefing field has done.
Bendigo’s best year was ’53, when 661,7490 z. was got
(mostly, it is true, from alluvial); next best, 609,7290 z. in
’56. In no other year did it exceed 500,0000 z. Ballarat’s
total in 54 years just passed 18 million ounces.
In Johannesburg they reckon upon an output of
500,0000 z. per month four years hence! “At the very
lowest computation,” the local Standard and Diggers’
News says, “ 7000 stamps will be at work on these fields.”
500,0000 z. a month—6,ooo,OOOoz. a year ! The premier
mine now is the Langlaate estate (13,0000 z. for June),
followed by the Robinson (12,5810 z.), New Primrose
(12,018 oz.), and Crown Reef (11,2590 z.).
The State Mining Engineer to the S. A. Repub-
lic, in his report for ’94, states that he had received re-
turns from 141 co.’s working around Johannesburg and
other local fields. Their issued capital amounted to
122,940,321, their working capital (inclusive of cash pay-
ments to vendors) to £7,869,811, and divs. paid (lining
1894, 11,647,063. Total number of workmen employed ;
5652 whites and 42,504 natives—l,4o4 whites and 12,253
natives more than in ’93. But, though the production
averages for black and white together £l6O per annum,
“4-44 out of every 1000 employed were killed and 10'24 in-
jured(exoluding those slightly wounded). In Germany, of
every 1000 employed only ITB9 are mortally injured,
though the mining conditions are more favourable in
this country.”
Langlaate Estate, the premier South African
mine, has 160 stamps running and a cyanide plant to
treat 5000 tons a week. Total number of stamps (average
woiorht, Scwt.l on the fields at end of last year, 4500.

‘ THE LAST OF SIXA
From the Maranoa : One Symons was
riding to Yingerbay village, 14 miles, from Roma.
Within half a mile of town, he observed to a
mate—“ Well, we’ve got this far without any
accident. I suppose we’ll get home all right,
now.” Not five minutes later a dead tree, stand-
ing near the road, fell as they were passing, a
limb striking Symons on the head and knocking
him off his horse. Only injury—a scalp wound.
Not a breath of wind was stirring at the time.
Next!
Asserted that the cocaine habit is largely
on the increase in Australia, especially among
doctors. Cocaine certainly makes the user feel
youthful and lively, augments his capacity for
work, and does away with the need of sleep ; but
this apparently beneficial effect does not last long
—not even so long as that of morphine. The bitter
after-results are manifested earlier and are more
disastrous. Cocaine-takers are utterly unreliable,
and careless of their personal appearance. While
under the drug’s power they are happy, hopeful,
and equal to anything. Without it they are
nervous, morose, even dangerous. Cocaine is
about the quickest of all roads to a hell on earth.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
A correspondent : —ln the Wilcannia and
Willyama (Broken Hill) land district of N.S. W.
over million acres of Crown land is open to
selection by homestead or improvement lessees.
Of this l| million acres are in one county—
Evelyn, Milparinka district. Most of it is a
desert, officially described as “ sandy soil in arid
country;” if there were no rabbits and it were
watered, its carrying capacity would probably
average a sheep to 12 or 14 acres. Much of this
country, being altogether without natural or con-
served water, has been absolutely abandoned by
the pastoral lessees ; it is not even held under
occupation license or annual lease. Thus aban-
doned, it is occupied by rabbits which die by tens
of thousands during every drought and increase
by twenties of thousands after every rain, when
they spread out over the surrounding country,
undoing all the work of destruction which the
lessees have done during all the months before.
It is most necessary, therefore, that these wholly
abandoned lands should be occupied ; occupation
is the only cure for the rabbit-disease. But the
largest area which any person can lease is 20,480 ac.
as an improvement lease, or 10,240 acres as a
homestead lease ; and as the land is so wretchedly
poor—the larger area, even if well watered, could
not be relied upon to maintain more than a thou-
sand sheep—nobody will look at the country.
The Legislature, in dealing with Crown lands,
keeps its mind on the lands which bring in an
annual rent of from 4d. to Is. per acre, and acts
accordingly ; hence in measure after measure no
adequate provision is made for the most necessary
occupation of the millions of acres of rabbit-
infested country which, even were there no rabbits,
would be dear at a-third of a penny.
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.*
The platypus has furthur distinguished
himself. First, he astonished the scientists by
wearing a bill like a duck; then his wife, not to be
outdone, shook them up by laying eggs. Not
content with being a beast and a bird at the same
time, old man platypus has shown now that, while
he is about, there need be no demand for snakes,
for he can take on the contract himself. Horne in
1802 noticed on the male platypus’s hind legs a
pair of spurs, and thought they had to do with
his courtship and marriage. But Jamieson, in
1807, seeing the results of spur-wounds in men
and dogs, declared the spur was a poison weapon.
Bennett and Owen pooh-poohed the ides, but bush-
men generally stuck to the poison theory. Then
along came Prof. Anderson Stuart, and said the
bushmen were right. To settle the matter once
for all, Drs. Martin and Tidswell, of Sydney,
have now shown by careful dissection that in each
thigh there is a secreting gland connected by a
duct with a sac immediately behind the spur,
which has an outlet through the spur. Chemical
analysis showed the secretion to contain albumin-
ous proteids, but no nuclear-albumen, and experi-
ments on animals showed it was poisonous.
Platypus venom resembles snake venom in (1)
owing its poisonous power to proteids, (2) re-
tarding or preventing coagulation of the blood
when slowly introduced into the system in small
doses, (3) causing clotting within the vessels if in-
troduced in larger doses directly into the blood-
stream, (4) causing an immediate drop in blood-
pressure, (5) causing capillary hemorrhage and
cedema whem locally applied. So the platypus
suckles its young like a mammal, has a bill and
lays eggs like a bird, lives in or out of water like a
frog, and poisons his aggressors like a snake.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.
“ Letter-carriers in Fiji,” writes a corres-
pondent, “hand you your missive in a very graceful
way. They carry a long reed, split at one end to
hold the letter. A friend of mine lately sent me
a note by one of these picturesque messengers,
around whom there seemed to be a poetic halo
until I opened the letter: ‘Give bearer two bob
and a biscuit for me, I haven’t a thing in the
house; see you later.’ ’*

E beR to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
winter s Straight-out No. 1” “Cameo ” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial made
cigarettes called Pure Gold and Dixon s No. I,’’have given us official notice that they will not
suppJy us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS Limited
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as ’ *
w . THREE CASTLES” and cc CJ±IPSTA.I<T ’>
We have declined* to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the ntereat
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in

should or should not smoke.

The Passing of Parker .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No, you “ 'aren't ’eard of Parker,” as was with us
in The Bay,
An’ you “ ’aven’t dropped acrost ’im in yer
cruises,” as you say ;
An’ you ain’t a-go’n’ to meet ’ira, for ’e’s safe an’
snug is Joe—
If the fishes ’asn’t eat ’im—fifty fathom down
below;
Gawd rest ’is Weedin’ bones,
’E ’as gone to Davy Jones,
An’ I ’opes, as passun told us, that ’is soul is with
the Lord,
For ’e slid down like a plummet,
’E went quick an’ lively summat;
Two shots about ’is feet, mate, an’ two above ’is
’ead
When ’e dived astarn one raornin’ like a bloomin’
chunk o’ lead.
Now, we’ll ’ave a pipe o’ baccy an’ another sip o
rum,
An’ we’ll drink to ole Joe Parker, an’ I’ll tell you
’ow it come:
We was on the China station, in the ’arbor of
Shanghai
(An’ the cuss o’ Gawd upon it for a dirty ’ole,
sez I),
In the port o’ Nagasaki
(Ta, that's decent baccy),
Up to bloomin’ “Daddy Stock-Pot”* where the
cold ’ud freeze a man,
Back again to Yokohama an’ the south’ard o’
J ap.i n.
Then a-cruisin’, cruisin’, cruisin’,
For some gory fool's amusin’,
With “Sir Stinker Jones” an’ “ Toecaps ”t on a
nasty, choppy sea,
An’ a extry dose o’ drillin’ —Gawd above! it
sickened me.
Waal, we’re back at Shanghai, messmate, an’ that
bloomin’ place o’ sin ;
We are raakin’ pretty lively when a Rooshun ship
comes in,
An’, of course, that starts the rowin’ in the drums
an’ pubs, ashore,
For a Rooshun is a Rooshun, an’ ’e can’t be any
more,
An’ as Britons we despise ’em,
An’ in fightin’ times chastise ’em,
For our vittles an’ our medals an’ our kiddies an’
our wives—
’Sides, I ’ates them Weedin’ Rooshuns, ’cause they
always carries knives.
So, from arguin’ an’ skitin’,
One fine night it comes to fightin’,
An’ they stiffens out a jolly—though for that I
wouldn’t grieve,
But Ole Toecaps ’ears about it an’ ’e stops a week
o’ leave.
Waal, we goes ashore again, mate, when our pun-
ishment is done,
An’ our fellers starts a-boozin’ in a pub. they calls
“The Sun,”
An’a Rooshun chivvies Parker, in is lingo, so ’e
said,
An’ Joe ’auls off an’ ’its ’im—such a wonner
on the ’ead 1
Gawd ! there wa’ant a blessed windy
But we busted in that shindy ;
There was broken glass an’ bottles, beer an’ Wood
along the floor ;
Oh, we played the very devil with the woman’s
furuitoor,
There was picters torn an’ labels,
An’ we broke the chairs an’ tables,
Smashed the mugs an’ things to blazes, turned ’er
over ’old an’ deck ;
Till you never see, so ’elp me, such a gawd-aban-
doned wreck!
Aye! we gev ’em “Rule Britannia,” but a
Rooshun’s ’ard to beat,
An’ we ’eard that more was cornin’, so we slips
along the street,
Through the foreign quarter runnin, through
them narrer, dirty lanes,
For we didn’t want no trouble with Ole Toecaps
for our pains.
It was dark as ’ell an’ darker,
So we never knew that Parker
’Ad been stiffened in the scrimmage by a flyin’
whisky-jar
An’ was layin’ on ’is stummick in a ’eap behind
the bar.
Fust we thought ’ed gone to glory—
Which ’ad ended up me story,
But ’e comes aboard next mornin’ with ’is top-
piece in a sling,
An’ Ole Toecaps ’ears about it, an’ performs like
anything.
But it wa’ant no use to Parker, so our ole ship’s
doctor said,
For |ed got a brain oon-oushion on the inside of
’is ’ead,
An’ ’e couldn’t do ’is duty for ’is case was criti-oal,
So they ’ad to give ’im treatment in the Weedin’
’ospital.
’E was gettin’ wuss an’ ravin’
Till they gev ’is ’ead a shavin’.
For to do a operation that they reckoned very
fine,
But I want no sawbones foolin’ round a broken
’ead o’ mine—
’Cause they out that ’ere con-cushion
That was gev him by the Rooshun
From the inside of his top-piece, an’, as true as
there’s a ’ell,
They took out a bit o’ skull-bone ’bout as big as
that ! as well.
Waal, Ole Toecaps gets ’is orders, an’ we says
“ Good-bye ” ashore,
An’ the engineers is ready an’ we ’eads for Singa-
pore.
Joe ’e feels a trifle better on the fust day out at
sea.
But the next day out ’e’s goin’ an’ ’e asks ’em
send for me,
Gawd ! I ain’t a cove for oryin’
But I ’ates to see ’im dyin’,
So I aits beside ’is ’ammock an’ I tries to cheer ’is
’eart,
Though ’e’s groanin’ somethin’ ’orrid, an’ ’is lips
is wide apart.
There’s a lump inside me throat, mate,
An’ I’m feelin’ like a goat, mate,
So I sings out, “Cheer up, messmate, cheer up,
Parker ! cheer up, Joe !
Pull yer bleedin’ self together, give ole Davy
Jones the go!”
Gawd ! ’E stares me white an’ ’orful with ’is ’ead
all bandaged up,
But ’e couldn’t make no answer till I let ’ira
’ave a sup ;
Then sez lie, “I’ve got my papers, ’taint no
tearin’ use,” sez he,
“That ere Rooshun’s put my lights out,” an’’e
glares like ’ell at me.
“ Cuss the crimson gory Rooshun
As ’as give you that con-cushion,
But it weren’t my fault, Parker,” in a bloomin’
funk, sez I.
“ No, old man, I knoiv it weren't /” , , An’ I
thought I’d ’ave to cry ;
But I ’ate them women’s squealin’s,
So I cussed —to ease me feelin’s —
All the dirty furrin’ varmints up an’ down an’ all
I could,
An’ ’e brightened as ’e ’eard me an’ I see it did
’im good !
“Ned,” sez’ee, “you’ll go to Chatham, see the
Missus an’ the kid ?”
“ Joe,” sez I, “ I won’t forget it nor neglect it,
Gawd forbid ;
But you ain’t a-dyin', messmate, you ain’t turning
turtle yet ?”
But ’e shook ’is ’ead and muttered orful feeble,
“Don’t forget.”
Fust’e lay awhile a-dreamin’,
Then ’e started up a-soreamin’ :
“ Blarst all Rooshun dogs to bl.izes I”—ripped the
bandage from ’is ’ead,
Clawed the bloomin’ air a minute, dropped ’is
’ands and flopped back —dead.
Then that bloomin’ Doctor Oarker
Comes along to look at Parker,
An’ I ’ears the sinner mutter ’fore they orders me
away,
“ Too much cuttin’to recover V’ What a bloomin’
shame, I say 1
So they sewed ’im in ’is ’ammock, as accordin’ to
the rule,
’Fore ’e’d stiffened out, I reckon, or ’ad time
enough to cool,
An’ we ’ears the bosun pipin’in the mornin’-watch
all-right,
’Sif they’d got to feelin’ sorry ’cause they kept ’im
over-night.
For it’s run ’em out, an’ shove ’em,
Get the water quick above ’em,
When a Jack is dead ’e’s done for an’ ’is body ain’t
no use,
’E ’as skipped ’is earthly dooty an’ ’is troubles
an’ abuse.
Sew ’is ’ammock up around ’im,
Chuck ’im overboard an’ drowud ’im,
For there’s no one wants to worry an’ there’s no
one got to weep
When the chaplain starts “ Oommittin’ this ’ere
body to the deep !”
“Fall in alt, there, tun’ral party ” —an’ they tells
’em wot to wear.
“ Mess one to ten, two men from each !” an’ don’t
them two men swear.
“ Chaplain’s ready 1 Rig yer gratin’ ! Fire three
volleys !”—rattle ! bang !
Someone shoves the bloomin’ body—gone ! and
no one cares a ’ang.
PT’aps in failin’ that poor feller
Struck ’is bloomin’ ship’s propeller—
If they ’aven’t stopped the vessel through the
weather, or the whim
Of the admiral or skipper—an’-it plays the deuce
with him.
Wot’s the odds? He can’t come back. It only
means a dead bluejacket
(Which for that same matter, messmate, might be
either you or me)
Gets a double kind o’ doin’ as they puts ’im down
at sea.
When I leaves the China station with me
“compo ”+ in me ’and;
Goes I down ’er side at Portsmouth for a good
spell-ho on laud.
Then I thinks o’ poor ole Parker, an’ I starts to
feelin’ blue,
For I said I’d see the missus, an’ I’ve got to get it
through.
“Wot a pretty pickle this is,
Ere I’m bound for Parker’s missis,
An’ the news I’ve got to fetch ’er won’t be wel-
come, ’less I lie.
An’ she’ll want the truth or nuthink,” to me
bloomin’ self sez I.
Now, I ’ate to start a-lyin’
’Bout a decent messmate’s dyin’, ,
But ’is dyin’ came so ugly, ’cause it started in a
row—
“ Damn,” sez I, “I’ll say it didn’t, and I’ll chance
it anyhow!”
No, she didn’t start to faintin’, and she didn’t
weep nor wail,
For they’d told ’er all about it, an’ ’er grief was
tired and stale;
But I see ole Parker’s kiddie an’ I chucked it ’arf-
a-crown,
An’ I sez, “ You’ll soon be married,” ’fore I took
the ’bus to town ;
But she sez before I’m leavin’-
“ Ned,” sez she, “ I ain’t a-grievin’;
’E was always kind an’ gentle to the young ’un
an’ to me,
But I feel it orful sometimes that ’e’s layin’ out
at sea,
An’ it do seem ’ard an’ funny
That they asked me for the money
For the ’ammock that they sewed my poor dead
’usbaud’s body in ! ”
“’Ard?” sez I, “Gawd strike me silly, it was
nuthink but a SIN ! ”
Now, them there newspaper jokers they would
’ardly credit that,
But for every such the Navy charges seving-an’-
a-sprat ;§
So I tells ’er “Never mind, mum,” an’ I gives
another quid
In a bit ’o Parker’s tunic (an’ me blessin’!) to the
kid,
An’ I left ’em as I found ’em
With their bits o’ sticks around ’em,
With the flower-pot in the windy and ’is chest
agen the wall,
An’ that gallant little woman bearin’ bravely
through it all.
Gawd rest ’is bleedin’ bones,
’E ’as gone to Davy Jones,
But I’m goin’ back to Chatham when I gets re-
lieved from ’ere,
An’ I'll ask ’er No ; she wouldn’t ’ave
me. . . Take another beer!
Sydney, Jan., 1895.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Oollins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials; 10, an Ornament Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition; 12, a Wonderland;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. *
THE GOOD SLEEP OF A BAD MAN.
In a certain prison that we all have heard of laj
a convict upon his narrow iron cot. He was to be
hanged the next morning. Yet he lay therq
covered by a rough blanket, sleeping as quietlj
and soundly as a tired schoolboy. Occasional!}
the guard in the passage outside peered between
the bars of the cell, only to find his charge breath-
ing deeply and regularly. This man had violated
the law prohibiting murder ; yet he had not vio-
lated the physical laws governing his own body,
and nature rewarded him as if he had been the
noblest of his race.
That same night, loss than a mile away, a rich
man tossed and tumbled upon his luxurious bed.
He was a good and useful member of society, yet
he could not sleep. And, worse still, this hap-
pened to him every night. Sleep—that blessing
which the Psalmist says, “God giveth his
beloved,” was practically a stranger to this man.
What ailed him ? The tortures of conscience ?
Want of money ? The fear of enemies ? Nothing
of the sort. Then why didn’t he sleep as well as
the murderer ? You would like to know ? Eight,
let us look into the matter.
“I got no sleep at night; I would lie for hours
tossing about. In the morning I was worse tired
than when I went to bed.”
Thus writes Mrs. Eliza Mathews, of 1, North
Road, Burnt Oak, Edgware, near London, under
date of September 22nd, 18 ( J2. Just two years
before this time she lost her health. A foul taste
in the mouth, loss of appetite, and great distress
after eating were among the first tilings she com-
plained of. She craved food at times, and fancied
she could eat heartily, yet when the very dishes
she had asked for were set before her she turned
from them as though they were filth from the
gutter. Her skin grew sallow, her eyes yellow,
and she had a constant pain at her chest, sides,
and between her shoulders. Her bowels were
constipated, and the least exertion set her heart
thumping as if it must jump up into her mouth.
At such times it was as much as ever that she
could get her breath. She got so thin and weak
she was no good for work. She couldn’t walk out
doors without stopping to rest every few rods
almost.
The doctor did what he could for her, all any
doctor could do. At first he said he thought her
illness was owing to the smell of the farm-yard.
This looked possible. Even the smell of violets
has made strong men turn pale and faint dead
away. Yet the doctor was wrong. If he had
been right, she would have got better when the
family left the farm at Bently Priory and went to
live at Burnt Oak. But she was not improved by
the change of air ; she grew worse and worse.
“In May, 1887,” says Mrs. Mathews, “I went
over to Chlemsford to visit my aunt, Mrs.
Troughton. She told me of the good Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup had done her when she
had indigestion and dyspepsia. She bought me a
bottle, and I began taking it. After a few doses I
felt relief. I kept on taking it, and in two months
I was strong and well as ever. My husband and
friends were astonished, yet I assured them that
Seigel’s Syrup had done it. Yours truly, (Signed)
Eliza Mathews.”
The point is plain enough. The convict slept
soundly because he was a healthy man, although
he was a wicked one. Our rich friend rolled about
all night because his nerves were unstrung by the
state of his stomach. Our correspondent was
prostrated by the same thing—indigestion and
dyspepsia. The remedy named cured her because
it has that power. The reason remains a secret
with the roots and herbs from which it is made.
Yet so long as it drives away disease and gives us
back our health and strength, who cares for its
mystery? Results, not arguments, are what we
all want.
“Burnt Oak House, Edgware, September, 22nd,
1892. I have known Mrs. Mathews for some
seven years, and remember her long and lingering
illness. She informs me that Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup cured her, after medical and other
means failed. Mrs. Mathews is a lady of respecta-
bility, and her word can be implicitly relied upon.
You can use this statement in any way you may
think proper. Yours truly, (Signed) T. H. House,
Grocer and General Provision Dealer, Burnt Oak
Stores, Edgware.”
