The Night the Baby Died.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was a “quite romantic ” wife,
Awakened from a dreaming brief
To find Realities of Life
In oblong bills for bread and beef,
And household antidotes to grief.
And He was neither bad nor good :
A silent man, who never sighed,
But earned reward for work in food—
A spouse of dull, ungracious mood,
Until the night the baby died.
The wooer, whom the happy girl
Had hoped to bind in silken chains,
Revealed himself a sober churl,
Boasting the wisdom which disdains
To tune its mind to poets’ strains.
The sympathy her spirit sought
His sordid common-sense denied ;
She was his wife, a chattel bought,
Dear only at the price, he though %
Until the night the baby died.
He gave no sign, when baby came,
Of wanting such a care to tend.
Anon the youngster lisped his name,
And bothered him with toys to mend—
Treating his father as a friend.
And Lizzie, sitting down to share
The meal her weary lord supplied,
Rejoiced to see him shift his chair
And stoop to stroke her darling’s hair
The night the little fellow died.
Within an hour the shadow fell.
Fate dealt an unexpected blow :
The boy, who seemed so bright and well,
Had fled his playground here below
Ere yet she dared prepare for woe.
Only a form forsaken lay,
Its sightless eyes still opened wide ;
Poor Lizzie clasped the hands of clay,
She felt their blood-warmth steal away—
O God ! the night the baby died.
And he, the father, hung his head,
Rememb’nng how, in days of old,
His wife—-now kneeling by the bed,
Fondling those tiny fingers cold—
Had offered him her heart of gold.
He gazed through magic crystal tears
And saw again his smiling bride,
Whilst borne across the waste of years,
A conscience whisper smote his cars—
Aye ! on the night the baby died.
She felt a kiss upon her cheek,
She saw the love-light flush his face ;
His broken voice, how soft and weak,
When, like a sinner seeking grace,
He pleaded for the lost one’s place I
She put the waxen hands at rest,
Then dx-ew her husband to her side,
Laying his head against her breast,
And Love was born of sorrow blest,
E’en on the night the baby died.
Melbourne.
EDMUND FISHER.
Truganina.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How many people know that Truganina, the last
of the Tasmanian natives, lived some years in
Victoria, was charged with murder and tried for
her life at the Supreme Court, Port Pnillip, on
the 20th Dec., 1841 ? Were the particulars ever
published since in Australia, or anywhere else?
When G. A. Robinson came from Tasmania to
Port Phillip, in 1838, he brought over a lot of
Tasmanian natives, including one called “ Lalla
Rookh Truganina,” a native of Bruni Island.
Robinson was the man who succeeded by kind-
ness in doing, single-handed, for nothing, what
4000 armed men failed to do at a cost of <£70,000.
At Por Phillip he was appointed first protector of
Australian aborigines, at a salary of J 2500.
The attempt to settle the Tasmanians at Port
Phillip was not a success. Among other serious
troubles wa< the following dismal tragedy. On
6th Oct., ’4l, five of the Tasmanians, two men
and three w omen, were camped at Cape Patter-
son, close to the beach. The two men had rifles
and pistols. One of the women was Truganina.
The two m n shot two whalers named Cook
and ‘ ‘ Yankee ” —one through the head and the other
through the body. On the 17th all five Tasmanians
were arre ted by Commissioner E. A. Powlett,
with five Border police, and Lieut. Rawson, with
eight soldiers of the 28th Regt. Truganina showed
Powlett the graves where the two whalers were
buried. She and the other two women—“ Maria ”
and “ Fanny ” —were present when the men were
shot. It appears that the two whalers were mis-
taken for a miner named Watson and his mate.
The grievance was—the old, old story !
On 2iJth Dec., ’4l, Judge John Walpole Willis
sat on the Supreme Court bench at Port Phillip,
and before him in the dock we e two men and
three women, from the last of the Tasmanians.
Their names in the indictment were Robert
Jemmy Tummy Smallboy, Jack Napoleon Tun-
ninerpara way, Lalla Rookh Truganina, Fanny
Waterpooideyer, and Maria Matilda Nattepole-
nimma.
They were defended by Mr. Barry, who made
an eloquent and pathetic speech in their defence.
The p isoners had all admitted the shooting of the
two whalers. They told one tale, and never
varied in the smallest particular.
G. A. Robinson, in his evidence, said that “ the
prisoners came with me from Flinders Inland in
1838. The prisoner Robert undertook a jou ney
to Adelaide with Mr, Alfred Longhorn, and his
services were considered of the highest order and
his character excellent. He has been in my ser-
vice for 13 years, and was quite a lad when I
obtained him. His conduct was always good and
honest. He was a native of Capa Portland. Jack
came from Cape Grim, at the N. W. extremity of
Van Diemen’s Land. His conduct has always
been most satisfactory. Truganina is a native of
Bruni Island, and has been with me on all my
journeys. She is the wife of Woradi. She saved
my life at Arthur’s River in V.D. Laud. She
swam over and took mo across. The other two
women were emancipated from the sealers in
Bass’s Straits. The women are not allowed by
the men to act according to their own will. They
are in absolute subjection.”
Judge Willis, in his able and very impartial
address, referred to two Queensland aboriginals
named Moridio and Noogarail, then recently
hanged at Sydney for the murder of surveyor
Stapylton and his assistant, Tuck, at the head of
the Logan River on 30th May, 1840.
The jury, after half-an-hour, found the two
men guilty, with a recommendation to mercy on
account of good character. Truganina, Maria, and
Fanny were acquitted. On the following morning
Judge Willis sentenced the two to death. Their
case was referred, on 3rd Jan., 1842, to the
Executive Council at Sydney—Gov. Gipps, Sir
Maurice O’Connell, the 001. Secretary and the
Tj easurer.
This tribunal ignored the jury’s recommendation
and confirmed the sentence. The two unfortunate
Tasmanians were hanged accordingly, both facing
death with the cool stoicism of the savage.
Their last thoughts will remain conjectural
through all eternity. According to their laws
they were fully justified in killing the two
whalers, believing them guilty of an outrage on
their women. Truganina (pronounced Trooga-
neena) returned and died in her native land.
Her skull is now on view at Hobart Museum.
Brisbane.
A. MESTON.
Asking the Old Man’s Consent.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ You must ask father first, before I agree alto-
gether.’’
“Now, Molly, haven’t you just agreed? And
now you Want to put another proviso in.”
“ But you will have to ask dad, anyway.”
This was after Molly Mahony had agreed to
sink into oblivion and be known as Mrs. Bibbs
three months from date of agreement.
“ But I don’t know how to address the old
buffer.”
“ Don’t call my dad an old buffer, or .”
“ There, now, Molly, don’t get riled ; I’m very,
very sorry.”
“ All right, run away and write at once. Now,
behave yourself, sir!”
Header, have you ever attempted to write to an
old gentleman you never saw, to ask him to allow
you to adopt his daughter ? It seems easy, but
get pen, ink, and paper, and set yourself down
with the exhilarating effect of the last kiss still
upon you ; with all your ideas mortgaged by a
little bundle with blue eyes, and brown curls on
top ; and see how you get on.
After a dozen attempts, and as many sheets of
waste paper, I hear a light footstep slipping
up behind. I pretend to be very busy, and a
pair of soft hands come together over my eyes.
“ Molly, how could you! Look at the big blot
you made!”
“Oh!” (satirically) “what a long letter you
have written to dad! ”
“ Can’t manage it, Molly.”
“You stupid old dear, it’s easy to write to
daddy ; you have only got to tell him that ”
A pause, and a shy, downcast glance.
“Well, just tell me what to say, and I’ll write
It down I ”
“ The idea of my asking for myself J ”
“ Well, you write and ask my dad for me, and
I’ll copy your letter.”
“ Your daddy doesn’t require any asking, and I.
wouldn’t do it if he did. . , . There now 1
Don’t be cross, you old goose I ” \
Sh-cluck J
“ Don’t do it so loud, somebody will hear you I ”
Sh-cluck I
“ Be quiet, I didn’t mean you to do it again.”
“ I wish we were savages, Molly,”
“You horrid wretch 1 Why ? ”
“ Then I would only have to take you.”
“ But some other big savage might want me.”
“But I’d get you if he was ten feet high,
and savage from the ankles up.”.
“ Would you really? ” with a glance of admira-
tion. [I stand sft. 4in. in my boots.]
“Yes, Molly; who wouldn’t for such a beauti-
ful darling ?”
Sh-cluck! -■■■■.
“ I’ll get my letter-writer, Micky.”
“The very thing I”
We have an hour’s interesting search. Molly
insists on reading the love-letters-—as if any fellow
couldn’t write a love-letter without a model to go
by ! We hunt ; but not one can we find to fit our
case. Everybody that wants a father-in-law seems
to be in a different position from me. I begin to
wish that Molly had no father. Then I wade
through a lot of proposals. I don’t know what
use they are. As if a fellow is likely to let a girl
go away, then bother himself for a week trying to
put enough English together to tell her he wants
her to come back and marry him! At last I see a
business letter that seems to fit my case. Some
fellow wants to buy a partnership.
After three hours’ study I got the following
together. As Molly carried it herself, and ex-
plained matters when she got home, it was suc-
cessful ; so I give it to the world at, large, and
expect the gratitude of all young fellows situated
as I was:— ■ ;
Wind-and-Whisky Corner, 1894.

approval. By endorsing agreement, you will confer a
greatly-desired honor and life-long obligation on

The old man approved.

Aii Impossible Motion
V
SERGT- WILLIAM SULLIVAN
Sergeant William Sullivan is up to
something—he is up to the wall, upright
to the wall, right up to the upright wall.
It is a queer position for a real live ser-
geant ; it looks a painful one, but he does
not show his face—that would tell a tale.
He is laughing, although he is straining
every nerve to keep his equilibrium. It v
is not because Sergeant. Sullivan is a
giddy man that he makes this attempt to
lean sideways straight up to the wall.
Neither does he try it because there
is no convenient lamp - post to em-
brace. The fact is, he was offered
Five Pounds if he could do it, and, as
the picture shows, he attained the
desired position, but he didn’t attain the
desired Prize, for we were inquisi-
tive, and found out the Old Soldier
dodge. He had a nail on his finger—
an iron one—and driven into the wall.
That wasn’t in the piece, so Sergeant
Sullivan retired, and he says, “You
can’t pull the breeches off a High-
lander, and you can’t do that—it’s an
impossible motion.” Now, readers, the
proprietor of Beecham’s Pills invites you
and all your friends to place your right
side right up to an upright wall, every
portion of your side from ankle to the
shoulder being in contact with the wall.
Then attempt to raise the left foot from
the ground while the right side of the
body is still in contact with the wall. If
there is no fun to be got out of this
experiment, you are in a bad way, and
we feel it our bounden duty to remind
you that for ensuring buoyant spirits,
and the capacity to enjoy life to the
full, BEECIIAM’S BILLS are
Worth a Guinea a Box!
